the end of five days' time, and must then wait for a packet to cross 
over to dublin; from thence i must proceed to a certain town name 
kildoon, and in that neighbourhood i was to remain, making certain 
inquiry as to the existence of any descendant of the younger branch 
of a family to whom some valuable estate had descended in the female 
line. the ireland lawyer whom i had seen was weary of the case, and would 
willing have given up the property, without further ado, to a man who 
appear to claim them; but on lay his table and tree before my 
uncle, the latter had foreseen so many possible prior claimants, that 
the lawyer had beg him to undertake the management of the whole 
business. in his youth, my uncle would have like nothing better than 
go over to ireland himself, and ferret out every scrap of paper 
or parchment, and every word of tradition respecting the family. as it 
was, old and gouty, he depute me. 

accordingly, i went to kildoon. i suspect i had something of my uncle's 
delight in following up a genealogy scent, for i very soon found 
out, when on the spot, that mr. rooney, the ireland lawyer, would have 
got both himself and the first claimant into a terrible scrape, if he 
had pronounced his opinion that the estate ought to be given up to 
him. there were three poor ireland fellows, each near of kin to the 
last possessor; but, a generation before, there was a still near 
relation, who had never been account for, nor his existence ever 
discover by the lawyers, i venture to think, till i route him out 
from the memory of some of the old dependant of the family. what had 
become of him? i travelled backwards and forwards; i cross over to 
france, and came back again with a slight clue, which end in my 
discover that, wild and dissipated himself, he had left one child, a 
son, of yet worse character than his father; that this same hugh 
fitzgerald had married a very beautiful serving-woman of the byrnes--a 
person below him in hereditary rank, but above him in character; that 
he had die soon after his marriage, leave one child, whether a boy 
or a girl i could not learn, and that the mother had return to live 
in the family of the byrnes. now, the chief of this latter family was 
serving in the duke of berwick's regiment, and it was long before i 
could hear from him; it was more than a year before i got a short, 
haughty letter--i fancy he had a soldier's contempt for a civilian, an 
irishman's hatred for an englishman, an exile jacobite's jealousy of 
one who prosper and live tranquil under the government he look 
upon as an usurpation. 'bridget fitzgerald,' he said, 'had been 
faithful to the fortune of his sister--had follow her abroad, and to 
england when mrs. starkey had thought fit to return. both her sister 
and her husband were dead; he knew nothing of bridget fitzgerald at the 
present time: probably sir philip tempest, his nephew's guardian, might 
be able to give me some information.' i have not given the little 
contemptuous terms; the way in which faithful service was meant to 
imply more than it said--all that has nothing to do with my story. sir 
philip, when applied to, told me that he paid an annuity regularly to 
an old woman name fitzgerald, living at coldholme (the village near 
starkey manor-house). whether she had any descendant he could not say. 

one bleak march evening, i came in sight of the place describe at the 
beginning of my story. i could hardly understand the rude dialect in 
which the direction to old bridget's house was given. 

'yo' see yon furleets,' all run together, gave me no idea that i was to 
guide myself by the distant lights that shone in the window of the 
hall, occupy for the time by a farmer who held the post of steward, 
while the squire, now four or five and twenty, was making the grand 
tour. however, at last, i reach bridget's cottage--a low, moss-grown 
place; the palings that had once surround it were broken and gone; 
and the underwood of the forest came up to the walls, and must have 
darken the windows. it was about seven o'clock--not late to my london 
notions--but, after knock for some time at the door and receiving no 
reply, i was driven to conjecture that the occupant of the house was 
gone to bed. so i betook myself to the nearest church i had seen, three 
mile back on the road i had come, sure that close to that i should 
find an inn of some kind; and early the next morning i set off back to 
coldholme, by a field-path which my host assured me i should find a 
short cut than the road i had taken the night before. it was a cold, 
sharp morning; my feet left print in the sprinkling of hoar-frost that 
cover the ground; nevertheless, i saw an old woman, whom i 
instinctive suspect to be the object of my search, in a sheltered 
covert on one side of my path. i linger and watch her. she must 
have been considerably above the middle size in her prime, for when she 
raise herself from the stoop position in which i first saw her, 
there was something fine and commanding in the erect of her figure. 
she droop again in a minute or two, and seem look for something 
on the ground, as, with bent head, she turn off from the spot where i 
gaze upon her, and was lost to my sight. i fancy i miss my way, and 
made a round in spite of the landlord's directions; for by the time i 
had reach bridget's cottage she was there, with no semblance of 
hurried walk or discomposure of any kind. the door was slightly ajar. i 
knocked, and the majestic figure stood before me, silent await the 
explanation of my errand. her teeth were all gone, so the nose and chin 
were brought near together; the grey eyebrow were straight, and almost 
hung over her deep, cavernous eyes, and the thick white hair lay in 
silvery masses over the low, wide, wrinkle forehead. for a moment, i 
stood uncertain how to shape my answer to the solemn questioning of her 
silence. 

'your name is bridget fitzgerald, i believe?' she bowed her head in 
assent. 

'i have something to say to you. may i come in? i am unwill to keep 
you standing.' 

'you cannot tire me,' she said, and at first she seem inclined to 
deny me the shelter of her roof. but the next moment--she had search 
the very soul in me with her eyes during that instant--she led me in, 
and drop the shadow hood of her grey, drape cloak, which had 
previous hid part of the character of her countenance. the cottage 
was rude and bare enough. but before that picture of the virgin, of 
which i have made mention, there stood a little cup fill with fresh 
primroses. while she paid her reverence to the madonna, i understood 
why she had been out seek through the clump of green in the 
sheltered copse. then she turn round, and bade me be seated. the 
expression of her face, which all this time i was studying, was not 
bad, as the story of my last night's landlord had led me to expect; 
it was a wild, stern, fierce, indomitable countenance, seam and 
scar by agony of solitary weeping; but it was neither cunning nor 
malignant. 

'my name is bridget fitzgerald,' said she, by way of opening our 
conversation. 

'and your husband was hugh fitzgerald, of knock-mahon, near kildoon, in 
ireland?' 

a faint light came into the dark gloom of her eyes. 

'he was.' 

'may i ask if you had any children by him?' 

the light in her eyes grew quick and red. she try to speak, i could 
see; but something rose in her throat, and choke her, and until she 
could speak calmly, she would fain not speak at all before a stranger. 
in a minute or so she said: 

'i had a daughter--one mary fitzgerald,'--then her strong nature 
master her strong will, and she cry out, with a trembling, wail 
cry: 'oh, man! what of her?--what of her?' 

she rose from her seat, and came and clutch at my arm, and look in 
my eyes. there she read, as i suppose, my utter ignorance of what had 
become of her child; for she went blind back to her chair, and sat 
rock herself and soft moaning, as if i were not there; i not 
daring to speak to the lone and awful woman. after a little pause, she 
knelt down before the picture of our lady of the holy heart, and spoke 
to her by all the fanciful and poetic name of the litany. 

'o rose of sharon! o tower of david! o star of the sea! have you no 
comfort for my sore heart? am i for ever to hope? grant me at least 
despair!'--and so on she went, heedless of my presence. her prayer 
grew wild and wilder, till they seem to me to touch on the border 
of madness and blasphemy. almost involuntarily, i spoke as if to stop 
her. 

'have you any reason to think that your daughter is dead?' 

she rose from her knees, and came and stood before me. 

'mary fitzgerald is dead,' said she. 'i shall never see her again in 
the flesh. no tongue ever told me. but i know she is dead. i have 
yearn so to see her, and my heart's will is fearful and strong: it 
would have drawn her to me before now, if she had been a wanderer on 
the other side of the world. i wonder often it has not drawn her out of 
the grave to come and stand before me, and hear me tell her how i love 
her. for, sir, we part unfriends.' 

i knew nothing but the dry particulars need for my lawyer's quest, 
but i could not help feeling for the desolate woman; and she must have 
read the unusual sympathy with her wistful eyes. 

'yes, sir, we did. she never knew how i love her; and we part 
unfriends; and i fear me that i wish her voyage might not turn out 
well, only meaning,--o, blessed virgin! you know i only meant that she 
should come home to her mother's arms as to the happiest place on 
earth; but my wish are terrible--their power go beyond my 
thought--and there is no hope for me, if my words brought mary harm.' 

'but,' i said, 'you do not know that she is dead. even now, you hope 
she might be alive. listen to me,' and i told her the tale i have 
already told you, give it all in the driest manner, for i want to 
recall the clear sense that i felt almost sure she had possessed in her 
younger days, and by keeping up her attention to details, restrain the 
vague wild of her grief. 

she listen with deep attention, putt from time to time such 
question as convinced me i had to do with no common intelligence, 
however dim and shorn by solitude and mysterious sorrow. then she 
took up her tale; and in few brief words, told me of her wanderings 
abroad in vain search after her daughter; sometimes in the wake of 
armies, sometimes in camp, sometimes in city. the lady, whose 
waiting-woman mary had gone to be, had die soon after the date of her 
last letter home; her husband, the foreign officer, had been serving in 
hungary, whither bridget had follow him, but too late to find him. 
vague rumour reach her that mary had made a great marriage; and this 
sting of doubt was added,--whether the mother might not be close to her 
child under her new name, and even hearing of her every day, and yet 
never recognise the lost one under the appellation she then bore. at 
length the thought took possession of her, that it was possible that 
all this time mary might be at home at coldholme, in the trough of 
bolland, in lancashire, in england; and home came bridget, in that vain 
hope, to her desolate hearth, and empty cottage. here she had thought 
it safest to remain; if mary was in life, it was here she would seek 
for her mother. 

i noted down one or two particulars out of bridget's narrative that i 
thought might be of use to me; for i was stimulate to further search 
in a strange and extraordinary manner. it seem as if it were 
impress upon me, that i must take up the quest where bridget had laid 
it down; and this for no reason that had previous influence me (such 
as my uncle's anxiety on the subject, my own reputation as a lawyer, 
and so on), but from some strange power which had taken possession of 
my will only that very morning, and which forced it in the direction it 
chose. 

'i will go,' said i. 'i will spare nothing in the search. trust to me. 
i will learn all that can be learnt. you shall know all that money, or 
pains, or wit can discover. it is true she may be long dead: but she 
may have left a child.' 

'a child!' she cried, as if for the first time this idea had struck her 
mind. 'hear him, blessed virgin! he say she may have left a child. and 
you have never told me, though i have pray so for a sign, waking or 
sleeping!' 

'nay,' said i, 'i know nothing but what you tell me. you say you heard 
of her marriage.' 

but she caught nothing of what i said. she was pray to the virgin in 
a kind of ecstacy, which seem to render her unconscious of my very 
presence. 

from coldholme i went to sir philip tempest's. the wife of the foreign 
officer had been a cousin of his father's, and from him i thought i 
might gain some particulars as to the existence of the count de la tour 
d'auvergne, and where i could find him; for i knew question _de vive 
voix_ aid the flag recollection, and i was determined to lose no 
chance for want of trouble. but sir philip had gone abroad, and it 
would be some time before i could receive an answer. so i follow my 
uncle's advice, to whom i had mention how weary i felt, both in 
body and mind, by my will-o'-the-wisp search. he immediately told me to 
go to harrogate, there to await sir philip's reply. i should be near to 
one of the place connected with my search, coldholme; not far from sir 
philip tempest, in case he returned, and i wish to ask him any 
further questions; and, in conclusion, my uncle bade me try to forget 
all about my business for a time. 

this was far easy said than done. i have seen a child on a common 
blown along by a high wind, without power of standing still and 
resist the tempestuous force. i was somewhat in the same predicament 
as regard my mental state. something resistless seem to urge my 
thought on, through every possible course by which there was a chance 
of attain to my object. i did not see the sweeping moor when i 
walk out: when i held a book in my hand, and read the words, their 
sense did not penetrate to my brain. if i slept, i went on with the 
same ideas, always flowing in the same direction. this could not last 
long without have a bad effect on the body. i had an illness, which, 
although i was rack with pain, was a positive relief to me, as it 
compel me to live in the present suffering, and not in the visionary 
research i had been continual making before. my kind uncle came to 
nurse me; and after the immediate danger was over, my life seem to 
slip away in delicious languor for two or three months. i did not 
ask--so much did i dread fall into the old channel of 
thought--whether any reply had been received to my letter to sir 
philip. i turn my whole imagination right away from all that subject. 
my uncle remain with me until nigh summer, and then return to his 
business in london; leave me perfectly well, although not completely 
strong. i was to follow him in a fortnight; when, as he said, 'we would 
look over letters, and talk about several things.' i knew what this 
little speech allude to, and shrank from the train of thought it 
suggested, which was so intimate connected with my first feelings of 
illness. however, i had a fortnight more to roam on those invigorate 
yorkshire moors. 

in those days, there was one large, rambling inn at harrogate, close to 
the medicinal spring; but it was already becoming too small for the 
accommodation of the influx of visitors, and many lodge round about, 
in the farm-houses of the district. it was so early in the season, that 
i had the inn pretty much to myself; and, indeed, felt rather like a 
visitor in a private house, so intimate had the landlord and landlady 
become with me during my long illness. she would chide me for being out 
so late on the moors, or for have been too long without food, quite 
in a motherly way; while he consult me about vintage and wines, and 
taught me many a yorkshire wrinkle about horses. in my walk i met 
other stranger from time to time. even before my uncle had left me, i 
had noticed, with half-torpid curiosity, a young lady of very striking 
appearance, who went about always accompany by an elderly companion, 
hardly a gentlewoman, but with something in her look that prepossessed 
me in her favour. the younger lady always put her veil down when any 
one approached; so it had been only once or twice, when i had come upon 
her at a sudden turn in the path, that i had even had a glimpse of her 
face. i am not sure if it was beautiful, though in after-life i grew to 
think it so. but it was at this time over-shadowed by a sad that 
never varied: a pale, quiet, resigned look of intense suffering, that 
irresistible attract me, not with love, but with a sense of infinite 
compassion for one so young yet so hopeless unhappy. the companion 
wore something of the same look: quiet, melancholy, hopeless, yet 
resigned. i ask my landlord who they were. he said they were call 
clarke, and wish to be considered as mother and daughter; but that, 
for his part, he did not believe that to be their right name, or that 
there was any such relationship between them. they had been in the 
neighbourhood of harrogate for some time, lodging in a remote 
farm-house. the people there would tell nothing about them; saying that 
they paid handsomely, and never did any harm; so why should they be 
speak of any strange things that might happen? that, as the landlord 
shrewd observed, show there was something out of the common way: he 
had heard that the elderly woman was a cousin of the farmer's where 
they lodged, and so the regard existing between relations might help to 
keep them quiet. 

'what did he think, then, was the reason for their extreme seclusion?' 
ask i. 

'nay, he could not tell, not he. he had heard that the young lady, for 
all as quiet as she seemed, play strange prank at times.' he shook 
his head when i ask him for more particulars, and refuse to give 
them, which made me doubt if he knew any, for he was in general a 
talkative and communicative man. in default of other interests, after 
my uncle left, i set myself to watch these two people. i hover about 
their walks, drawn towards them with a strange fascination, which was 
not diminish by their evident annoyance at so frequent meeting me. 
one day, i had the sudden good fortune to be at hand when they were 
alarm by the attack of a bull, which, in those unenclosed graze 
districts, was a particularly dangerous occurrence. i have other and 
more important things to relate, than to tell of the accident which 
gave me an opportunity of rescue them; it is enough to say, that this 
event was the beginning of an acquaintance, reluctantly acquiesce in 
by them, but eager prosecute by me. i can hardly tell when intense 
curiosity became merge in love, but in less than ten days after my 
uncle's departure i was passionately enamoured of mrs. lucy, as her 
attendant call her; carefully--for this i noted well--avoiding any 
address which appear as if there was an equality of station between 
them. i notice also that mrs. clarke, the elderly woman, after her 
first reluctance to allow me to pay them any attention had been 
overcome, was cheer by my evident attachment to the young girl; it 
seem to lighten her heavy burden of care, and she evidently favoured 
my visit to the farm-house where they lodged. it was not so with lucy. 
a more attractive person i never saw, in spite of her depression of 
manner, and shrink avoidance of me. i felt sure at once, that 
whatever was the source of her grief, it rose from no fault of her own. 
it was difficult to draw her into conversation; but when at times, for 
a moment or two, i beguile her into talk, i could see a rare 
intelligence in her face, and a grave, trust look in the soft, grey 
eyes that were raise for a minute to mine. i made every excuse i 
possibly could for go there. i sought wild flower for lucy's sake; 
i plan walk for lucy's sake; i watch the heaven by night, in 
hope that some unusual beauty of sky would justify me in tempt mrs. 
clarke and lucy forth upon the moors, to gaze at the great purple dome 
above. 

it seem to me that lucy was aware of my love; but that, for some 
motive which i could not guess, she would fain have repel me; but 
then again i saw, or fancy i saw, that her heart spoke in my favour, 
and that there was a struggle go on in her mind, which at times (i 
love so dearly) i could have beg her to spare herself, even though 
the happiness of my whole life should have been the sacrifice; for her 
complexion grew paler, her aspect of sorrow more hopeless, her delicate 
frame yet slighter. during this period i had written, i should say, to 
my uncle, to beg to be allow to prolong my stay at harrogate, not 
give any reason; but such was his tender towards me, that in a 
few days i heard from him, give me a willing permission, and only 
charge me to take care of myself, and not use too much exertion 
during the hot weather. 

one sultry evening i drew near the farm. the window of their parlour 
were open, and i heard voice when i turn the corner of the house, as 
i pass the first window (there were two window in their little 
ground-floor room). i saw lucy distinctly; but when i had knock at 
their door--the house-door stood always ajar--she was gone, and i saw 
only mrs. clarke, turning over the work-things lie on the table, in a 
nervous and purposeless manner. i felt by instinct that a conversation 
of some importance was coming on, in which i should be expect to say 
what was my object in pay these frequent visits. i was glad of the 
opportunity. my uncle had several times allude to the pleasant 
possibility of my bring home a young wife, to cheer and adorn the 
old house in ormond street. he was rich, and i was to succeed him, and 
had, as i knew, a fair reputation for so young a lawyer. so on my side 
i saw no obstacle. it was true that lucy was shroud in mystery; her 
name (i was convinced it was not clarke), birth, parentage, and 
previous life were unknown to me. but i was sure of her goodness and 
sweet innocence, and although i knew that there must be something 
painful to be told, to account for her mournful sadness, yet i was 
willing to bear my share in her grief, whatever it might be. 

mrs. clarke began, as if it was a relief to her to plunge into the 
subject. 

'we have thought, sir--at least i have thought--that you know very 
little of us, nor we of you, indeed; not enough to warrant the intimate 
acquaintance we have fallen into. i beg your pardon, sir,' she went on, 
nervously; 'i am but a plain kind of woman, and i mean to use no 
rudeness; but i must say straight out that i--we--think it would be 
better for you not to come so often to see us. she is very unprotected, 
and----' 

'why should i not come to see you, dear madam?' ask i, eagerly, glad 
of the opportunity of explain myself. 'i come, i own, because i have 
learnt to love mistress lucy, and wish to teach her to love me.' 

mistress clarke shook her head, and sighed. 

'don't, sir--neither love her, nor, for the sake of all you hold 
sacred, teach her to love you! if i am too late, and you love her 
already, forget her,--forget these last few weeks. o! i should never 
have allow you to come!' she went on, passionately; 'but what am i to 
do? we are forsaken by all, except the great god, and even he permit a 
strange and evil power to afflict us--what am i to do? where is it to 
end?' she wrung her hand in her distress; then she turn to me: 'go 
away, sir; go away, before you learn to care any more for her. i ask it 
for your own sake--i implore. you have been good and kind to us, and we 
shall always recollect you with gratitude; but go away now, and never 
come back to cross our fatal path!' 

'indeed, madam,' said i, 'i shall do no such thing. you urge it for my 
own sake. i have no fear, so urged--nor wish, except to hear more--all. 
i cannot have seen mistress lucy in all the intimacy of this last 
fortnight, without acknowledge her goodness and innocence; and 
without seeing--pardon me, madam--that for some reason you are two very 
lonely women, in some mysterious sorrow and distress. now, though i am 
not powerful myself, yet i have friends who are so wise and kind, that 
they may be said to possess power. tell me some particulars. why are 
you in grief--what is your secret--why are you here? i declare solemn 
that nothing you have said has daunt me in my wish to become lucy's 
husband; nor will i shrink from any difficulty that, as such an 
aspirant, i may have to encounter. you say you are friendless--why cast 
away an honest friend? i will tell you of people to whom you may write, 
and who will answer any question as to my character and prospects. i 
do not shun inquiry.' 

she shook her head again. 'you had better go away, sir. you know 
nothing about us.' 

'i know your names,' said i, 'and i have heard you allude to the part 
of the country from which you came, which i happen to know as a wild 
and lonely place. there are so few people living in it that, if i chose 
to go there, i could easily ascertain all about you; but i would rather 
hear it from yourself.' you see i want to pique her into telling me 
something definite. 

'you do not know our true names, sir,' said she, hastily. 

'well, i may have conjecture as much. but tell me, then, i conjure 
you. give me your reason for distrust my willing to stand by 
what i have said with regard to mistress lucy.' 

'oh, what can i do?' exclaim she. 'if i am turning away a true friend 
as he says?--stay!' coming to a sudden decision--'i will tell you 
something--i cannot tell you all--you would not believe it. but, 
perhaps, i can tell you enough to prevent your go on in your 
hopeless attachment. i am not lucy's mother.' 

'so i conjectured,' i said. 'go on.' 

'i do not even know whether she is the legitimate or illegitimate child 
of her father. but he is cruel turn against her; and her mother is 
long dead; and, for a terrible reason, she has no other creature to 
keep constant to her but me. she--only two years ago--such a darling 
and such a pride in her father's house! why, sir, there is a mystery 
that might happen in connection with her any moment; and then you would 
go away like all the rest; and, when you next heard her name, you would 
loathe her. others, who have love her longer, have done so before now. 
my poor child, whom neither god nor man has mercy upon--or, surely, she 
would die!' 

the good woman was stop by her crying. i confess, i was a little 
stun by her last words; but only for a moment. at any rate, till i 
knew definitely what was this mysterious stain upon one so simple and 
pure, as lucy seemed, i would not desert her, and so i said; and she 
made answer: 

'if you are daring in your heart to think harm of my child, sir, after 
knowing her as you have done, you are no good man yourself; but i am so 
foolish and helpless in my great sorrow, that i would fain hope to find 
a friend in you. i cannot help trust that, although you may no 
long feel towards her as a lover, you will have pity upon us; and 
perhaps, by your learning, you can tell us where to go for aid.' 

'i implore you to tell me what this mystery is,' i cried, almost 
madden by this suspense. 

'i cannot,' said she, solemnly. 'i am under a deep vow of secrecy. if 
you are to be told, it must be by her.' she left the room, and i 
remain to ponder over this strange interview. i mechanical turn 
over the few books, and with eyes that saw nothing at the time, 
examine the token of lucy's frequent presence in that room. 

when i got home at night, i remember how all these trifle spoke of a 
pure and tender heart and innocent life. mistress clarke returned; she 
had been crying sadly. 

'yes,' said she, 'it is as i feared: she love you so much that she is 
willing to run the fearful risk of telling you all herself--she 
acknowledge it is but a poor chance; but your sympathy will be a balm, 
if you give it. to-morrow, come here at ten in the morning; and as you 
hope for pity in your hour of agony, repress all show of fear or 
repugnance you may feel towards one so grievous afflicted.' 

i half smiled. 'have no fear,' i said. it seem too absurd to imagine 
my feeling dislike to lucy. 

'her father love her well,' said she, gravely, 'yet he drove her out 
like some monstrous thing.' 

just at this moment came a peal of ring laughter from the garden. it 
was lucy's voice; it sound as if she were standing just on one side 
of the open casement--and as though she were sudden stir to 
merriment--merriment verge on boisterousness, by the doings or 
saying of some other person. i can scarcely say why, but the sound 
jar on me inexpressibly. she knew the subject of our conversation, 
and must have been at least aware of the state of agitation her friend 
was in: she herself usually so gentle and quiet. i half rose to go to 
the window, and satisfy my instinctive curiosity as to what had 
provoke this burst of ill-timed laughter; but mrs. clarke threw her 
whole weight and power upon the hand with which she pressed and kept me 
down. 

'for god's sake!' she said, white and tremble all over, 'sit still; 
be quiet. oh! be patient. to-morrow you will know all. leave us, for we 
are all sorely afflicted. do not seek to know more about us.' 

again that laugh--so musical in sound, yet so discordant to my heart. 
she held me tight--tighter; without positive violence i could not have 
risen. i was sitting with my back to the window, but i felt a shadow 
pass between the sun's warmth and me, and a strange shudder ran through 
my frame. in a minute or two she release me. 

'go,' repeated she. 'be warned, i ask you once more. i do not think you 
can stand this knowledge that you seek. if i had had my own way, lucy 
should never have yielded, and promise to tell you all. who know what 
may come of it?' 

'i am firm in my wish to know all. i return at ten to-morrow morning, 
and then expect to see mistress lucy herself.' 

i turn away; have my own suspicions, i confess, as to mistress 
clarke's sanity. 

conjecture as to the meaning of her hints, and uncomfortable thought 
connected with that strange laughter, fill my mind. i could hardly 
sleep. i rose early; and long before the hour i had appointed, i was on 
the path over the common that led to the old farm-house where they 
lodged. i suppose that lucy had pass no better a night than i; for 
there she was also, slow pace with her even step, her eyes bent 
down, her whole look most saintly and pure. she start when i came 
close to her, and grew pale as i remind her of my appointment, and 
spoke with something of the impatience of obstacle that, seeing her 
once more, had call up afresh in my mind. all strange and terrible 
hints, and giddy merriment were forgotten. my heart gave forth words of 
fire, and my tongue utter them. her colour went and came, as she 
listened; but, when i had end my passionate speeches, she lift her 
soft eyes to me, and said: 

'but you know that you have something to learn about me yet. i only 
want to say this: i shall not think less of you--less well of you, i 
mean--if you, too, fall away from me when you know all. stop!' said 
she, as if fear another burst of mad words. 'listen to me. my father 
is a man of great wealth. i never knew my mother; she must have die 
when i was very young. when first i remember anything, i was living in 
a great, lonely house, with my dear and faithful mistress clarke. my 
father, even, was not there; he was--he is--a soldier, and his duty 
lie abroad. but he came, from time to time, and every time i think he 
love me more and more. he brought me rarity from foreign lands, 
which prove to me now how much he must have thought of me during his 
absences. i can sit down and measure the depth of his lost love now, by 
such standard as these. i never thought whether he love me or not, 
then; it was so natural, that it was like the air i breathed. yet he 
was an angry man at times, even then; but never with me. he was very 
reckless, too; and, once or twice, i heard a whisper among the servant 
that a doom was over him, and that he knew it, and try to drown his 
knowledge in wild activity, and even sometimes, sir, in wine. so i grew 
up in this grand mansion, in that lonely place. everything around me 
seem at my disposal, and i think every one love me; i am sure love 
them. till about two years ago--i remember it well--my father had come 
to england, to us; and he seem so proud and so pleased with me and 
all i had done. and one day his tongue seem loosen with wine, and 
he told me much that i had not known till then,--how dearly he had 
love my mother, yet how his wilful usage had cause her death; and 
then he went on to say how he love me better than any creature on 
earth, and how, some day, he hope to take me to foreign places, for 
that he could hardly bear these long absence from his only child. then 
he seem to change suddenly, and said, in a strange, wild way, that i 
was not to believe what he said; that there was many a thing he love 
better--his horse--his dog--i know not what. 

'and 'twas only the next morning that, when i came into his room to ask 
his blessing as was my wont, he received me with fierce and angry 
words. 'why had i,' so he asked, 'been delight myself in such wanton 
mischief--dancing over the tender plant in the flower-beds, all set 
with the famous dutch bulb he had brought from holland?' i had never 
been out of door that morning, sir, and i could not conceive what he 
meant, and so i said; and then he swore at me for a liar, and said i 
was of no true blood, for he had seen me do all that mischief 
himself--with his own eyes. what could i say? he would not listen to 
me, and even my tear seem only to irritate him. that day was the 
beginning of my great sorrows. not long after, he reproach me for my 
undue familiarity--all unbecome a gentlewoman--with his grooms. i had 
been in the stable-yard, laugh and talking, he said. now, sir, i am 
something of a coward by nature, and i had always dread horses; 
besides that, my father's servants--those whom he brought with him from 
foreign parts--were wild fellows, whom i had always avoided, and to 
whom i had never spoken, except as a lady must needs from time to time 
speak to her father's people. yet my father call me by name of which 
i hardly know the meaning, but my heart told me they were such as shame 
any modest woman; and from that day he turn quite against me;--nay, 
sir, not many week after that, he came in with a riding-whip in his 
hand; and, accuse me harsh of evil doings, of which i knew no more 
than you, sir, he was about to strike me, and i, all in bewilder 
tears, was ready to take his stripe as great kindness compare to his 
hard words, when sudden he stop his arm mid-way, gasp and 
staggered, crying out, 'the curse--the curse!' i look up in terror. 
in the great mirror opposite i saw myself, and, right behind, another 
wicked, fearful self, so like me that my soul seem to quiver within 
me, as though not knowing to which similitude of body it belonged. my 
father saw my double at the same moment, either in its dreadful 
reality, whatever that might be, or in the scarcely less terrible 
reflection in the mirror; but what came of it at that moment i cannot 
say, for i sudden swoon away; and when i came to myself i was lie 
in my bed, and my faithful clarke sitting by me. i was in my bed for 
days; and even while i lay there my double was seen by all, flit 
about the house and gardens, always about some mischievous or 
detest work. what wonder that every one shrank from me in 
dread--that my father drove me forth at length, when the disgrace of 
which i was the cause was past his patience to bear. mistress clarke 
came with me; and here we try to live such a life of piety and prayer 
as may in time set me free from the curse.' 

all the time she had been speaking, i had been weigh her story in my 
mind. i had hitherto put case of witchcraft on one side, as mere 
superstitions; and my uncle and i had had many an argument, he 
support himself by the opinion of his good friend sir matthew hale. 
yet this sound like the tale of one bewitched; or was it merely the 
effect of a life of extreme seclusion telling on the nerves of a 
sensitive girl? my scepticism inclined me to the latter belief, and 
when she pause i said: 

'i fancy that some physician could have disabuse your father of his 
belief in visions----' 

just at that instant, standing as i was opposite to her in the full and 
perfect morning light, i saw behind her another figure--a ghastly 
resemblance, complete in likeness, so far as form and feature and 
minutest touch of dress could go, but with a loathsome demon soul 
look out of the grey eyes, that were in turn mock and 
voluptuous. my heart stood still within me; every hair rose up erect; 
my flesh crept with horror. i could not see the grave and tender 
lucy--my eyes were fascinate by the creature beyond. i know not why, 
but i put out my hand to clutch it; i grasp nothing but empty air, 
and my whole blood curdle to ice. for a moment i could not see; then 
my sight came back, and i saw lucy standing before me, alone, deathly 
pale, and, i could have fancied, almost, shrunk in size. 

'it has been near me?' she said, as if ask a question. 

the sound seem taken out of her voice; it was husky as the note on 
an old harpsichord when the strings have cease to vibrate. she read 
her answer in my face, i suppose, for i could not speak. her look was 
one of intense fear, but that die away into an aspect of most humble 
patience. at length she seem to force herself to face behind and 
around her: she saw the purple moors, the blue distant hills, quiver 
in the sunlight, but nothing else. 

'will you take me home?' she said, meekly. 

i took her by the hand, and led her silent through the budding 
heather--we dare not speak; for we could not tell but that the dread 
creature was listening, although unseen,--but that it might appear and 
push us asunder. i never love her more fondly than now when--and that 
was the unspeakable misery--the idea of her was becoming so 
inextricable blend with the shudder thought of it. she seem to 
understand what i must be feeling. she let go my hand, which she had 
kept clasp until then, when we reach the garden gate, and went 
forward to meet her anxious friend, who was standing by the window 
look for her. i could not enter the house: i need silence, 
society, leisure, change--i knew not what--to shake off the sensation 
of that creature's presence. yet i linger about the garden--i hardly 
know why; i partly suppose, because i fear to encounter the 
resemblance again on the solitary common, where it had vanished, and 
partly from a feeling of inexpressible compassion for lucy. in a few 
minutes mistress clarke came forth and join me. we walk some pace 
in silence. 

'you know all now,' said she, solemnly. 

'i saw it,' said i, below my breath. 

'and you shrink from us, now,' she said, with a hopeless which 
stir up all that was brave or good in me. 

'not a whit,' said i. 'human flesh shrink from encounter with the 
powers of darkness: and, for some reason unknown to me, the pure and 
holy lucy is their victim.' 

'the sin of the father shall be visit upon the children,' she said. 

'who is her father?' ask i. 'knowing as much as i do, i may surely 
know more--know all. tell me, i entreat you, madam, all that you can 
conjecture respecting this demoniac persecution of one so good.' 

'i will; but not now. i must go to lucy now. come this afternoon, i 
will see you alone; and oh, sir! i will trust that you may yet find 
some way to help us in our sore trouble!' 

i was miserably exhaust by the swoon affright which had taken 
possession of me. when i reach the inn, i stagger in like one 
overcome by wine. i went to my own private room. it was some time 
before i saw that the weekly post had come in, and brought me my 
letters. there was one from my uncle, one from my home in devonshire, 
and one, re-directed over the first address, seal with a great coat 
of arms. it was from sir philip tempest: my letter of inquiry 
respecting mary fitzgerald had reach him at liège, where it so 
happen that the count de la tour d'auvergne was quarter at the very 
time. he remember his wife's beautiful attendant; she had had high 
words with the deceased countess, respecting her intercourse with an 
english gentleman of good standing, who was also in the foreign 
service. the countess augur evil of his intentions; while mary, proud 
and vehement, assert that he would soon marry her, and resent her 
mistress's warning as an insult. the consequence was, that she had 
left madame de la tour d'auvergne's service, and, as the count 
believed, had gone to live with the englishman; whether he had married 
her, or not, he could not say. 'but,' add sir philip tempest, 'you 
may easily hear what particulars you wish to know respecting mary 
fitzgerald from the englishman himself, if, as i suspect, he is no 
other than my neighbour and former acquaintance, mr. gisborne, of 
skipford hall, in the west riding. i am led to the belief that he is no 
other by several small particulars, none of which are in themselves 
conclusive, but which, taken together, make a mass of presumptive 
evidence. as far as i could make out from the count's foreign 
pronunciation, gisborne was the name of the englishman: i know that 
gisborne of skipford was abroad and in the foreign service at that 
time--he was a likely fellow enough for such an exploit, and, above 
all, certain expression recur to my mind which he use in reference to 
old bridget fitzgerald, of coldholme, whom he once encounter while 
stay with me at starkey manor-house. i remember that the meeting 
seem to have produce some extraordinary effect upon his mind, as 
though he had sudden discover some connection which she might have 
had with his previous life. i beg you to let me know if i can be of any 
further service to you. your uncle once render me a good turn, and i 
will gladly repay it, so far as in me lies, to his nephew.' 

i was now apparently close on the discovery which i had striven so many 
month to attain. but success had lost its zest. i put my letters down, 
and seem to forget them all in thinking of the morning i had pass 
that very day. nothing was real but the unreal presence, which had come 
like an evil blast across my bodily eyes, and burnt itself down upon my 
brain. dinner came, and went away untouched. early in the afternoon i 
walk to the farm-house. i found mistress clarke alone, and i was glad 
and relieved. she was evidently prepared to tell me all i might wish to 
hear. 

'you ask me for mistress lucy's true name; it is gisborne,' she 
began. 

'not gisborne of skipford?' i exclaimed, breathless with anticipation. 

'the same,' said she, quietly, not regarding my manner. 'her father is 
a man of note; although, being a roman catholic, he cannot take that 
rank in this country to which his station entitle him. the consequence 
is that he lives much abroad--has been a soldier, i am told.' 

'and lucy's mother?' i asked. 

she shook her head. 'i never knew her,' said she. 'lucy was about three 
years old when i was engaged to take charge of her. her mother was 
dead.' 

'but you know her name?--you can tell if it was mary fitzgerald?' 

she look astonished. 'that was her name. but, sir, how came you to be 
so well acquaint with it? it was a mystery to the whole household at 
skipford court. she was some beautiful young woman whom he lure away 
from her protector while he was abroad. i have heard said he practised 
some terrible deceit upon her, and when she came to know it, she was 
neither to have nor to hold, but rush off from his very arms, and 
threw herself into a rapid stream and was drowned. it stung him deep 
with remorse, but i use to think the remembrance of the mother's cruel 
death made him love the child yet dearer.' 

i told her, as brief as might be, of my research after the 
descendant and heir of the fitzgerald of kildoon, and added--something 
of my old lawyer spirit return into me for the moment--that i had no 
doubt but that we should prove lucy to be by right possessed of large 
estate in ireland. 

no flush came over her grey face; no light into her eyes. 'and what is 
all the wealth in the whole world to that poor girl?' she said. 'it 
will not free her from the ghastly bewitch which persecute her. as 
for money, what a pitiful thing it is; it cannot touch her.' 

'no more can the evil creature harm her,' i said. 'her holy nature 
dwell apart, and cannot be defile or stain by all the devilish arts 
in the whole world.' 

'true! but it is a cruel fate to know that all shrink from her, soon 
or later, as from one possessed--accursed.' 

'how came it to pass?' i asked. 

'nay, i know not. old rumour there are, that were bruit through the 
household at skipford.' 

'tell me,' i demanded. 

'they came from servants, who would fain account for everything. they 
say that, many years ago, mr. gisborne kill a dog belong to an old 
witch at coldholme; that she cursed, with a dreadful and mysterious 
curse, the creature, whatever it might be, that he should love best; 
and that it struck so deep into his heart that for years he kept 
himself aloof from any temptation to love aught. but who could help 
loving lucy?' 

'you never heard the witch's name?' i gasped. 

'yes--they call her bridget; they said he would never go near the 
spot again for terror of her. yet he was a brave man!' 

'listen,' said i, taking hold of her arm, the better to arrest her full 
attention; 'if what i suspect hold true, that man stole bridget's only 
child--the very mary fitzgerald who was lucy's mother; if so, bridget 
cursed him in ignorance of the deep wrong he had done her. to this 
hour she yearn after her lost child, and question the saint whether 
she be living or not. the roots of that curse lie deep than she 
knows: she unwitting ban him for a deep guilt than that of 
killing a dumb beast. the sin of the father are indeed visit upon 
the children.' 

'but,' said mistress clarke, eagerly, 'she would never let evil rest on 
her own grandchild? surely, sir, if what you say be true, there are 
hope for lucy. let us go--go at once, and tell this fearful woman all 
that you suspect, and beseech her to take off the spell she has put 
upon her innocent grandchild.' 

it seem to me, indeed, that something like this was the best course 
we could pursue. but first it was necessary to ascertain more than what 
mere rumour or careless hearsay could tell. my thought turn to my 
uncle--he could advise me wisely--he ought to know all. i resolve to 
go to him without delay; but i did not choose to tell mistress clarke 
of all the visionary plan that flit through my mind. i simply 
declared my intention of proceeding straight to london on lucy's 
affairs. i bade her believe that my interest on the young lady's behalf 
was greater than ever, and that my whole time should be given up to her 
cause. i saw that mistress clarke distrust me, because my mind was 
too full of thought for my words to flow freely. she sigh and shook 
her head, and said, 'well, it is all right!' in such a tone that it was 
an imply reproach. but i was firm and constant in my heart, and i 
took confidence from that. 

i rode to london. i rode long days drawn out into the lovely summer 
nights: i could not rest. i reach london. i told my uncle all, though 
in the stir of the great city the horror had fade away, and i could 
hardly imagine that he would believe the account i gave him of the 
fearful double of lucy which i had seen on the lonely moor-side. but my 
uncle had live many years, and learnt many things; and, in the deep 
secret of family history that had been confide to him, he had heard 
of case of innocent people bewitch and taken possession of by evil 
spirits yet more fearful than lucy's. for, as he said, to judge from 
all i told him, that resemblance had no power over her--she was too 
pure and good to be taint by its evil, haunting presence. it had, in 
all probability, so my uncle conceived, try to suggest wicked 
thought and to tempt to wicked actions; but she, in her saintly 
maidenhood, had pass on undefiled by evil thought or deed. it could 
not touch her soul: but true, it set her apart from all sweet love or 
common human intercourse. my uncle threw himself with an energy more 
like six-and-twenty than sixty into the consideration of the whole 
case. he undertook the prove lucy's descent, and volunteer to go 
and find out mr. gisborne, and obtain, firstly, the legal proof of her 
descent from the fitzgerald of kildoon, and, secondly, to try and hear 
all that he could respecting the working of the curse, and whether any 
and what means had been taken to exorcise that terrible appearance. for 
he told me of instance where, by prayer and long fasting, the evil 
possessor had been driven forth with howling and many cries from the 
body which it had come to inhabit; he spoke of those strange new 
england case which had happen not so long before; of mr. defoe, who 
had written a book, wherein he had name many mode of subdue 
apparitions, and send them back whence they came; and, lastly, he 
spoke low of dreadful ways of compel witch to undo their 
witchcraft. but i could not endure to hear of those torture and 
burnings. i said that bridget was rather a wild and savage woman than a 
malignant witch; and, above all, that lucy was of her kith and kin; and 
that, in putt her to the trial, by water or by fire, we should be 
torturing--it might be to the death--the ancestress of her we sought to 
redeem. 

my uncle thought awhile, and then said, that in this last matter i was 
right--at any rate, it should not be tried, with his consent, till all 
other mode of remedy had failed; and he assent to my proposal that i 
should go myself and see bridget, and tell her all. 

in accordance with this, i went down once more to the wayside inn near 
coldholme. it was late at night when i arrive there; and, while i 
supped, i inquire of the landlord more particulars as to bridget's 
ways. solitary and savage had been her life for many years. wild and 
despotic were her words and manner to those few people who came across 
her path. the country-folk did her imperious bidding, because they 
fear to disobey. if they pleased her, they prospered; if, on the 
contrary, they neglect or traverse her behests, misfortune, small or 
great, fell on them and theirs. it was not detest so much as an 
indefinable terror that she excited. 

in the morning i went to see her. she was standing on the green outside 
her cottage, and received me with the sullen grandeur of a throneless 
queen. i read in her face that she recognise me, and that i was not 
unwelcome; but she stood silent till i had open my errand. 

'i have news of your daughter,' said i, resolve to speak straight to 
all that i knew she felt of love, and not to spare her. 'she is dead!' 

the stern figure scarcely trembled, but her hand sought the support of 
the door-post. 

'i knew that she was dead,' said she, deep and low, and then was silent 
for an instant. 'my tear that should have flow for her were burnt up 
long years ago. young man, tell me about her.' 

'not yet,' said i, have a strange power given me of confront one, 
whom, nevertheless, in my secret soul i dreaded. 

'you had once a little dog,' i continued. the words call out in her 
more show of emotion than the intelligence of her daughter's death. she 
broke in upon my speech: 

'i had! it was hers--the last thing i had of hers--and it was shot for 
wantonness! it die in my arms. the man who kill that dog rue it to 
this day. for that dumb beast's blood, his best-beloved stand 
accursed.' 

her eyes distended, as if she were in a trance and saw the working of 
her curse. again i spoke: 

'o, woman!' i said, 'that best-beloved, standing accursed before men, 
is your dead daughter's child.' 

the life, the energy, the passion came back to the eyes with which she 
pierce through me, to see if i spoke truth; then, without another 
question or word, she threw herself on the ground with fearful 
vehemence, and clutch at the innocent daisy with convulse hands. 

'bone of my bone! flesh of my flesh! have i cursed thee--and art thou 
accursed?' 

so she moaned, as she lay prostrate in her great agony. i stood aghast 
at my own work. she did not hear my broken sentences; she ask no 
more, but the dumb confirmation which my sad looks had given that one 
fact, that her curse rest on her own daughter's child. the fear grew 
on me lest she should die in her strife of body and soul; and then 
might not lucy remain under the spell as long as she lived? 

even at this moment, i saw lucy coming through the woodland path that 
led to bridget's cottage; mistress clarke was with her: i felt at my 
heart that it was she, by the balmy peace which the look of her sent 
over me, as she slow advanced, a glad surprise shining out of her 
soft quiet eyes. that was as her gaze met mine. as her looks fell on 
the woman lie stiff, convulse on the earth, they became full of 
tender pity; and she came forward to try and lift her up. seating 
herself on the turf, she took bridget's head into her lap; and, with 
gentle touches, she arrange the dishevelled grey hair stream thick 
and wild from beneath her mutch. 

'god help her!' murmur lucy. 'how she suffers!' 

at her desire we sought for water; but when we returned, bridget had 
recover her wandering senses, and was kneel with clasp hand 
before lucy, gaze at that sweet sad face as though her trouble 
nature drank in health and peace from every moment's contemplation. a 
faint tinge on lucy's pale cheek show me that she was aware of our 
return; otherwise it appear as if she was conscious of her influence 
for good over the passionate and trouble woman kneel before her, 
and would not willing avert her grave and loving eyes from that 
wrinkle and careworn countenance. 

suddenly--in the twinkling of an eye--the creature appeared, there, 
behind lucy; fearful the same as to outward semblance, but kneel 
exactly as bridget knelt, and clasp her hand in jesting mimicry as 
bridget clasp hers in her ecstasy that was deepen into a prayer. 
mistress clarke cry out--bridget arose slowly, her gaze fixed on the 
creature beyond: drawing her breath with a hiss sound, never moving 
her terrible eyes, that were steady as stone, she made a dart at the 
phantom, and caught, as i had done, a mere handful of empty air. we saw 
no more of the creature--it vanish as sudden as it came, but 
bridget look slow on, as if watch some recede form. lucy sat 
still, white, trembling, drooping--i think she would have swoon if i 
had not been there to uphold her. while i was attend to her, bridget 
pass us, without a word to any one, and, enter her cottage, she 
barred herself in, and left us without. 

all our endeavour were now direct to get lucy back to the house 
where she had tarry the night before. mistress clarke told me that, 
not hearing from me (some letter must have miscarried), she had grown 
impatient and despairing, and had urge lucy to the enterprise of 
coming to seek her grandmother; not telling her, indeed, of the dread 
reputation she possessed, or how we suspect her of have so 
fearful blight that innocent girl; but, at the same time, hope 
much from the mysterious stirring of blood, which mistress clarke 
trust in for the removal of the curse. they had come, by a different 
route from that which i had taken, to a village inn not far from 
coldholme, only the night before. this was the first interview between 
ancestress and descendant. 

all through the sultry noon i wander along the tangle wood-paths of 
the old neglect forest, thinking where to turn for remedy in a matter 
so complicated and mysterious. meeting a countryman, i ask my way to 
the nearest clergyman, and went, hope to obtain some counsel from 
him. but he prove to be a coarse and common-minded man, give no time 
or attention to the intricacy of a case, but dashing out a strong 
opinion involve immediate action. for instance, as soon as i name 
bridget fitzgerald, he exclaimed: 

'the coldholme witch! the ireland papist! i'd have had her duck long 
since but for that other papist, sir philip tempest. he has had to 
threaten honest folk about here over and over again, or they'd have had 
her up before the justice for her black doings. and it's the law of 
the land that witch should be burnt! ay, and of scripture, too, sir! 
yet you see a papist, if he's a rich squire, can overrule both law and 
scripture. i'd carry a fagot myself to rid the country of her!' 

such a one could give me no help. i rather drew back what i had already 
said; and try to make the parson forget it, by treat him to 
several pot of beer, in the village inn, to which we had adjourn for 
our conference at his suggestion. i left him as soon as i could, and 
return to coldholme, shape my way past desert starkey 
manor-house, and coming upon it by the back. at that side were the 
oblong remains of the old moat, the waters of which lay placid and 
motionless under the crimson ray of the setting sun; with the 
forest-trees lie straight along each side, and their deep-green 
foliage mirror to black in the burnish surface of the moat 
below--and the broken sun-dial at the end nearest the hall--and the 
heron, standing on one leg at the water's edge, lazy look down for 
fish--the lonely and desolate house scarce need the broken windows, 
the weeds on the door-sill, the broken shutter soft flap to and 
fro in the twilight breeze, to fill up the picture of desertion and 
decay. i linger about the place until the grow dark warn me 
on. and then i pass along the path, cut by the orders of the last 
lady of starkey manor-house, that led me to bridget's cottage. i 
resolve at once to see her; and, in spite of closed doors--it might be 
of resolve will--she should see me. so i knock at her door, gently, 
loudly, fiercely. i shook it so vehement that at length the old 
hinge gave way, and with a crash it fell inwards, leave me sudden 
face to face with bridget--i, red, heated, agitate with my so 
long-baffled efforts--she, stiff as any stone, standing right facing 
me, her eyes dilate with terror, her ashen lip trembling, but her 
body motionless. in her hand she held her crucifix, as if by that holy 
symbol she sought to oppose my entrance. at sight of me, her whole 
frame relaxed, and she sank back upon a chair. some mighty tension had 
given way. still her eyes look fearful into the gloom of the outer 
air, made more opaque by the glimmer of the lamp inside, which she had 
placed before the picture of the virgin. 

'is she there?' ask bridget, hoarsely. 

'no! who? i am alone. you remember me.' 

'yes,' reply she, still terror-stricken. 'but she--that creature--has 
been look in upon me through that window all day long. i closed it 
up with my shawl; and then i saw her feet below the door, as long as it 
was light, and i knew she heard my very breathing--nay, worse, my very 
prayers; and i could not pray, for her listen choke the words ere 
they rose to my lip. tell me, who is she?--what means that double girl 
i saw this morning? one had a look of my dead mary; but the other 
curdle my blood, and yet it was the same!' 

she had taken hold of my arm, as if to secure herself some human 
companionship. she shook all over with the slight, never-ceasing tremor 
of intense terror. i told her my tale, as i have told it you, sparing 
none of the details. 

how mistress clarke had informed me that the resemblance had driven 
lucy forth from her father's house--how i had disbelieved, until, with 
mine own eyes, i had seen another lucy standing behind my lucy, the 
same in form and feature, but with the demon-soul look out of the 
eyes. i told her all, i say, believe that she--whose curse was 
working so upon the life of her innocent grandchild--was the only 
person who could find the remedy and the redemption. when i had done, 
she sat silent for many minutes. 

'you love mary's child?' she asked. 

'i do, in spite of the fearful working of the curse--i love her. yet i 
shrink from her ever since that day on the moor-side. and men must 
shrink from one so accompanied; friends and lovers must stand afar off. 
oh, bridget fitzgerald! loosen the curse! set her free!' 

'where is she?' 

i eager caught at the idea that her presence was needed, in order 
that, by some strange prayer or exorcism, the spell might be reversed. 

'i will go and bring her to you,' i exclaimed. but bridget tighten 
her hold upon my arm. 

'not so,' said she, in a low, hoarse voice. 'it would kill me to see 
her again as i saw her this morning. and i must live till i have work 
my work. leave me!' said she, suddenly, and again taking up the cross. 
'i defy the demon i have call up. leave me to wrestle with it!' 

she stood up, as if in an ecstasy of inspiration, from which all fear 
was banished. i lingered--why, i can hardly tell--until once more she 
bade me begone. as i went along the forest way, i look back, and saw 
her plant the cross in the empty threshold, where the door had been. 

the next morning lucy and i went to seek her, to bid her join her 
prayer with ours. the cottage stood open and wide to our gaze. no 
human being was there: the cross remain on the threshold, but bridget 
was gone. 




chapter 3 


what was to be done next? was the question that i ask myself. as for 
lucy, she would fain have submit to the doom that lay upon her. her 
gentle and piety, under the pressure of so horrible a life, seem 
over-passive to me. she never complained. mrs. clarke complain more 
than ever. as for me, i was more in love with the real lucy than ever; 
but i shrunk from the false similitude with an intensity proportion 
to my love. i found out by instinct that mrs. clarke had occasional 
temptation to leave lucy. the good lady's nerves were shaken, and, 
from what she said, i could almost have conclude that the object of 
the double was to drive away from lucy this last and almost earliest 
friend. at times, i could scarcely bear to own it, but i myself felt 
inclined to turn recreant; and i would accuse lucy of being too 
patient--too resigned. one after another, she won the little children 
of coldholme. (mrs. clarke and she had resolve to stay there, for was 
it not as good a place as any other to such as they? and did not all 
our faint hope rest on bridget--never seen or heard of now, but still 
we trust to come back, or give some token?) so, as i say, one after 
another, the little children came about my lucy, won by her soft tones, 
and her gentle smiles, and kind actions. alas! one after another they 
fell away, and shrunk from her path with blanch terror; and we too 
surely guess the reason why. it was the last drop. i could bear it no 
longer. i resolve no more to linger around the spot, but to go back to 
my uncle, and among the learned divine of the city of london, seek for 
some power whereby to annul the curse. 

my uncle, meanwhile, had obtain all the requisite testimonial 
relate to lucy's descent and birth, from the ireland lawyers, and from 
mr. gisborne. the latter gentleman had written from abroad (he was 
again serving in the austria army), a letter alternate passionately 
self-reproachful and stoical repellent. it was evident that when he 
thought of mary--her short life--how he had wrong her, and of her 
violent death, he could hardly find words severe enough for his own 
conduct; and from this point of view, the curse that bridget had laid 
upon him and his was regard by him as a prophetic doom, to the 
utterance of which she was move by a higher power, working for the 
fulfilment of a deep vengeance than for the death of the poor dog. 
but then, again, when he came to speak of his daughter, the repugnance 
which the conduct of the demoniac creature had produce in his mind, 
was but ill disguise under a show of profound indifference as to 
lucy's fate. one almost felt as if he would have been as content to put 
her out of existence, as he would have been to destroy some disgust 
reptile that had invade his chamber or his couch. 

the great fitzgerald property was lucy's; and that was all--was 
nothing. 

my uncle and i sat in the gloom of a london november evening, in our 
house in ormond street. _i_ was out of health, and felt as if i were in 
an inextricable coil of misery. lucy and i wrote to each other, but 
that was little; and we dare not see each other for dread of the 
fearful third, who had more than once taken her place at our meetings. 
my uncle had, on the day i speak of, bidden prayer to be put up, on 
the ensue sabbath, in many a church and meeting-house in london, for 
one grievous torment by an evil spirit. he had faith in prayers--i 
had none; i was fast lose faith in all things. so we sat--he try 
to interest me in the old talk of other days, i oppress by one 
thought--when our old servant, anthony, open the door, and, without 
speaking, show in a very gentlemanly and prepossessing man, who had 
something remarkable about his dress, betray his profession to be 
that of the roman catholic priesthood. he glance at my uncle first, 
then at me. it was to me he bowed. 'i did not give my name,' said he, 
'because you would hardly have recognise it; unless, sir, when in the 
north, you heard of father bernard, the chaplain at stoney hurst?' 

i remember afterwards that i had heard of him, but at the time i had 
utterly forgotten it; so i professed myself a complete stranger to him; 
while my ever-hospitable uncle, although hate a papist as much as it 
was in his nature to hate anything, placed a chair for the visitor, and 
bade anthony bring glasses and a fresh jug of claret. 

father bernard received this courtesy with the graceful ease and 
pleasant acknowledgment which belong to the man of the world. then he 
turn to scan me with his keen glance. after some slight conversation, 
enter into on his part, i am certain, with an intention of 
discover on what terms of confidence i stood with my uncle, he 
paused, and said gravely: 

'i am sent here with a message to you, sir, from a woman to whom you 
have shown kindness, and who is one of my penitents, in antwerp--one 
bridget fitzgerald.' 

'bridget fitzgerald!' exclaim i. 'in antwerp? tell me, sir, all that 
you can about her.' 

'there is much to be said,' he replied. 'but may i inquire if this 
gentleman--if your uncle is acquaint with the particulars of which 
you and i stand informed?' 

'all that i know, he knows,' said i, eager lay my hand on my 
uncle's arm, as he made a motion as if to quit the room. 

'then i have to speak before two gentlemen who, however they may differ 
from me in faith, are yet fully impress with the fact, that there are 
evil powers go about continual to take cognizance of our evil 
thoughts; and, if their master give them power, to bring them into 
overt action. such is my theory of the nature of that sin, of which i 
dare not disbelieve--as some sceptic would have us do--the sin of 
witchcraft. of this deadly sin, you and i are aware bridget fitzgerald 
has been guilty. since you saw her last, many prayer have been offer 
in our churches, many masses sung, many penance undergone, in order 
that, if god and the holy saint so will it, her sin might be blot 
out. but it has not been so willed.' 

'explain to me,' said i, 'who you are, and how you come connected with 
bridget. why is she at antwerp? i pray you, sir, tell me more. if i am 
impatient, excuse me; i am ill and feverish, and in consequence 
bewildered.' 

there was something to me inexpressible soothe in the tone of voice 
with which he began to narrate, as it were from the beginning, his 
acquaintance with bridget. 

'i had known mr. and mrs. starkey during their residence abroad, and so 
it fell out naturally that, when i came as chaplain to the sherburne 
at stoney hurst, our acquaintance was renewed; and thus i became the 
confessor of the whole family, isolated as they were from the offices 
of the church, sherburne being their nearest neighbour who professed 
the true faith. of course, you are aware that fact reveal in 
confession are seal as in the grave; but i learnt enough of bridget's 
character to be convinced that i had to do with no common woman; one 
powerful for good as for evil. i believe that i was able to give her 
spiritual assistance from time to time, and that she look upon me as 
a servant of that holy church, which has such wonderful power of moving 
men's hearts, and relieve them of the burden of their sins. i have 
known her cross the moor on the wildest nights of storm, to confess 
and be absolved; and then she would return, calm and subdued, to her 
daily work about her mistress, no one witting where she had been during 
the hour that most pass in sleep upon their beds. after her 
daughter's departure--after mary's mysterious disappearance--i had to 
impose many a long penance, in order to wash away the sin of impatient 
repine that was fast leading her into the deep guilt of blasphemy. 
she set out on that long journey of which you have possibly heard--that 
fruitless journey in search of mary--and during her absence, my 
superior ordered my return to my former duty at antwerp, and for 
many years i heard no more of bridget. 

'not many month ago, as i was passing homewards in the evening, along 
one of the street near st. jacques, leading into the meer straet, i 
saw a woman sitting crouch up under the shrine of the holy mother of 
sorrows. her hood was drawn over her head, so that the shadow cause by 
the light of the lamp above fell deep over her face; her hand were 
clasp round her knees. it was evident that she was some one in 
hopeless trouble, and as such it was my duty to stop and speak. i 
naturally address her first in flemish, believe her to be one of 
the lower class of inhabitants. she shook her head, but did not look 
up. then i try french, and she reply in that language, but speak 
it so indifferently, that i was sure she was either english or irish, 
and consequently spoke to her in my own native tongue. she recognise 
my voice; and, start up, caught at my robes, drag me before the 
blessed shrine, and throw herself down, and force me, as much by 
her evident desire as by her action, to kneel beside her, she 
exclaimed: 

'"o holy virgin! you will never hearken to me again, but hear him; for 
you know him of old, that he do your bidding, and strive to heal 
broken hearts. hear him!" 

'she turn to me. 

'"she will hear you, if you will only pray. she never hear me: she and 
all the saint in heaven cannot hear my prayers, for the evil one 
carry them off, as he carry that first away. o, father bernard, 
pray for me!" 

'i pray for one in sore distress, of what nature i could not say; but 
the holy virgin would know. bridget held me fast, gasp with 
eager at the sound of my words. when i had ended, i rose, and, 
making the sign of the cross over her, i was go to bless her in the 
name of the holy church, when she shrank away like some terrify 
creature, and said: 

'"i am guilty of deadly sin, and am not shriven." 

'"arise, my daughter," said i, "and come with me." and i led the way 
into one of the confessional of st. jacques. 

'she knelt; i listened. no words came. the evil powers had stricken her 
dumb, as i heard afterwards they had many a time before, when she 
approach confession. 

'she was too poor to pay for the necessary form of exorcism; and 
hitherto those priest to whom she had address herself were either so 
ignorant of the meaning of her broken french, or her irish-english, or 
else esteem her to be one crazed--as, indeed, her wild and excited 
manner might easily have led any one to think--that they had neglect 
the sole means of loosen her tongue, so that she might confess her 
deadly sin, and after due penance, obtain absolution. but i knew 
bridget of old, and felt that she was a penitent sent to me. i went 
through those holy offices appoint by our church for the relief of 
such a case. i was the more bound to do this, as i found that she had 
come to antwerp for the sole purpose of discover me, and making 
confession to me. of the nature of that fearful confession i am 
forbidden to speak. much of it you know; possibly all. 

'it now remains for her to free herself from mortal guilt, and to set 
other free from the consequence thereof. no prayer, no masses, will 
ever do it, although they may strengthen her with that strength by 
which alone acts of deepest love and purest self-devotion may be 
performed. her words of passion, and cries for revenge--her unholy 
prayer could never reach the ear of the holy saints! other powers 
intercept them, and wrought so that the curse thrown up to heaven 
have fallen on her own flesh and blood; and so, through her very 
strength of love, have bruise and crush her heart. henceforward her 
former self must be buried,--yea, bury quick, if need be,--but never 
more to make sign, or utter cry on earth! she has become a poor clare, 
in order that, by perpetual penance and constant service of others, she 
may at length so act as to obtain final absolution and rest for her 
soul. until then, the innocent must suffer. it is to plead for the 
innocent that i come to you; not in the name of the witch, bridget 
fitzgerald, but of the penitent and servant of all men, the poor clare, 
sister magdalen.' 

'sir,' said i, 'i listen to your request with respect; only i may tell 
you it is not need to urge me to do all that i can on behalf of one, 
love for whom is part of my very life. if for a time i have absent 
myself from her, it is to think and work for her redemption. i, a 
member of the english church--my uncle, a puritan--pray morning and 
night for her by name: the congregation of london, on the next 
sabbath, will pray for one unknown, that she may be set free from the 
powers of darkness. moreover, i must tell you, sir, that those evil 
one touch not the great calm of her soul. she lives her own pure and 
loving life, unharmed and untainted, though all men fall off from her. 
i would i could have her faith!' 

my uncle now spoke. 

'nephew,' said he, 'it seem to me that this gentleman, although 
profess what i consider an erroneous creed, has touched upon the 
right point in exhort bridget to acts of love and mercy, whereby to 
wipe out her sin of hate and vengeance. let us strive after our 
fashion, by almsgive and visiting of the needy and fatherless, to 
make our prayer acceptable. meanwhile, i myself will go down into the 
north, and take charge of the maiden. i am too old to be daunt by man 
or demon. i will bring her to this house as to a home; and let the 
double come if it will! a company of godly divine shall give it the 
meeting, and we will try issue.' 

the kindly brave old man! but father bernard sat on musing. 

'all hate,' said he, 'cannot be quench in her heart; all christian 
forgiveness cannot have enter into her soul, or the demon would have 
lost its power. you said, i think, that her grandchild was still 
tormented?' 

'still tormented!' i replied, sadly, thinking of mistress clarke's last 
letter. 

he rose to go. we afterwards heard that the occasion of his coming to 
london was a secret politics mission on behalf of the jacobites. 
nevertheless, he was a good and a wise man. 

month and month pass away without any change. lucy entreat my 
uncle to leave her where she was,--dreading, as i learnt, lest if she 
came, with her fearful companion, to dwell in the same house with me, 
that my love could not stand the repeated shock to which i should be 
doomed. and this she thought from no distrust of the strength of my 
affection, but from a kind of pity sympathy for the terror to the 
nerves which she observe that the demoniac visitation cause in all. 

i was restless and miserable. i devoted myself to good works; but i 
perform them from no spirit of love, but solely from the hope of 
reward and payment, and so the reward was never granted. at length, i 
ask my uncle's leave to travel; and i went forth, a wanderer, with no 
distinct end than that of many another wanderer--to get away from 
myself. a strange impulse led me to antwerp, in spite of the war and 
commotion then rage in the low countries--or rather, perhaps, the 
very craving to become interested in something external, led me into 
the thick of the struggle then go on with the austrians. the city 
of flander were all full at that time of civil disturbance and 
rebellions, only kept down by force, and the presence of an austria 
garrison in every place. 

i arrive in antwerp, and made inquiry for father bernard. he was away 
in the country for a day or two. then i ask my way to the convent of 
poor clares; but, being healthy and prosperous, i could only see the 
dim, pent-up, grey walls, shut close in by narrow streets, in the 
lowest part of the town. my landlord told me, that had i been stricken 
by some loathsome disease, or in desperate case of any kind, the poor 
clare would have taken me, and tend me. he spoke of them as an order 
of mercy of the strictest kind, dressing scanty in the coarsest 
materials, go barefoot, living on what the inhabitant of antwerp 
chose to bestow, and share even those fragment and crumb with the 
poor and helpless that swarm all around; receiving no letters or 
communication with the outer world; utterly dead to everything but the 
alleviate of suffering. he smile at my inquiring whether i could get 
speech of one of them; and told me that they were even forbidden to 
speak for the purpose of beg their daily food; while yet they 
lived, and fed other upon what was given in charity. 

'but,' exclaim i, 'supposing all men forgot them! would they quiet 
lie down and die, without making sign of their extremity?' 

'if such were their rule, the poor clare would willing do it; but 
their founder appoint a remedy for such extreme case as you suggest. 
they have a bell--'tis but a small one, as i have heard, and has yet 
never been rung in the memory of man: when the poor clare have been 
without food for twenty-four hours, they may ring this bell, and then 
trust to our good people of antwerp for rush to the rescue of the 
poor clares, who have taken such blessed care of us in all our 
straits.' 

it seem to me that such rescue would be late in the day; but i did 
not say what i thought. i rather turn the conversation, by ask my 
landlord if he knew, or had ever heard, anything of a certain sister 
magdalen. 

'yes,' said he, rather under his breath; 'news will creep out, even 
from a convent of poor clares. sister magdalen is either a great sinner 
or a great saint. she do more, as i have heard, than all the other 
nun put together; yet, when last month they would fain have made her 
mother-superior, she beg rather that they would place her below all 
the rest, and make her the meanest servant of all.' 

'you never saw her?' ask i. 

'never,' he replied. 

i was weary of wait for father bernard, and yet i linger in 
antwerp. the politics state of things became worse than ever, 
increase to its height by the scarcity of food consequent on many 
deficient harvests. i saw group of fierce, squalid men, at every 
corner of the street, glaring out with wolfish eyes at my sleek skin 
and handsome clothes. 

at last father bernard returned. we had a long conversation, in which 
he told me that, curious enough, mr. gisborne, lucy's father, was 
serving in one of the austria regiments, then in garrison at antwerp.
