i ask father bernard if he would make us acquainted; which he 
consent to do. but, a day or two afterwards, he told me that, on 
hearing my name, mr. gisborne had decline respond to any advances 
on my part, saying he had abjure his country, and hate his 
countrymen. 

probably he recollect my name in connection with that of his daughter 
lucy. anyhow, it was clear enough that i had no chance of making his 
acquaintance. father bernard confirmed me in my suspicion of the 
hidden fermentation, for some coming evil, working among the 'blouses' 
of antwerp, and he would fain have had me depart from out the city; but 
i rather crave the excitement of danger, and stubborn refuse to 
leave. 

one day, when i was walking with him in the place verte, he bowed to an 
austria officer, who was crossing towards the cathedral. 

'that is mr. gisborne,' said he, as soon as the gentleman was past. 

i turn to look at the tall, slight figure of the officer. he carry 
himself in a stately manner, although he was past middle age, and from 
his years, might have had some excuse for a slight stoop. as i look 
at the man, he turn round, his eyes met mine, and i saw his face. 
deep lined, sallow, and scathe was that countenance; scar by 
passion as well as by the fortune of war. 'twas but a moment our eyes 
met. we each turn round, and went on our separate way. 

but his whole appearance was not one to be easily forgotten; the 
thorough appointment of the dress, and evident thought bestow on it, 
made but an incongruous whole with the dark, gloomy expression of his 
countenance. because he was lucy's father, i sought instinctive to 
meet him everywhere. at last he must have become aware of my 
pertinacity, for he gave me a haughty scowl whenever i pass him. in 
one of these encounters, however, i chance to be of some service to 
him. he was turning the corner of a street, and came sudden on one of 
the group of discontented fleming of whom i have spoken. some words 
were exchanged, when my gentleman out with his sword, and with a slight 
but skilful cut drew blood from one of those who had insult him, as 
he fancied, though i was too far off to hear the words. they would all 
have fallen upon him had i not rush forward and raise the cry, then 
well known in antwerp, of rally, to the austria soldier who were 
perpetual patrol the streets, and who came in numbers to the 
rescue. i think that neither mr. gisborne nor the mutinous group of 
plebeian owe me much gratitude for my interference. he had plant 
himself against a wall, in a skilful attitude of fence, ready with his 
bright glancing rapier to do battle with all the heavy, fierce, unarmed 
men, some six or seven in number. but when his own soldier came up, he 
sheathe his sword; and, give some careless word of command, sent 
them away again, and continue his saunter all alone down the street, 
the workmen snarl in his rear, and more than half-inclined to fall 
on me for my cry for rescue. i care not if they did, my life seem so 
dreary a burden just then; and, perhaps, it was this daring loiter 
among them that prevent their attack me. instead, they suffer me 
to fall into conversation with them; and i heard some of their 
grievances. sore and heavy to be borne were they, and no wonder the 
sufferer were savage and desperate. 

the man whom gisborne had wound across his face would fain have got 
out of me the name of his aggressor, but i refuse to tell it. another 
of the group heard his inquiry, and made answer: 

'i know the man. he is one gisborne, aide-de-camp to the 
general-commandant. i know him well.' 

he began to tell some story in connection with gisborne in a low and 
mutter voice; and while he was relate a tale, which i saw excited 
their evil blood, and which they evidently wish me not to hear, i 
saunter away and back to my lodgings. 

that night antwerp was in open revolt. the inhabitant rose in 
rebellion against their austria masters. the austrians, holding the 
gate of the city, remain at first pretty quiet in the citadel; only, 
from time to time, the boom of a great cannon swept sullen over the 
town. but, if they expect the disturbance to die away, and spend 
itself in a few hours' fury, they were mistaken. in a day or two, the 
riot held possession of the principal municipal buildings. then the 
austria pour forth in bright flaming array, calm and smiling, as 
they march to the post assigned, as if the fierce mob were no more 
to them than the swarm of buzz summer flies. their practised 
manoeuvres, their well-aimed shot, told with terrible effect; but in 
the place of one slain rioter, three sprang up of his blood to avenge 
his loss. but a deadly foe, a ghastly ally of the austrians, was at 
work. food, scarce and dear for months, was now hardly to be obtain 
at any price. desperate effort were being made to bring provisions 
into the city, for the riot had friends without. close to the city 
port nearest to the scheldt, a great struggle took place. i was there, 
helping the rioters, whose cause i had adopted. we had a savage 
encounter with the austrians. numbers fell on both sides; i saw them 
lie bleeding for a moment; then a volley of smoke obscure them; and 
when it clear away, they were dead--trampled upon or smothered, 
pressed down and hidden by the freshly-wounded whom those last gun had 
brought low. and then a grey-robed and grey-veiled figure came right 
across the flash guns, and stoop over some one, whose life-blood 
was ebb away; sometimes it was to give him drink from can which 
they carry slung at their sides, sometimes i saw the cross held above 
a die man, and rapid prayer were being uttered, unheard by men in 
that hellish din and clangour, but listen to by one above. i saw all 
this as in a dream: the reality of that stern time was battle and 
carnage. but i knew that these grey figures, their bare feet all wet 
with blood, and their face hidden by their veils, were the poor 
clares--sent forth now because dire agony was abroad and imminent 
danger at hand. therefore, they left their cloister shelter, and came 
into that thick and evil mêlée. 

close to me--driven past me by the struggle of many fighters--came the 
antwerp burgess with the scarce-healed scar upon his face; and in an 
instant more, he was thrown by the press upon the austria officer 
gisborne, and ere either had recover the shock, the burgess had 
recognise his opponent. 

'ha! the englishman gisborne!' he cried, and threw himself upon him 
with redouble fury. he had struck him hard--the englishman was down; 
when out of the smoke came a dark-grey figure, and threw herself right 
under the uplift flash sword. the burgess's arm stood arrested. 
neither austria nor anversoi willing harm the poor clares. 

'leave him to me!' said a low stern voice. 'he is mine enemy--mine for 
many years.' 

those words were the last i heard. i myself was struck down by a 
bullet. i remember nothing more for days. when i came to myself, i was 
at the extremity of weakness, and was craving for food to recruit my 
strength. my landlord sat watch me. he, too, look pinched and 
shrunken; he had heard of my wound state, and sought me out. yes! the 
struggle still continued, but the famine was sore; and some, he had 
heard, had die for lack of food. the tear stood in his eyes as he 
spoke. but soon he shook off his weakness, and his natural cheerful 
returned. father bernard had been to see me--no one else. (who should, 
indeed?) father bernard would come back that afternoon--he had 
promised. but father bernard never came, although i was up and dressed, 
and look eager for him. 

my landlord brought me a meal which he had cook himself: of what it 
was compose he would not say, but it was most excellent, and with 
every mouthful i seem to gain strength. the good man sat look at 
my evident enjoyment with a happy smile of sympathy; but, as my 
appetite became satisfied, i began to detect a certain wistful in 
his eyes, as if craving for the food i had so nearly devoured--for, 
indeed, at that time i was hardly aware of the extent of the famine. 
suddenly, there was a sound of many rush feet past our window. my 
landlord open one of the side of it, the better to learn what was 
go on. then we heard a faint, cracked, tinkle bell, coming shrill 
upon clear and distinct from all other sounds. 'holy mother!' exclaim 
my landlord, 'the poor clares!' 

he snatch up the fragment of my meal, and cram them into my 
hands, bidding me follow. down-stairs he ran, clutch at more food, 
as the women of his house eager held it out to him; and in a moment 
we were in the street, moving along with the great current, all tend 
towards the convent of the poor clares. and still, as if piercing our 
ear with its inarticulate cry, came the shrill tinkle of the bell. in 
that strange crowd were old men tremble and sobbing, as they carry 
their little pittance of food; women with the tear running down their 
cheeks, who had snatch up what provisions they had in the vessel in 
which they stood, so that the burden of these was in many case much 
greater than that which they contained; children, with flushed faces, 
grasping tight the morsel of bitten cake or bread, in their eager 
to carry it safe to the help of the poor clares; strong men--yea, both 
anversoi and austrians--pressing onwards with set teeth, and no word 
spoken; and over all, and through all, came that sharp tinkle--that cry 
for help in extremity. 

we met the first torrent of people return with blanch and piteous 
faces: they were issue out of the convent to make way for the 
offering of others. 'haste, haste!' said they. 

'a poor clare is dying! a poor clare is dead for hunger! god forgive 
us, and our city!' 

we pressed on. the stream bore us along where it would. we were carry 
through refectories, bare and crumbless; into cell over whose door 
the conventual name of the occupant was written. thus it was that i, 
with others, was forced into sister magdalen's cell. on her couch lay 
gisborne, pale unto death, but not dead. by his side was a cup of 
water, and a small morsel of mouldy bread, which he had pushed out of 
his reach, and could not move to obtain. over against his bed were 
these words, copy in the english version: 'therefore, if thine enemy 
hunger, feed him; if he thirst, give him drink.' 

some of us gave him of our food, and left him eat greedily, like 
some famished wild animal. for now it was no long the sharp tinkle, 
but that one solemn toll, which in all christian country tell of the 
passing of the spirit out of earthly life into eternity; and again a 
murmur gather and grew, as of many people speak with awed breath, 
'a poor clare is dying! a poor clare is dead!' 

borne along once more by the motion of the crowd, we were carry into 
the chapel belong to the poor clares. on a bier before the high 
altar, lay a woman--lay sister magdalen--lay bridget fitzgerald. by her 
side stood father bernard, in his robe of office, and holding the 
crucifix on high while he pronounced the solemn absolution of the 
church, as to one who had newly confessed herself of deadly sin. i 
pushed on with passionate force, till i stood close to the die woman, 
as she received extreme unction amid the breathless and awed hush of 
the multitude around. her eyes were glazing, her limb were stiffening; 
but when the rite was over and finished, she raise her gaunt figure 
slow up, and her eyes brighten to a strange intensity of joy, as, 
with the gesture of her finger and the trance-like gleam of her eye, 
she seem like one who watch the disappearance of some loathe and 
fearful creature. 

'she is free from the curse!' said she, as she fell back dead. 

* * * * * 

now, of all our party who had first listen to my lady ludlow, mr. 
preston was the only one who had not told us something, either of 
information, tradition, history, or legend. we naturally turn to him; 
but we did not like ask him directly for his contribution, for he 
was a grave, reserved, and silent man. 

he understood us, however, and, rousing himself as it were, he said: 

'i know you wish me to tell you, in my turn, of something which i have 
learnt or heard during my life. i could tell you something of my own 
life, and of a life dear still to my memory; but i have shrunk from 
narrate anything so purely personal. yet, shrink as i will, no other 
but those sad recollection will present themselves to my mind. i call 
them sad when i think of the end of it all. however i am not go to 
moralize. if my dear brother's life and death do not speak for 
itself, no words of mine will teach you what may be learnt from it.' 




loi the witch 

chapter 1 


in the year 1691, loi barclay stood on a little wooden pier, steady 
herself on the stable land, in much the same manner as, eight or nine 
week ago, she had try to steady herself on the deck of the rock 
ship which had carry her across from old to new england. it seem as 
strange now to be on solid earth as it had been, not long ago, to be 
rock by the sea, both by day and by night; and the aspect of the land 
was equally strange. the forest which show in the distance all 
round, and which, in truth, were not very far from the wooden house 
form the town of boston, were of different shades of green, and 
different, too, in shape of outline to those which loi barclay knew 
well in her old home in warwickshire. her heart sank a little as she 
stood alone, wait for the captain of the good ship redemption, the 
kind rough old sailor, who was her only known friend in this unknown 
continent. captain holdernesse was busy, however, as she saw, and it 
would probably be some time before he would be ready to attend, to her; 
so loi sat down on one of the cask that lay about, and wrap her 
grey duffle cloak tight around her, and sheltered herself under her 
hood, as well as might be, from the piercing wind, which seem to 
follow those whom it had tyrannize over at sea with a dogged wish of 
still torment them on land. very patient did loi sit there, 
although she was weary, and shiver with cold; for the day was severe 
for may, and the redemption, with store of necessaries and comfort for 
the puritan colonist of new england, was the earliest ship that had 
venture across the seas. 

how could loi help thinking of the past, and speculate on the 
future, as she sat on boston pier, at this breathing-time of her life? 
in the dim sea-mist which she gaze upon with ache eyes (filled, 
against her will, with tears, from time to time), there rose the little 
village church of barford (not three mile from warwick--you may see it 
yet), where her father had preach ever since 1661, long before she 
was born. he and her mother both lay dead in barford churchyard; and 
the old low grey church could hardly come before her vision without her 
seeing the old parsonage too, the cottage cover with austria roses, 
and yellow jessamine, where she had been born, sole child of parent 
already long past the prime of youth. she saw the path, not a hundred 
yard long, from the parsonage to the vestry door: that path which her 
father trod daily; for the vestry was his study, and the sanctum, where 
he pore over the ponderous tome of the father, and compare their 
precept with those of the authority of the anglican church of that 
day--the day of the later stuarts; for barford parsonage at that time 
scarcely exceed in size and dignity the cottage by which it was 
surrounded: it only contained three rooms on a floor, and was but two 
story high. on the first, or ground floor, were the parlour, kitchen, 
and back or working kitchen; up-stairs, mr. and mrs. barclay's room, 
that belong to lois, and the maid-servant's room. if a guest came, 
loi left her own chamber, and share old clemence's bed. but those 
days were over. never more should loi see father or mother on earth; 
they slept, calm and still, in barford churchyard, careless of what 
became of their orphan child, as far as earthly manifestation of care 
or love went. and clemence lay there too, bound down in her grassy bed 
by withe of the briar-rose, which loi had train over those three 
precious grave before leave england for ever. 

there were some who would fain have kept her there; one who swore in 
his heart a great oath unto the lord that he would seek her soon or 
later, if she was still upon the earth. but he was the rich heir and 
only son of the miller lucy, whose mill stood by the avon-side in the 
grassy barford meadows, and his father look higher for him than the 
penniless daughter of parson barclay (so low were clergymen esteem in 
those days!); and the very suspicion of hugh lucy's attachment to loi 
barclay made his parent think it more prudent not to offer the orphan 
a home, although none other of the parishioner had the means, even if 
they had the will, to do so. 

so loi swallow her tear down till the time came for crying, and 
act upon her mother's words: 

'lois, thy father is dead of this terrible fever, and i am dying. nay, 
it is so, though i am easy from pain for these few hours, the lord be 
praised! the cruel men of the commonwealth have left thee very 
friendless. thy father's only brother was shot down at edgehill. i, 
too, have a brother, though thou hast never heard me speak of him, for 
he was a schismatic; and thy father and he had words, and he left for 
that new country beyond the seas, without ever saying farewell to us. 
but ralph was a kind lad until he took up these new-fangled notions, 
and for the old days' sake he will take thee in, and love thee as a 
child, and place thee among his children. blood is thick than water. 
write to him as soon as i am gone--for lois, i am going--and i bless 
the lord that has letten me join my husband again so soon.' such was 
the selfish of conjugal love; she thought little of lois's 
desolate in comparison with her rejoicing over her speedy reunion 
with her dead husband! 'write to thine uncle, ralph hickson, salem, new 
england (put it down, child, on thy tablets), and say that i, henrietta 
barclay, charge him, for the sake of all he hold dear in heaven or on 
earth,--for his salvation's sake, as well as for the sake of the old 
home at lester-bridge,--for the sake of the father and mother that gave 
us birth, as well as for the sake of the six little children who lie 
dead between him and me,--that he take thee into his home as if thou 
wert his own flesh and blood, as indeed thou art. he has a wife and 
children of his own, and no one need fear have thee, my lois, my 
darling, my baby, among his household. oh, lois, would that thou wert 
die with me! the thought of thee make death sore!' loi comfort 
her mother more than herself, poor child, by promise to obey her die 
wish to the letter, and by express hope she dare not feel of her 
uncle's kindness. 

'promise me'--the die woman's breath came hard and harder--'that 
thou wilt go at once. the money our goods will bring--the letter thy 
father wrote to captain holdernesse, his old schoolfellow--thou knowest 
all i would say--my lois, god bless thee!' 

solemn did loi promise; strict she kept her word. it was all the 
more easy, for hugh lucy met her, and told her, in one great burst of 
love, of his passionate attachment, his vehement struggle with his 
father, his impotence at present, his hope and resolve for the future. 
and, intermingle with all this, came such outrageous threat and 
expression of uncontroll vehemence, that loi felt that in barford 
she must not linger to be a cause of desperate quarrel between father 
and son, while her absence might soften down matters, so that either 
the rich old miller might relent, or--and her heart ache to think of 
the other possibility--hugh's love might cool, and the dear play-fellow 
of her childhood learn to forget. if not--if hugh were to be trust in 
one tithe of what he said--god might permit him to fulfil his resolve 
of coming to seek her out before many years were over. it was all in 
god's hands, and that was best, thought loi barclay. 

she was rouse out of her trance of recollection by captain 
holdernesse, who, have done all that was necessary in the way of 
orders and directions to his mate, now came up to her, and, praise 
her for her quiet patience, told her that he would now take her to the 
widow smith's, a decent kind of house, where he and many other sailor 
of the better order were in the habit of lodging, during their stay on 
the new england shores. widow smith, he said, had a parlour for herself 
and her daughters, in which loi might sit, while he went about the 
business that, as he had told her, would detain him in boston for a day 
or two, before he could accompany her to her uncle's at salem. all this 
had been to a certain degree arrange on ship-board; but captain 
holdernesse, for want of anything else that he could think of to talk 
about, recapitulate it as he and loi walk along. it was his way of 
showing sympathy with the emotion that made her grey eyes full of 
tears, as she start up from the pier at the sound of his voice. in 
his heart he said, 'poor wench! poor wench! it's a strange land to her, 
and they are all strange folks, and, i reckon, she will be feeling 
desolate. i'll try and cheer her up.' so he talk on about hard facts, 
connected with the life that lay before her, until they reach widow 
smith's; and perhaps loi was more brighten by this style of 
conversation, and the new idea it present to her, than she would 
have been by the tenderest woman's sympathy. 

'they are a queer set, these new englanders,' said captain holdernesse. 
'they are rare chaps for praying; down on their knee at every turn of 
their life. folk are none so busy in a new country, else they would 
have to pray like me, with a "yo-hoy!" on each side of my prayers, and 
a rope cutting like fire through my hand. yon pilot was for calling us 
all to thanksgiving for a good voyage, and lucky escape from the 
pirates; but i said i always put up my thanks on dry land, after i had 
got my ship into harbour. the france colonists, too, are vow 
vengeance for the expedition against canada, and the people here are 
rage like heathens--at least, as like as godly folk can be--for the 
loss of their charter. all that is the news the pilot told me; for, for 
all he want us to be thanksgiving instead of casting the lead, he was 
as down in the mouth as could be about the state of the country. but 
here we are at widow smith's! now, cheer up, and show the godly a 
pretty smile warwickshire lass!' 

anybody would have smile at widow smith's greeting. she was a comely, 
motherly woman, dress in the primmest fashion in vogue twenty years 
before, in england, among the class to which she belonged. but, 
somehow, her pleasant face gave the lie to her dress; were it as brown 
and sober-coloured as could be, folk remember it bright and cheerful, 
because it was a part of widow smith herself. 

she kiss loi on both cheeks, before she rightly understood who the 
stranger maiden was, only because she was a stranger, and look sad 
and forlorn; and then she kiss her again, because captain holdernesse 
commend her to the widow's good offices. and so she led loi by the 
hand into her rough, substantial log-house, over the door of which hung 
a great bough of a tree, by way of sign of entertainment for man and 
horse. yet not all men were received by widow smith. to some she could 
be as cold and reserved as need be, deaf to all inquiry save 
one--where else they could find accommodation? to this question she 
would give a ready answer, and speed the unwelcome guest on his way. 
widow smith was guide in these matter by instinct: one glance at a 
man's face told her whether or not she chose to have him as an inmate 
of the same house as her daughters; and her promptness of decision in 
these matter gave her manner a kind of authority which no one like to 
disobey, especially as she had stalwart neighbour within call to back 
her, if her assume deaf in the first instance, and her voice and 
gesture in the second, were not enough to give the would-be guest his 
dismissal. widow smith chose her customer merely by their physical 
aspect; not one whit with regard to their apparent worldly 
circumstances. those who had been stay at her house once, always 
came again, for she had the knack of making every one beneath her roof 
comfortable and at his ease. her daughters, prudence and hester, had 
somewhat of their mother's gifts, but not in such perfection. they 
reasoned a little upon a stranger's appearance, instead of knowing at 
the first moment whether they like him or no; they notice the 
indication of his clothes, the quality and cut thereof, as telling 
somewhat of his station in society; they were more reserved, they 
hesitate more than their mother; they had not her prompt authority, 
her happy power. their bread was not so light, their cream went 
sometimes to sleep when it should have been turning into butter, their 
ham were not always 'just like the ham of the old country,' as their 
mother's were invariable pronounced to be; yet they were good, orderly, 
kindly girls, and rose and greet loi with a friendly shake of the 
hand, as their mother, with her arm round the stranger's waist, led her 
into the private room which she call her parlour. the aspect of this 
room was strange in the english girl's eyes. the log of which the 
house was built, show here and there through the mud plaster, 
although before both plaster and log were hung the skin of many 
curious animals,--skins present to the widow by many a trader of her 
acquaintance, just as her sailor guest brought her another description 
of gift--shells, strings of wampum-beads, sea-birds' eggs, and presents 
from the old country. the room was more like a small museum of natural 
history of these days than a parlour; and it had a strange, peculiar, 
but not unpleasant smell about it, neutralize in some degree by the 
smoke from the enormous trunk of pinewood which smoulder on the 
hearth. 

the instant their mother told them that captain holdernesse was in the 
outer room, the girl began putt away their spinning-wheel and 
knitting-needles, and prepare for a meal of some kind; what meal, 
lois, sitting there and unconscious watching, could hardly tell. 
first, dough was set to rise for cakes; then came out of a corner 
cupboard--a present from england--an enormous square bottle of a 
cordial call golden wasser; next, a mill for grind chocolate--a 
rare unusual treat anywhere at that time; then a great cheshire cheese. 
three venison steak were cut ready for broiling, fat cold pork slice 
up and treacle pour over it, a great pie something like a mince-pie, 
but which the daughter spoke of with honour as the 'punken-pie,' fresh 
and salt fish brandered, oyster cook in various ways. loi wonder 
where would be the end of the provisions for hospitable receiving the 
stranger from the old country. at length everything was placed on the 
table, the hot food smoking; but all was cool, not to say cold, before 
elder hawkin (an old neighbour of much repute and standing, who had 
been invite in by widow smith to hear the news) had finished his 
grace, into which was embody thanksgiving for the past and prayer 
for the future lives of every individual present, adapt to their 
several cases, as far as the elder could guess at them from 
appearances. this grace might not have end so soon as it did, had it 
not been for the somewhat impatient drum of his knife-handle on the 
table with which captain holdernesse accompany the latter half of the 
elder's words. 

when they first sat down to their meal, all were too hungry for much 
talking; but as their appetite diminish their curiosity increased, 
and there was much to be told and heard on both sides. with all the 
english intelligence loi was, of course, well acquainted; but she 
listen with natural attention to all that was said about the new 
country, and the new people among whom she had come to live. her father 
had been a jacobite, as the adherent of the stuart were beginning at 
this time to be called. his father, again, had been a follower of 
archbishop laud; so loi had hitherto heard little of the conversation, 
and seen little of the ways of the puritans. elder hawkin was one of 
the strictest of the strict, and evidently his presence kept the two 
daughter of the house considerably in awe. but the widow herself was a 
privileged person; her known goodness of heart (the effects of which 
had been experienced by many) gave her the liberty of speech which was 
tacit deny to many, under penalty of being esteem ungodly if they 
infringe certain conventional limits. and captain holdernesse and his 
mate spoke out their minds, let who would be present. so that on this 
first landing in new england, loi was, as it were, gently let down 
into the midst of the puritan peculiarities, and yet they were 
sufficient to make her feel very lonely and strange. 

the first subject of conversation was the present state of the 
colony--lois soon found out that, although at the beginning she was not 
a little perplexed by the frequent reference to name of place which 
she naturally associate with the old country. widow smith was 
speaking: 'in the county of essex the folk are ordered to keep four 
scouts, or company of minute-men; six person in each company; to be 
on the look-out for the wild indians, who are for ever stirring about 
in the woods, stealthy brute as they are! i am sure, i got such a 
fright the first harvest-time after i came over to new england, i go on 
dreaming, now near twenty years after lothrop's business, of paint 
indians, with their shaven scalp and their war-streaks, lurk behind 
the trees, and coming near and near with their noiseless steps.' 

'yes,' broke in one of her daughters; 'and, mother, don't you remember 
how hannah benson told us how her husband had cut down every tree near 
his house at deerbrook, in order that no one might come near him, under 
cover; and how one evening she was a-sitting in the twilight, when all 
her family were gone to bed, and her husband gone off to plymouth on 
business, and she saw a log of wood, just like a trunk of a fell 
tree, lie in the shadow, and thought nothing of it, till, on look 
again a while after, she fancy it was come a bit near to the house, 
and how her heart turn sick with fright, and how she dare not stir 
at first, but shut her eyes while she count a hundred, and look 
again, and the shadow was deeper, but she could see that the log was 
nearer; so she ran in and bolt the door, and went up to where her 
eldest lad lay. it was elijah, and he was but sixteen then; but he rose 
up at his mother's words, and took his father's long duck-gun down, and 
he try the loading, and spoke for the first time to put up a prayer 
that god would give his aim good guidance, and went to a window that 
gave a view upon the side where the log lay, and fired, and no one 
dare to look what came of it, but all the household read the 
scriptures, and pray the whole night long, till morning came and 
show a long stream of blood lie on the grass close by the log, 
which the full sunlight show to be no log at all, but just a red 
indian cover with bark, and paint most skilfully, with his 
war-knife by his side.' 

all were breathless with listening, though to most the story, or such 
like it, were familiar. then another took up the tale of horror: 

'and the pirate have been down at marblehead since you were here, 
captain holdernesse. 'twas only the last winter they landed,--french 
papist pirates; and the people kept close within their houses, for they 
knew not what would come of it; and they drag folk ashore. there was 
one woman among those folk--prisoners from some vessel, doubtless--and 
the pirate took them by force to the inland marsh; and the marblehead 
folk kept still and quiet, every gun loaded, and every ear on the 
watch, for who knew but what the wild sea-robbers might take a turn on 
land next; and, in the dead of the night, they heard a woman's loud and 
pitiful outcry from the marsh, 'lord jesu! have mercy on me! save me 
from the power of man, o lord jesu!' and the blood of all who heard the 
cry ran cold with terror, till old nance hickson, who had been 
stone-deaf and bedridden for years, stood up in the midst of the folk 
all gather together in her grandson's house, and said, that as they, 
the dwell in marblehead, had not had brave heart or faith enough to 
go and succour the helpless, that cry of a die woman should be in 
their ears, and in their children's ears, till the end of the world. 
and nance drop down dead as soon as she had made an end of speaking, 
and the pirate set sail from marblehead at morning dawn; but the folk 
there hear the cry still, shrill and pitiful, from the waste marshes, 
"lord jesu! have mercy on me! save me from the power of man, o lord 
jesu!"' 

'and by token,' said elder hawkins's deep bass voice, speak with the 
strong nasal twang of the puritan (who, say butler, 

"blasphemed custard through the nose"), 

'godly mr. noye ordain a fast at marblehead, and preach a 
soul-stirring discourse on the words; "inasmuch as ye did it not unto 
one of the least of these, my brethren, ye did it not unto me." but it 
has been borne in upon me at times, whether the whole vision of the 
pirate and the cry of the woman was not a device of satan's to sift 
the marblehead folk, and see what fruit their doctrine bore, and so to 
condemn them in the sight of the lord. if it were so, the enemy had a 
great triumph, for assured it was no part of christian men to leave a 
helpless woman unaided in her sore distress.' 

'but, elder,' said widow smith, 'it was no vision; they were real 
living men who went ashore, men who broke down branch and left their 
footmark on the ground.' 

'as for that matter, satan hath many powers, and if it be the day when 
he is permit to go about like a roaring lion, he will not stick at 
trifles, but make his work complete. i tell you, many men are spiritual 
enemy in visible forms, permit to roam about the waste place of 
the earth. i myself believe that these red indian are indeed the evil 
creature of whom we read in holy scripture; and there is no doubt that 
they are in league with those abominable papists, the france people in 
canada. i have heard tell, that the france pay the indian so much gold 
for every dozen scalp off englishmen's heads.' 

'pretty cheerful talk this,' said captain holdernesse to lois, 
perceive her blanch cheek and terror-stricken mien. 'thou art 
thinking that thou hadst better have stay at barford, i'll answer for 
it, wench. but the devil is not so black as he is painted.' 

'ho! there again!' said elder hawkins. 'the devil is painted, it hath 
been said so from old times; and are not these indian painted, even 
like unto their father?' 

'but is it all true?' ask lois, aside, of captain holdernesse, 
letting the elder hold forth unheeded by her, though listen to, 
however, with the utmost reverence by the two daughter of the house. 

'my wench,' said the old sailor, 'thou hast come to a country where 
there are many perils, both from land and from sea. the indian hate 
the white men. whether other white men' (meaning the france away to the 
north) 'have hound on the savages, or whether the english have taken 
their lands and hunting-grounds without due recompense, and so raise 
the cruel vengeance of the wild creatures--who knows? but it is true 
that it is not safe to go far into the woods, for fear of the lurk 
paint savages; nor has it been safe to build a dwelling far from a 
settlement; and it take a brave heart to make a journey from one town 
to another, and folk do say the indian creature rise up out of the 
very ground to waylay the english; and then offer affirm they are all 
in league with satan to affright the christian out of the heathen 
country over which he has reign so long. then, again, the seashore is 
infest by pirates, the scum of all nations: they land, and plunder, 
and ravage, and burn, and destroy. folk get affrighte of the real 
dangers, and in their fright imagine, perchance, danger that are not. 
but who knows? holy scripture speak of witch and wizards, and of the 
power of the evil one in desert places; and even in the old country we 
have heard tell of those who have sold their soul for ever for the 
little power they get for a few years on earth.' 

by this time the whole table was silent, listen to the captain; it 
was just one of those chance silence that sometimes occur, without any 
apparent reason, and often without any apparent consequence. but all 
present had reason, before many month had-passed over, to remember the 
words which loi spoke in answer, although her voice was low, and she 
only thought, in the interest of the moment, of being heard by her old 
friend the captain. 

'they are fearful creatures, the witches! and yet i am sorry for the 
poor old women, whilst i dread them. we had one in barford, when i was 
a little child. no one knew whence she came, but she settled herself 
down in a mud hut by the common side; and there she lived, she and her 
cat.' (at the mention of the cat, elder hawkin shook his head long and 
gloomily.) 'no one knew how she lived, if it were not on nettle and 
scraps of oatmeal and such-like food given her more for fear than for 
pity. she went double, always talk and mutter to herself. folk 
said she snare bird and rabbits, in the thicket that came down to her 
hovel. how it came to pass i cannot say, but many a one fell sick in 
the village, and much cattle die one spring, when i was near four 
years old. i never heard much about it, for my father said it was ill 
talk about such things; i only know i got a sick fright one 
afternoon, when the maid had gone out for milk and had taken me with 
her, and we were passing a meadow where the avon, circling, make a 
deep round pool, and there was a crowd of folk, all still--and a still, 
breathless crowd make the heart beat worse than a shouting, noisy one. 
they were all gaze towards the water, and the maid held me up in her 
arms to see the sight above the shoulder of the people; and i saw old 
hannah in the water, her grey hair all stream down her shoulders, 
and her face bloody and black with the stone and the mud they had been 
throw at her, and her cat tie round her neck. i hid my face, i 
know, as soon as i saw the fearsome sight, for her eyes met mine as 
they were glaring with fury--poor, helpless, bait creature!--and she 
caught the sight of me, and cry out, "parson's wench, parson's wench, 
yonder, in thy nurse's arms, thy dad hath never try for to save me, 
and none shall save thee when thou art brought up for a witch." oh! the 
words rang in my ears, when i was drop asleep, for years after. i 
use to dream that i was in that pond, all men hate me with their 
eyes because i was a witch; and, at times, her black cat use to seem 
living again, and say over those dreadful words.' 

loi stopped: the two daughter look at her excitement with a kind of 
shrink surprise, for the tear were in her eyes. elder hawkin shook 
his head, and mutter text from scripture; but cheerful widow smith, 
not liking the gloomy turn of the conversation, try to give it a 
lighter cast by saying, 'and i don't doubt but what the parson's bonny 
lass has bewitch many a one since, with her dimple and her pleasant 
ways--eh, captain holdernesse? it's you must tell us tale of this 
young lass' doings in england.' 

'ay, ay,' said the captain, 'there's one under her charm in 
warwickshire who will never get the better of it, i'm thinking.' 

elder hawkin rose to speak; he stood leaning on his hands, which were 
placed on the table: 'brethren,' said he, 'i must upbraid you if ye 
speak lightly; charm and witchcraft are evil things. i trust this 
maiden hath had nothing to do with them, even in thought. but my mind 
misgive me at her story. the hellish witch might have power from satan 
to infect her mind, she being yet a child, with the deadly sin. instead 
of vain talking, i call upon you all to join with me in prayer for this 
stranger in our land, that her heart may be purge from all iniquity. 
let us pray.' 

'come, there's no harm in that,' said the captain; 'but, elder hawkins, 
when you are at work, just pray for us all, for i am afeard there be 
some of us need purge from iniquity a good deal more than loi 
barclay, and a prayer for a man never do mischief.' 

captain holdernesse had business in boston which detain him there for 
a couple of days, and during that time loi remain with the widow 
smith, seeing what was to be seen of the new land that contained her 
future home. the letter of her die mother was sent off to salem, 
meanwhile, by a lad go thither, in order to prepare her uncle ralph 
hickson for his niece's coming, as soon as captain holdernesse could 
find leisure to take her; for he considered her given into his own 
personal charge, until he could consign her to her uncle's care. when 
the time came for go to salem, loi felt very sad at leave the 
kindly woman under whose roof she had been staying, and look back as 
long as she could see anything of widow smith's dwelling. she was 
packed into a rough kind of country cart, which just held her and 
captain holdernesse, beside the driver. there was a basket of 
provisions under their feet, and behind them hung a bag of provender 
for the horse; for it was a good day's journey to salem, and the road 
was reputed so dangerous that it was ill tarry a minute long than 
necessary for refreshment. english road were bad enough at that period 
and for long after, but in america the way was simply the clear 
ground of the forest; the stump of the fell tree still remain in 
the direct line, form obstacles, which it require the most careful 
driving to avoid; and in the hollows, where the ground was swampy, the 
pulpy nature of it was obviate by log of wood laid across the boggy 
part. the deep green forest, tangle into heavy dark even thus 
early in the year, came within a few yard of the road all the way, 
though effort were regularly made by the inhabitant of the 
neighbouring settlement to keep a certain space clear on each side, 
for fear of the lurk indians, who might otherwise come upon them 
unawares. the cries of strange birds, the unwonted colour of some of 
them, all suggest to the imaginative or unaccustomed traveller the 
idea of war-whoops and paint deadly enemies. but at last they drew 
near to salem, which rival boston in size in those days, and boast 
the name of one or two streets, although to an english eye they look 
rather more like irregular built houses, cluster round the 
meeting-house, or rather one of the meeting-houses, for a second was in 
process of building. the whole place was surround with two circle of 
stockades; between the two were the garden and graze ground for 
those who dread their cattle stray into the woods, and the 
consequent danger of reclaim them. 

the lad who drove them flog his spent horse into a trot, as they 
went through salem to ralph hickson's house. it was evening, the 
leisure time for the inhabitants, and their children were at play 
before the houses. loi was struck by the beauty of one wee toddle 
child, and turn to look after it; it caught its little foot in a 
stump of wood, and fell with a cry that brought the mother out in 
affright. as she ran out, her eye caught lois's anxious gaze, although 
the noise of the heavy wheels drown the sound of her words of inquiry 
as to the nature of the hurt the child had received. nor had loi time 
to think long upon the matter, for the instant after, the horse was 
pull up at the door of a good, square, substantial wooden house, 
plastered over into a creamy white, perhaps as handsome a house as any 
in salem; and there she was told by the driver that her uncle, ralph 
hickson, lived. in the flurry of the moment she did not notice, but 
captain holdernesse did, that no one came out at the unwonted sound of 
wheels, to receive and welcome her. she was lift down by the old 
sailor, and led into a large room, almost like the hall of some english 
manor-house as to size. a tall, gaunt young man of three or four and 
twenty, sat on a bench by one of the windows, reading a great folio by 
the fade light of day. he did not rise when they came in, but look 
at them with surprise, no gleam of intelligence coming into his stern, 
dark face. there was no woman in the house-place. captain holdernesse 
pause a moment, and then said: 

'is this house ralph hickson's?' 

'it is,' said the young man, in a slow, deep voice. but he add no 
word further. 

'this is his niece, loi barclay,' said the captain, taking the girl's 
arm, and push her forwards. the young man look at her steady and 
grave for a minute; then rose, and careful marking the page in the 
folio which hitherto had lain open upon his knee, said, still in the 
same heavy, indifferent manner, 'i will call my mother, she will know.' 

he open a door which look into a warm bright kitchen, ruddy with 
the light of the fire over which three women were apparently engaged 
in cooking something, while a fourth, an old indian woman, of a 
greenish-brown colour, shrivel up and bent with apparent age, move 
backwards and forwards, evidently fetching the other the articles they 
required. 

'mother,' said the young man; and have arrest her attention, he 
pointed over his shoulder to the newly-arrived strangers, and return 
to the study of his book, from time to time, however, furtive 
examine loi from beneath his dark shaggy eyebrows. 

a tall, largely made woman, past middle life, came in from the kitchen, 
and stood reconnoitre the strangers. 

captain holdernesse spoke. 

'this is loi barclay, master ralph hickson's niece.' 

'i know nothing of her,' said the mistress of the house, in a deep 
voice, almost as masculine as her son's. 

'master hickson received his sister's letter, did he not? i sent it off 
myself by a lad name elia wellcome, who left boston for this place 
yester morning.' 

'ralph hickson has received no such letter. he lie bedridden in the 
chamber beyond. any letters for him must come through my hands; 
wherefore i can affirm with certainty that no such letter has been 
deliver here. his sister barclay, she that was henrietta hickson, and 
whose husband took the oath to charles stuart, and stuck by his living 
when all godly men left theirs----' 

lois, who had thought her heart was dead and cold a minute before at 
the ungracious reception she had met with, felt words come up into her 
mouth at the imply insult to her father, and spoke out, to her own 
and the captain's astonishment: 

'they might be godly men who left their church on that day of which 
you speak, madam; but they alone were not the godly men, and no one has 
a right to limit true godly for mere opinion's sake.' 

'well said, lass,' spoke out the captain, look round upon her with a 
kind of admire wonder, and pat her on the back. 

loi and her aunt gaze into each other's eyes unflinchingly, for a 
minute or two of silence; but the girl felt her colour coming and 
going, while the elder woman's never varied; and the eyes of the young 
maiden were filling fast with tears, while those of grate hickson kept 
on their stare, dry and unwavering. 

'mother!' said the young man, rising up with a quick motion than any 
one had yet use in this house, 'it is ill speak of such matter 
when my cousin come first among us. the lord may give her grace 
hereafter, but she has travelled from boston city to-day, and she and 
this seafaring man must need rest and food.' 

he did not attend to see the effect of his words, but sat down again, 
and seem to be absorb in his book in an instant. perhaps he knew 
that his word was law with his grim mother, for he had hardly cease 
speak before she had pointed to a wooden settle; and smooth the 
lines on her countenance, she said, 'what manasseh say is true. sit 
down here, while i bid faith and nattee get food ready; and meanwhile i 
will go tell my husband, that one who call herself his sister's child 
is come over to pay him a visit.' 

she went to the door leading into the kitchen, and gave some directions 
to the elder girl, whom loi now knew to be the daughter of the house. 
faith stood impassive, while her mother spoke, scarcely care to look 
at the newly-arrived strangers. she was like her brother manasseh in 
complexion, but had handsome features, and large, mysterious-looking 
eyes, as loi saw, when once she lift them up, and took in, as it 
were, the aspect of the sea-captain and her cousin with one swift 
searching look. about the stiff, tall, angular mother, and the scarce 
less pliant figure of the daughter, a girl of twelve years old, or 
thereabouts, play all manner of impish antics, unheeded by them, as 
if it were her accustomed habit to peep about, now under their arms, 
now at this side, now at that, making grimace all the while at loi 
and captain holdernesse, who sat facing the door, weary, and somewhat 
dishearten by their reception. the captain pull out tobacco, and 
began to chew it by way of consolation; but in a moment or two, his 
usual elasticity of spirit came to his rescue, and he said in a low 
voice to lois: 

'that scoundrel elias, i will give it him! if the letter had but been 
delivered, thou wouldst have had a different kind of welcome; but as 
soon as i have had some victuals, i will go out and find the lad, and 
bring back the letter, and that will make all right, my wench. nay, 
don't be downhearted, for i cannot stand women's tears. thou'rt just 
worn out with the shake and the want of food.' 

loi brush away her tears, and look round to try and divert her 
thought by fix them on present object, she caught her cousin 
manasseh's deep-set eyes furtive watch her. it was with no 
unfriend gaze, yet it made loi uncomfortable, particularly as he did 
not withdraw his looks after he must have seen that she observe him. 
she was glad when her aunt call her into an inner room to see her 
uncle, and she escape from the steady observance of her gloomy, silent 
cousin. 

ralph hickson was much old than his wife, and his illness made him 
look old still. he had never had the force of character that grace, 
his spouse, possessed, and age and sickness had now render him almost 
childish at times. but his nature was affectionate, and stretch out 
his tremble arms from where he lay bedridden, he gave loi an 
unhesitate welcome, never wait for the confirmation of the missing 
letter before he acknowledge her to be his niece. 

'oh! 'tis kind in thee to come all across the sea to make acquaintance 
with thine uncle; kind in sister barclay to spare thee!' 

loi had to tell him that there was no one living to miss her at home 
in england; that in fact she had no home in england, no father nor 
mother left upon earth; and that she had been bidden by her mother's 
last words to seek him out, and ask him for a home. her words came up, 
half choke from a heavy heart, and his dull wit could not take 
their meaning in without several repetitions; and then he cry like a 
child, rather at his own loss of a sister, whom he had not seen for 
more than twenty years, than at that of the orphan's standing before 
him, try hard not to cry, but to start brave in this new strange 
home. what most of all help loi in her self-restraint was her aunt's 
unsympathetic look. born and bred in new england, grace hickson had a 
kind of jealous dislike to her husband's english relations, which had 
increase since of late years his weaken mind yearn after them, and 
he forgot the good reason he had had for his self-exile, and moan 
over the decision which had led to it as the great mistake of his life. 
'come,' said she, 'it strike me that, in all this sorrow for the loss 
of one who die full of years, ye are forget in whose hand life 
and death are!' 

true words, but ill-spoken at that time. loi look up at her with a 
scarcely disguise indignation; which increase as she heard the 
contemptuous tone in which her aunt went on talk to ralph hickson, 
even while she was arrange his bed with a regard to his greater 
comfort. 

'one would think thou wert a godless man, by the moan thou art always 
making over spilt milk; and truth is, thou art but childish in thine 
old age. when we were wed, thou left all things to the lord; i would 
never have married thee else. nay, lass,' said she, catching the 
expression on lois's face, 'thou art never go to browbeat me with 
thine angry looks. i do my duty as i read it, and there is never a man 
in salem that dare speak a word to grace hickson about either her works 
or her faith. godly mr. cotton mather hath said, that even he might 
learn of me; and i would advise thee rather to humble thyself, and see 
if the lord may not convert thee from thy ways, since he has sent thee 
to dwell, as it were, in zion, where the precious dew falls daily on 
aaron's beard.' 

loi felt ashamed and sorry to find that her aunt had so truly 
interpret the momentary expression of her features; she blame 
herself a little for the feeling that had cause that expression, 
try to think how much her aunt might have been trouble with 
something before the unexpected irruption of the strangers, and again 
hope that the remembrance of this little misunderstanding would soon 
pass away. so she endeavour to reassure herself, and not to give way 
to her uncle's tender tremble pressure of her hand, as, at her aunt's 
bidding, she wish him good night, and return into the outer, or 
'keeping'-room, where all the family were now assembled, ready for the 
meal of flour cake and venison-steaks which nattee, the indian 
servant, was bring in from the kitchen. no one seem to have been 
speak to captain holdernesse while loi had been away. manasseh sat 
quiet and silent where he did, with the book open upon his knee, his 
eyes thoughtful fixed on vacancy, as if he saw a vision, or dream 
dreams. faith stood by the table, lazy direct nattee in her 
preparations; and prudence loll against the door-frame, between 
kitchen and keeping-room, play trick on the old indian woman as she 
pass backwards and forwards, till nattee appear to be in a strong 
state of express irritation, which he try in vain to repress, as 
whenever she show any sign of it, prudence only seem excited to 
greater mischief. when all was ready, manasseh lift his right hand, 
and 'asked a blessing,' as it was termed; but the grace became a long 
prayer for abstract spiritual blessings, for strength to combat satan, 
and to quench his fiery darts, and at length assumed, so loi thought, 
a purely personal character, as if the young man had forgotten the 
occasion, and even the people present, but was searching into the 
nature of the disease that beset his own sick soul, and spread them 
out before the lord. he was brought back by a pluck at the coat from 
prudence; he open his shut eyes, cast an angry glance at the child, 
who made a face at him for sole reply, and then he sat down, and they 
all fell to. grace hickson would have thought her hospitality sadly at 
fault, if she had allow captain holdernesse to go out in search of a 
bed. skin were spread for him on the floor of the keeping-room; a 
bible, and a square bottle of spirits were placed on the table, to 
supply his wants during the night; and in spite of all the care and 
troubles, temptations, or sin of the member of that household, they 
were all asleep before the town clock struck ten. 

in the morning, the captain's first care was to go out in search of the 
boy elias, and the missing letter. he met him bring it with an easy 
conscience, for, thought elias, a few hour soon or later will make 
no difference; to-night or the morrow morning will be all the same. but 
he was startle into a sense of wrong-doing by a sound box on the ear, 
from the very man who had charged him to deliver it speedily, and whom 
he believe to be at that very moment in boston city. 

the letter delivered, all possible proof being given that loi had a 
right to claim a home from her nearest relations, captain holdernesse 
thought it best to take leave. 

'thou'lt take to them, lass, maybe, when there is no one here to make 
thee think on the old country. nay, nay! parting is hard work at all 
times, and best get hard work done out of hand. keep up thine heart, my 
wench, and i'll come back and see thee next spring, if we are all 
spare till then; and who know what fine young miller mayn't come with 
me? don't go and get wed to a pray puritan, meanwhile. there, 
there--i'm off! god bless thee!' 

and loi was left alone in new england. 




chapter 2 


it was hard up-hill work for loi to win herself a place in this 
family. her aunt was a woman of narrow, strong affections. her love for 
her husband, if ever she had any, was burnt out and dead long ago. what 
she did for him she did from duty; but duty was not strong enough to 
restrain that little member the tongue; and lois's heart often bled at 
the continual flow of contemptuous reproof which grace constant 
address to her husband, even while she was sparing no pains or 
trouble to minister to his bodily ease and comfort. it was more as a 
relief to herself that she spoke in this way, than with any desire that 
her speech should affect him; and he was too deaden by illness to 
feel hurt by them; or, it may be, the constant repetition of her 
sarcasm had made him indifferent; at any rate, so that he had his food 
and his state of bodily warmth attend to, he very seldom seem to 
care much for anything else. even his first flow of affection towards 
loi was soon exhausted; he care for her because she arrange his 
pillow well and skilfully, and because she could prepare new and 
dainty kind of food for his sick appetite, but no long for her as 
his dead sister's child. still he did care for her, and loi was too 
glad of this little hoard of affection to examine how or why it was 
given. to him she could give pleasure, but apparently to no one else in 
that household. her aunt look askance at her for many reasons: the 
first coming of loi to salem was inopportune, the expression of 
disapprobation on her face on that evening still linger and rankle 
in grace's memory, early prejudices, and feelings, and prepossession 
of the english girl were all on the side of what would now be call 
church and state, what was then esteem in that country a 
superstitious observance of the directions of a popish rubric, and a 
servile regard for the family of an oppress and irreligious king. 
nor is it to be supposed that loi did not feel, and feel acutely, the 
want of sympathy that all those with whom she was now living manifest 
towards the old hereditary loyalty (religious as well as politics 
loyalty) in which she had been brought up. with her aunt and manasseh 
it was more than want of sympathy; it was positive, active antipathy to 
all the idea loi held most dear. the very allusion, however 
incidentally made, to the little old grey church at barford, where her 
father had preach so long,--the occasional reference to the trouble 
in which her own country had been distracted when she left,--and the 
adherence, in which she had been brought up, to the notion that the 
king could do no wrong, seem to irritate manasseh past endurance. he 
would get up from his reading, his constant employment when at home, 
and walk angry about the room after loi had said anything of this 
kind, mutter to himself; and once he had even stop before her, 
and in a passionate tone bade her not talk so like a fool. now this was 
very different to his mother's sarcastic, contemptuous way of treat 
all poor lois's little loyal speeches. grace would lead her on--at 
least she did at first, till experience made loi wiser--to express her 
thought on such subjects, till, just when the girl's heart was 
opening, her aunt would turn round upon her with some bitter sneer that 
rouse all the evil feelings in lois's disposition by its sting. now 
manasseh seemed, through all his anger, to be so really grieve by what 
he considered her error, that he went much near to convincing her 
that there might be two side to a question. only this was a view, that 
it appear like treachery to her dead father's memory to entertain. 

somehow, loi felt instinctive that manasseh was really friendly 
towards her. he was little in the house; there was farming, and some 
kind of mercantile business to be transact by him, as real head of 
the house; and as the season drew on, he went shoot and hunting in 
the surrounding forests, with a daring which cause his mother to warn 
and reprove him in private, although to the neighbour she boast 
largely of her son's courage and disregard of danger. loi did not 
often walk out for the mere sake of walking, there was generally some 
household errand to be transact when any of the women of the family 
went abroad; but once or twice she had caught glimpse of the dreary, 
dark wood, hem in the clear land on all sides,--the great wood 
with its perpetual movement of branch and bough, and its solemn wail, 
that came into the very street of salem when certain wind blew, 
bearing the sound of the pine-trees clear upon the ear that had 
leisure to listen. and from all accounts, this old forest, girdle 
round the settlement, was full of dread and mysterious beasts, and 
still more to be dread indians, steal in and out among the 
shadows, intent on bloody scheme against the christian people; 
panther-streaked, shaven indians, in league by their own confession, as 
well as by the popular belief, with evil powers. 

nattee, the old indian servant, would occasional make lois's blood 
run cold as she and faith and prudence listen to the wild story she 
told them of the wizard of her race. it was often in the kitchen, in 
the darken evening, while some cooking process was go on, that 
the old indian crone, sitting on her haunch by the bright red wood 
ember which sent up no flame, but a lurid light reverse the shadow 
of all the face around, told her weird story while they were 
await the rising of the dough, perchance, out of which the household 
bread had to be made. there ran through these story always a ghastly, 
unexpress suggestion of some human sacrifice being need to complete 
the success of any incantation to the evil one; and the poor old 
creature, herself believe and shudder as she narrate her tale in 
broken english, took a strange, unconscious pleasure in her power over 
her hearers--young girl of the oppress race, which had brought her 
down into a state little differ from slavery, and reduce her people 
to outcast on the hunting-grounds which had belong to her fathers. 
after such tales, it require no small effort on lois's part to go out, 
at her aunt's command, into the common pasture round the town, and 
bring the cattle home at night. who knew but what the double-headed 
snake might start up from each blackberry-bush--that wicked, cunning, 
accursed creature in the service of the indian wizards, that had such 
power over all those white maiden who met the eyes placed at either 
end of his long, sinuous, creep body, so that loathe him, loathe the 
indian race as they would, off they must go into the forest to seek but 
some indian man, and must beg to be taken into his wigwam, abjure 
faith and race for ever? or there were spells--so nattee said--hidden 
about the ground by the wizards, which change that person's nature who 
found them; so that, gentle and loving as they might have been before, 
thereafter they took no pleasure but in the cruel torment of others, 
and had a strange power given to them of cause such torment at their 
will. once nattee, speak low to lois, who was alone with her in the 
kitchen, whisper out her terrify belief that such a spell had 
prudence found; and when the indian show her arms to lois, all 
pinched and black and blue by the impish child, the english girl began 
to be afraid of her cousin as of one possessed. but it was not nattee 
alone, nor young imaginative girl alone, that believe in these 
stories. we can afford to smile at them now; but our english ancestor 
entertain superstition of much the same character at the same 
period, and with less excuse, as the circumstances surrounding them 
were better known, and consequently more explicable by common sense 
than the real mystery of the deep, untrodden forest of new england. 
the gravest divine not only believe story similar to that of the 
double-headed serpent, and other tale of witchcraft, but they made 
such narration the subject of preach and prayer; and as cowardice 
make us all cruel, men who were blameless in many of the relations of 
life, and even praiseworthy in some, became, from superstition, cruel 
persecute about this time, showing no mercy towards any one whom they 
believe to be in league with the evil one. 

faith was the person with whom the english girl was the most intimate 
associate in her uncle's house. the two were about the same age, and 
certain household employment were share between them. they took it in 
turn to call in the cows, to make up the butter which had been churn 
by hosea, a stiff old out-door servant, in whom grace hickson placed 
great confidence; and each lassie had her great spinning-wheel for 
wool, and her lesser for flax, before a month had elapse after lois's 
coming. faith was a grave, silent person, never merry, sometimes very 
sad, though loi was a long time in even guess why. she would try in 
her sweet, simple fashion to cheer her cousin up, when the latter was 
depressed, by telling her old story of english ways and life. 
occasionally, faith seem to care to listen, occasional she did not 
heed one word, but dream on. whether of the past or of the future, 
who could tell? 

stern old minister came in to pay their pastoral visits. on such 
occasions, grace hickson would put on clean apron and clean cap, and 
make them more welcome than she was ever seen to do no one else, 
bring out the best provisions of her store, and setting of all 
before them. also, the great bible was brought forth, and hosea and 
nattee summon from their work to listen while the minister read a 
chapter, and, as he read, expound it at considerable length. after 
this all knelt, while he, standing, lift up his right hand, and 
pray for all possible combinations of christian men, for all possible 
case of spiritual need; and lastly, taking the individual before him, 
he would put up a very personal supplicate for each, according to his 
notion of their wants. at first loi wonder at the aptitude of one or 
two prayer of this description to the outward circumstances of each 
case; but when she perceive that her aunt had usually a pretty long 
confidential conversation with the minister in the early part of his 
visit, she became aware that he received both his impression and his 
knowledge through the medium of 'that godly woman, grace hickson;' and 
i am afraid she paid less regard to the prayer 'for the maiden from 
another land, who hath brought the error of that land as a seed with 
her, even across the great ocean, and who is letting even now the 
little seed shoot up into an evil tree, in which all unclean creature 
may find shelter.' 

'i like the prayer of our church better,' said lois, one day to faith. 
'no clergyman in england can pray his own words, and therefore it is 
that he cannot judge of other so as to fit his prayer to what he 
esteem to be their case, as mr. tappau did this morning.' 

'i hate mr. tappau!' said faith, shortly, a passionate flash of light 
coming out of her dark, heavy eyes. 

'why so cousin? it seem to me as if he were a good man, although i 
like not his prayers.' 

faith only repeated her words, 'i hate him.' 

loi was sorry for this strong bad feeling; instinctive sorry, for 
she was loving herself, delight in being loved, and felt a jar run 
through her at every sign of want of love in others. but she did not 
know what to say, and was silent at the time. faith, too, went on 
turning her wheel with vehemence, but spoke never a word until her 
thread snapped, and then she pushed the wheel away hasty and left the 
room. 

then prudence crept soft up to lois's side. this strange child seem 
to be toss about by vary moods: to-day she was caress and 
communicative, to-morrow she might be deceitful, mocking, and so 
indifferent to the pain or sorrow of other that you could call her 
almost inhuman. 

'so thou dost not like pastor tappau's prayers?' she whispered. 

loi was sorry to have been overheard, but she neither would nor could 
take back her words. 

'i like them not so well as the prayer i use to hear at home.' 

'mother say thy home was with the ungodly. nay, don't look at me 
so--it was not i that said it. i'm none so fond of pray myself, nor 
of pastor tappau for that matter. but faith cannot abide him, and i 
know why. shall i tell thee, cousin lois?' 

'no! faith did not tell me, and she was the right person to give her 
own reasons.' 

'ask her where young mr. nolan is gone to, and thou wilt hear. i have 
seen faith cry by the hour together about mr. nolan.' 

'hush, child, hush!' said lois, for she heard faith's approach step, 
and fear lest she should overhear what they were saying. 

the truth was that, a year or two before, there had been a great 
struggle in salem village, a great division in the religious body, and 
pastor tappau had been the leader of the more violent, and, ultimately, 
the successful party. in consequence of this, the less popular 
minister, mr. nolan, had had to leave the place. and him faith hickson 
love with all the strength of her passionate heart, although he never 
was aware of the attachment he had excited, and her own family were too 
regardless of manifestation of mere feeling to ever observe the sign 
of any emotion on her part. but the old indian servant nattee saw and 
observe them all. she knew, as well as if she had been told the 
reason, why faith had lost all care about father or mother, brother and 
sister, about household work and daily occupation, nay, about the 
observance of religion as well. nattee read the meaning of the deep 
smoulder of faith's dislike to pastor tappau aright; the indian 
woman understood why the girl (whom alone of all the white people she 
loved) avoid the old minister,--would hide in the wood-stack soon 
than be call in to listen to his exhortation and prayers. with 
savage, untutored people, it is not 'love me, love my dog,' they are 
often jealous of the creature beloved; but it is, 'whom thou hatest i 
will hate;' and nattee's feeling towards pastor tappau was even an 
exaggeration of the mute unspoken hatred of faith. 

for a long time, the cause of her cousin's dislike and avoidance of the 
minister was a mystery to lois; but the name of nolan remain in her 
memory whether she would or no, and it was more from girlish interest 
in a suspect love affair, than from any indifferent and heartless 
curiosity, that she could not help piece together little speech and 
actions, with faith's interest in the absent banish minister, for an 
explanatory clue, till not a doubt remain in her mind. and this 
without any further communication with prudence, for loi decline 
hearing any more on the subject from her, and so gave deep offence. 

faith grew sad and dull as the autumn drew on. she lost her 
appetite, her brown complexion became sallow and colourless, her dark 
eyes look hollow and wild. the first of november was near at hand. 
lois, in her instinctive, well-intentioned effort to bring some life 
and cheerful into the monotonous household, had been telling faith 
of many english customs, silly enough, no doubt, and which scarcely 
light up a flicker of interest in the america girl's mind. the 
cousin were lie awake in their bed in the great unplastered room, 
which was in part store-room, in part bedroom. loi was full of 
sympathy for faith that night. for long she had listen to her 
cousin's heavy, irrepressible sighs, in silence. faith sigh because 
her grief was of too old a date for violent emotion or crying. loi 
listen without speak in the dark, quiet night hours, for a long, 
long time. she kept quite still, because she thought such vent for 
sorrow might relieve her cousin's weary heart. but when at length, 
instead of lie motionless, faith seem to be grow restless even 
to convulsive motions of her limbs, loi began to speak, to talk about 
england, and the dear old ways at home, without exciting much attention 
on faith's part, until at length she fell upon the subject of 
hallow-e'en, and told about customs then and long afterwards practised 
in england, and that have scarcely yet die out in scotland. as she 
told of trick she had often played, of the apple eaten facing a 
mirror, of the dripping sheet, of the basin of water, of the nuts 
burning side by side, and many other such innocent ways of divination, 
by which laughing, tremble english maiden sought to see the form of 
their future husbands, if husband they were to have, then faith 
listen breathlessly, ask short, eager questions, as if some ray of 
hope had enter into her gloomy heart. loi went on speaking, telling 
her of all the story that would confirm the truth of the second sight 
vouchsafe to all seek in the accustomed methods, half believing, 
half incredulous herself, but desiring, above all things, to cheer up 
poor faith. 

suddenly, prudence rose up from her truckle-bed in the dim corner of 
the room. they had not thought that she was awake, but she had been 
listen long. 

'cousin loi may go out and meet satan by the brook-side if she will, 
but if thou goest, faith, i will tell mother--ay, and i will tell 
pastor tappau, too. hold thy stories, cousin lois, i am afeard of my 
very life. i would rather never be wed at all, than feel the touch of 
the creature that would take the apple out of my hand, as i held it 
over my left shoulder.' the excited girl gave a loud scream of terror 
at the image her fancy had conjure up. faith and loi sprang out 
towards her, flying across the moonlit room in their white nightgowns. 
at the same instant, summon by the same cry, grace hickson came to 
her child. 

'hush! hush!' said faith, authoritatively. 

'what is it, my wench?' ask grace. while lois, feeling as if she had 
done all the mischief, kept silence. 

'take her away, take her away!' scream prudence. 'look over her 
shoulder--her left shoulder--the evil one is there now, i see him 
stretch over for the half-bitten apple.' 

'what is this she says?' said grace, austerely. 

'she is dreaming,' said faith; 'prudence, hold thy tongue.' and she 
pinched the child severely, while loi more tender try to soothe 
the alarm she felt that she had conjure up. 

'be quiet, prudence,' said she, 'and go to sleep. i will stay by thee 
till thou hast gone off into slumber.' 

'no, go! go away,' sob prudence, who was really terrify at first, 
but was now assume more alarm: than she felt, if from the pleasure 
she received at perceive herself the centre of attention. 'faith 
shall stay by me, not you, wicked english witch!' 

so faith sat by her sister; and grace, displease and perplexed, 
withdrew to her own bed, purpose to inquire more into the matter in 
the morning. loi only hope it might all be forgotten by that time, 
and resolve never to talk again of such things. but an event happen 
in the remain hour of the night to change the current of affairs. 
while grace had been absent from her room, her husband had had another 
paralytic stroke: whether he, too, had been alarm by that eldritch 
scream no one could ever know. by the faint light of the rush candle 
burning at the bedside, his wife perceive that a great change had 
taken place in his aspect on her return: the irregular breathing came 
almost like snorts--the end was drawing near. the family were roused, 
and all help given that either the doctor or experience could suggest. 
but before the late november morning light, all was end for ralph 
hickson. 

the whole of the ensue day, they sat or move in darken rooms, and 
spoke few words, and those below their breath. manasseh kept at home, 
regret his father, no doubt, but showing little emotion. faith was 
the child that bewail her loss most grievously; she had a warm heart, 
hidden away somewhere under her moody exterior, and her father had 
shown her far more passive kindness than ever her mother had done, for 
grace made distinct favourite of manasseh, her only son, and prudence, 
her youngest child. loi was about as unhappy as any of them, for she 
had felt strong drawn towards her uncle as her kindest friend, and 
the sense of his loss renew the old sorrow she had experienced at her 
own parents' death. but she had no time and no place to cry in. on her 
devolve many of the cares, which it would have seem indecorous in 
the near relative to interest themselves in enough to take an active 
part: the change require in their dress, the household preparation 
for the sad feast of the funeral--lois had to arrange all under her 
aunt's stern direction. 

but a day or two afterwards--the last day before the funeral--she went 
into the yard to fetch in some fagot for the oven; it was a solemn, 
beautiful, starlit evening, and some sudden sense of desolate in the 
midst of the vast universe thus reveal touched lois's heart, and she 
sat down behind the woodstack, and cry very plentiful tears. 

she was startle by manasseh, who sudden turn the corner of the 
stack, and stood before her. 

'lois crying!' 

'only a little,' she said, rising up, and gathering her bundle of 
fagots, for she dread being question by her grim, impassive cousin. 
to her surprise, he laid his hand on her arm, and said: 

'stop one minute. why art thou crying, cousin?' 

'i don't know,' she said, just like a child question in like manner; 
and she was again on the point of weeping. 

'my father was very kind to thee, lois; i do not wonder that thou 
grievest after him. but the lord who taketh away can restore tenfold. i 
will be as kind as my father--yea, kinder. this is not a time to talk 
of marriage and give in marriage. but after we have bury our dead, 
i wish to speak to thee.' 

loi did not cry now, but she shrank with affright. what did her cousin 
mean? she would far rather that he had been angry with her for 
unreasonable grieving, for folly. 

she avoid him carefully--as careful as she could, without seeming 
to dread him--for the next few days. sometimes she thought it must have 
been a bad dream; for if there had been no english lover in the case, 
no other man in the whole world, she could never have thought of 
manasseh as her husband; indeed, till now, there had been nothing in 
his words or action to suggest such an idea. now it had been 
suggested, there was no telling how much she loathe him. he might be 
good, and pious--he doubtless was--but his dark fixed eyes, moving so 
slow and heavily, his lank black hair, his grey coarse skin, all made 
her dislike him now--all his personal ugly and ungainly struck 
on her senses with a jar, since those few words spoken behind the 
haystack. 

she knew that soon or later the time must come for further discussion 
of this subject; but, like a coward, she try to put it off, by 
clinging to her aunt's apron-string, for she was sure that grace 
hickson had far different view for her only son. as, indeed, she had, 
for she was an ambitious, as well as a religious woman; and by an early 
purchase of land in salem village, the hickson had become wealthy
