people, without any great exertion of their own; partly, also, by the 
silent process of accumulation, for they had never care to change 
their manner of living from the time when it had been suitable to a far 
small income than that which they at present enjoyed. so much for 
worldly circumstances. as for their worldly character, it stood as 
high. no one could say a word against any of their habit or actions. 
the righteous and godly were patent to every one's eyes. so 
grace hickson thought herself entitle to pick and choose among the 
maidens, before she should meet with one fitted to be manasseh's wife. 
none in salem came up to her imaginary standard. she had it in her mind 
even at this very time--so soon after her husband's death--to go to 
boston, and take counsel with the leading minister there, with worthy 
mr. cotton mather at their head, and see if they could tell her of a 
well-favoured and godly young maiden in their congregation worthy of 
being the wife of her son. but, besides good looks and godliness, the 
wench must have good birth, and good wealth, or grace hickson would 
have put her contemptuous on one side. when once this paragon was 
found, the minister had approved, grace anticipate no difficulty on 
her son's part. so loi was right in feeling that her aunt would 
dislike any speech of marriage between manasseh and herself. 

but the girl was brought to bay one day in this wise. manasseh had 
ridden forth on some business, which every one said would occupy him 
the whole day; but, meeting the man with whom he had to transact his 
affairs, he return early than any one expected. he miss loi from 
the keeping-room where his sister were spinning, almost immediately. 
his mother sat by at her knitting--he could see nattee in the kitchen 
through the open door. he was too reserved to ask where loi was, but 
he quiet sought till he found her--in the great loft, already pile 
with winter stores of fruit and vegetables. her aunt had sent her there 
to examine the apple one by one, and pick out such as were unsound, 
for immediate use. she was stoop down, and intent upon this work, 
and was hardly aware of his approach, until she lift up her head and 
saw him standing close before her. she drop the apple she was 
holding, went a little pale than her wont, and face him in silence. 

'lois,' he said, 'thou rememberest the words that i spoke while we yet 
mourn over my father. i think that i am call to marriage now, as 
the head of this household. and i have seen no maiden so pleasant in my 
sight as thou art, lois!' he try to take her hand. but she put it 
behind her with a childish shake of her head, and, half-crying, said: 

'please, cousin manasseh, do not say this to me. i dare say you ought 
to be married, being the head of the household now; but i don't want to 
be married. i would rather not.' 

'that is well spoken,' reply he, frown a little, nevertheless. 'i 
should not like to take to wife an over-forward maiden, ready to jump 
at wedlock. besides, the congregation might talk, if we were to be 
married too soon after my father's death. we have, perchance, said 
enough, even now. but i wish thee to have thy mind set at ease as to 
thy future well-doing. thou wilt have leisure to think of it, and to 
bring thy mind more fully round to it.' again he held out his hand. 
this time she took hold of it with a free, frank gesture. 

'i owe you somewhat for your kindness to me ever since i came, cousin 
manasseh; and i have no way of pay you but by telling you truly i 
can love you as a dear friend, if you will let me, but never as a 
wife.' 

he flung her hand away, but did not take his eyes off her face, though 
his glance was lower and gloomy. he mutter something which she did 
not quite hear, and so she went on brave although she kept tremble 
a little, and had much ado to keep from crying. 

'please let me tell you all. there was a young man in barford--nay, 
manasseh, i cannot speak if you are so angry; it is hard work to tell 
you any how--he said that he want to marry me; but i was poor, and 
his father would have none of it, and i do not want to marry any one; 
but if i did, it would be--' her voice dropped, and her blush told 
the rest. manasseh stood look at her with sullen, hollow eyes, that 
had a glittering touch of wilderness in them, and then he said: 

'it is borne in upon me--verily i see it as in a vision--that thou must 
be my spouse, and no other man's. thou canst not escape what is 
foredoomed. month ago, when i set myself to read the old godly books 
in which my soul use to delight until thy coming, i saw no letters of 
printers' ink marked on the page, but i saw a gold and ruddy type of 
some unknown language, the meaning whereof was whisper into my soul; 
it was, "marry lois! marry lois!" and when my father died, i knew it 
was the beginning of the end. it is the lord's will, lois, and thou 
canst not escape from it.' and again he would have taken her hand and 
drawn her towards him. but this time she elude him with ready 
movement. 

'i do not acknowledge it be the lord's will, manasseh,' said she. 'it 
is not "borne in upon me," as you puritan call it, that i am to be 
your wife. i am none so set upon wedlock as to take you, even though 
there be no other chance for me. for i do not care for you as i ought 
to care for my husband. but i could have care for you very much as a 
cousin--as a kind cousin.' 

she stop speaking; she could not choose the right words with which 
to speak to him of her gratitude and friendliness, which yet could 
never be any feeling near and dearer, no more than two parallel lines 
can ever meet. 

but he was so convinced, by what he considered the spirit of prophecy, 
that loi was to be his wife, that he felt rather more indignant at 
what he considered to be her resistance to the preordain decree, than 
really anxious as to the result. again he try to convince her that 
neither he nor she had any choice in the matter, by saying: 

'the voice said unto me "marry lois," and i said, "i will, lord."' 

'but,' loi replied, 'the voice, as you call it, has never spoken such 
a word to me.' 

'lois,' he answered, solemnly, 'it will speak. and then wilt thou obey, 
even as samuel did?' 

'no, indeed i cannot!' she answered, briskly. 'i may take a dream to be 
truth, and hear my own fancies, if i think about them too long. but i 
cannot marry any one from obedience.' 

'lois, lois, thou art as yet unregenerate; but i have seen thee in a 
vision as one of the elect, robe in white. as yet thy faith is too 
weak for thee to obey meekly, but it shall not always be so. i will 
pray that thou mayest see thy preordain course. meanwhile, i will 
smooth away all worldly obstacles.' 

'cousin manasseh! cousin manasseh!' cry loi after him, as he was 
leave the room, 'come back. i cannot put it in strong enough words. 
manasseh, there is no power in heaven or earth that can make me love 
thee enough to marry thee, or to wed thee without such love. and this i 
say solemnly, because it is better that this should end at once.' 

for a moment he was staggered; then he lift up his hands, and said, 

'god forgive thee thy blasphemy! remember hazael, who said, "is thy 
servant a dog, that he should do this great thing?" and went straight 
and did it, because his evil course were fixed and appoint for him 
from before the foundation of the world. and shall not thy path be 
laid out among the godly as it hath been foretold to me?' 

he went away; and for a minute or two loi felt as if his words must 
come true, and that, struggle as she would, hate her doom as she would, 
she must become his wife; and, under the circumstances, many a girl 
would have succumb to her apparent fate. isolated from all previous 
connections, hearing no word from england, living in the heavy, 
monotonous routine of a family with one man for head, and this man 
esteem a hero by most of those around him, simply because he was the 
only man in the family,--these fact alone would have form strong 
presumption that most girl would have yield to the offer of such a 
one. but, besides this, there was much to tell upon the imagination in 
those days, in that place, and time. it was prevalent believe that 
there were manifestation of spiritual influence--of the direct 
influence both of good and bad spirits--constantly to be perceive in 
the course of men's lives. lot were drawn, as guidance from the lord; 
the bible was opened, and the leaves allow to fall apart, and the 
first text the eye fell upon was supposed to be appoint from above a 
direction. sound were heard that could not be account for; they were 
made by the evil spirits not yet banish from the desert place of 
which they had so long held possession. sights, inexplicable and 
mysterious, were dim seen--satan, in some shape, seek whom he 
might devour. and at the beginning of the long winter season, such 
whisper tales, such old temptation and hauntings, and devilish 
terrors, were supposed to be peculiarly rife. salem was, as it were, 
snow up, and left to prey upon itself. the long, dark evenings, the 
dimly-lighted rooms, the creak passages, where heterogeneous 
articles were pile away out of reach of the keen-piercing frost, and 
where occasionally, in the dead of night, a sound was heard, as of some 
heavy fall body, when, next morning, everything appear to be in 
its right place--so accustomed are we to measure noise by comparison 
with themselves, and not with the absolute still of the 
night-season--the white mist, coming near and near to the window 
every evening in strange shapes, like phantoms,--all these, and many 
other circumstances, such as the distant fall of mighty tree in the 
mysterious forest girdle them round, the faint whoop and cry of some 
indian seek his camp, and unwitting near to the white men's 
settlement than either he or they would have like could they have 
chosen, the hungry yell of the wild beast approach the 
cattle-pens,--these were the things which made that winter life in 
salem, in the memorable time of 1691-2, seem strange, and haunted, and 
terrific to many: peculiarly weird and awful to the english girl in her 
first year's sojourn in america. 

and now imagine loi work upon perpetual by manasseh's conviction 
that it was decree that she should be his wife, and you will see that 
she was not without courage and spirit to resist as she did, steadily, 
firmly, and yet sweetly. take one instance out of many, when her nerves 
were subject to a shock, slight in relation it is true, but then 
remember that she had been all day, and for many days, shut up within 
doors, in a dull light, that at mid-day was almost dark with a 
long-continued snow-storm. evening was coming on, and the wood fire was 
more cheerful than any of the human being surrounding it; the 
monotonous whirr of the small spinning-wheels had been go on all 
day, and the store of flax down stairs was nearly exhausted, when grace 
hickson bade loi fetch down some more from the store-room, before the 
light so entire wane away that it could not be found without a 
candle, and a candle it would be dangerous to carry into that apartment 
full of combustible materials, especially at this time of hard frost, 
when every drop of water was lock up and bound in icy hardness. so 
loi went, half-shrinking from the long passage that led to the stairs 
leading up into the storeroom, for it was in this passage that the 
strange night sound were heard, which every one had begun to notice, 
and speak about in lower tones. she sang, however, as she went, 'to 
keep her courage up'--sang, however, in a subdued voice, the evening 
hymn she had so often sung in barford church: 

'glory to thee, my god, this night--' 

and so it was, i suppose, that she never heard the breathing or motion 
of any creature near her till, just as she was load herself with 
flax to carry down, she heard some one--it was manasseh--say close to 
her ear: 

'has the voice spoken yet? speak, lois! has the voice spoken yet to 
thee--that speaketh to me day and night, "marry lois?"' 

she start and turn a little sick, but spoke almost directly in a 
brave, clear manner: 

'no! cousin manasseh. and it never will.' 

'then i must wait yet longer,' he replied, hoarsely, as if to himself. 
'but all submission--all submission.' 

at last a break came upon the monotony of the long, dark winter. the 
parishioner once more raise the discussion whether--the parish 
extend as it did--it was not absolutely necessary for pastor tappau 
to have help. this question had been moot once before; and then 
pastor tappau had acquiesce in the necessity, and all had gone on 
smooth for some month after the appointment of his assistant, until 
a feeling had sprung up on the part of the elder minister, which might 
have been call jealousy of the younger, if so godly a man as pastor 
tappau could have been supposed to entertain so evil a passion. however 
that might be, two party were speedy formed, the younger and more 
ardent being in favour of mr. nolan, the elder and more 
persistent--and, at the time, the more numerous--clinging to the old 
grey-headed, dogmatic mr. tappau, who had married them, baptize their 
children, and was to them literally as a 'pillar of the church.' so mr. 
nolan left salem, carry away with him, possibly, more heart than 
that of faith hickson's; but certainly she had never been the same 
creature since. 

but now--christmas, 1691--one or two of the old member of the 
congregation being dead, and some who were younger men have come to 
settle in salem--mr. tappau being also older, and, some charitable 
supposed, wiser--a fresh effort had been made, and mr. nolan was 
return to labour in ground apparently smooth over. loi had taken 
a keen interest in all the proceedings for faith's sake,--far more than 
the latter did for herself, any spectator would have said. faith's 
wheel never went fast or slower, her thread never broke, her colour 
never came, her eyes were never uplift with sudden interest, all the 
time these discussion respecting mr. nolan's return were go on. but 
lois, after the hint given by prudence, had found a clue to many a sigh 
and look of despairing sorrow, even without the help of nattee's 
improvise songs, in which, under strange allegories, the helpless love 
of her favourite was told to ear heedless of all meaning, except those 
of the tender-hearted and sympathetic lois. occasionally, she heard a 
strange chant of the old indian woman's--half in her own language, half 
in broken english--droned over some simmer pipkin, from which the 
smell was, to say the least, unearthly. once, on perceive this odour 
in the keeping-room, grace hickson sudden exclaimed: 

'nattee is at her heathen ways again; we shall have some mischief 
unless she is stayed.' 

but faith, moving quick than ordinary, said something about putt a 
stop to it, and so forestall her mother's evident intention of go 
into the kitchen. faith shut the door between the two rooms, and 
enter upon some remonstrance with nattee; but no one could hear the 
words used. faith and nattee seem more bound together by love and 
common interest, than any other two among the self-contained 
individual comprise this household. loi sometimes felt as if her 
presence as a third interrupt some confidential talk between her 
cousin and the old servant. and yet she was fond of faith, and could 
almost think that faith like her more than she did either mother, 
brother, or sister; for the first two were indifferent as to any 
unspoken feelings, while prudence delight in discover them only to 
make an amusement to herself out of them. 

one day loi was sitting by herself at her sewing table, while faith 
and nattee were holding one of the secret conclave from which loi 
felt herself to be tacit excluded, when the outer door opened, and a 
tall, pale young man, in the strict professional habit of a minister, 
entered. loi sprang up with a smile and a look of welcome for faith's 
sake, for this must be the mr. nolan whose name had been on the tongue 
of every one for days, and who was, as loi knew, expect to arrive 
the day before. 

he seem half surprise at the glad alacrity with which he was 
received by this stranger: possibly he had not heard of the english 
girl, who was an inmate in the house where formerly he had seen only 
grave, solemn, rigid, or heavy faces, and had been received with a 
stiff form of welcome, very different from the blushing, smiling, 
dimple looks that innocent met him with the greeting almost of an 
old acquaintance. loi have placed a chair for him, hasten out to 
call faith, never doubt but that the feeling which her cousin 
entertain for the young pastor was mutual, although it might be 
unrecognised in its full depth by either. 

'faith!' said she, bright and breathless. 'guess--no,' check herself 
to an assume unconscious of any particular importance likely to be 
affix to her words, 'mr. nolan, the new pastor, is in the 
keeping-room. he has ask for my aunt and manasseh. my aunt is gone to 
the prayer meeting at pastor tappau's, and manasseh is away.' loi went 
on speak to give faith time, for the girl had become deadly white at 
the intelligence, while, at the same time, her eyes met the keen, 
cunning eyes of the old indian with a peculiar look of half-wondering 
awe, while nattee's looks express triumphant satisfaction. 

'go,' said lois, smooth faith's hair, and kiss the white, cold 
cheek, 'or he will wonder why no one come to see him, and perhaps 
think he is not welcome.' faith went without another word into the 
keeping-room, and shut the door of communication. nattee and loi were 
left together. loi felt as happy as if some piece of good fortune had 
befallen herself. for the time, her grow dread of manasseh's wild, 
ominous persistence in his suit, her aunt's coldness, her own 
loneliness, were all forgotten, and she could almost have dance with 
joy. nattee laugh aloud, and talk and chuckle to herself: 'old 
indian woman great mystery. old indian woman sent hither and thither; 
go where she is told, where she hear with her ears. but old indian 
woman'--and here she drew herself up, and the expression of her face 
quite changed--'know how to call, and then white man must come; and old 
indian have spoken never a word, and white man have hear nothing with 
his ears.' so, the old crone muttered. 

all this time, things were go on very different in the 
keeping-room to what loi imagined. faith sat still even than usual; 
her eyes downcast, her words few. a quick observer might have notice a 
certain tremulous about her hands, and an occasional twitch 
throughout all her frame. but pastor nolan was not a keen observer upon 
this occasion; he was absorb with his own little wonders and 
perplexities. his wonder was that of a carnal man--who that pretty 
stranger might be, who had seemed, on his first coming, so glad to see 
him, but had vanish instantly, apparently not to reappear. and, 
indeed, i am not sure if his perplexity was not that of a carnal man 
rather than that of a godly minister, for this was his dilemma. it was 
the custom of salem (as we have already seen) for the minister, on 
enter a household for the visit which, among other people and in 
other times, would have been term a 'morning call,' to put up a 
prayer for the eternal welfare of the family under whose roof-tree he 
was. now this prayer was expect to be adapt to the individual 
character, joys, sorrows, wants, and failing of every member present; 
and here was he, a young pastor, alone with a young woman, and he 
thought--vain thoughts, perhaps, but still very natural--that the 
imply guess at her character, involved in the minute supplicate 
above described, would be very awkward in a tête-à-tête prayer; so, 
whether it was his wonder or his perplexity, i do not know, but he did 
not contribute much to the conversation for some time, and at last, by 
a sudden burst of courage and impromptu hit, he cut the gordian knot by 
making the usual proposal for prayer, and add to it a request that 
the household might be summoned. in came lois, quiet and decorous; in 
came nattee, all one impassive, stiff piece of wood,--no look of 
intelligence or trace of giggle near her countenance. solemn 
recall each wandering thought, pastor nolan knelt in the midst of 
these three to pray. he was a good and truly religious man, whose name 
here is the only thing disguised, and play his part brave in the 
awful trial to which he was afterwards subjected; and if at the time, 
before he went through his fiery persecutions, the human fancies which 
beset all young heart came across his, we at this day know that these 
fancies are no sin. but now he pray in earnest, pray so heartily for 
himself, with such a sense of his own spiritual need and spiritual 
failings, that each one of his hearer feel as if a prayer and a 
supplicate had gone up for each of them. even nattee mutter the few 
words she knew of the lord's prayer; gibberish though the disjointed 
noun and verb might be, the poor creature said them because she was 
stir to unwonted reverence. as for lois, she rose up comfort and 
strengthened, as no special prayer of pastor tappau had ever made her 
feel. but faith was sobbing, sob aloud, almost hysterically, and 
made no effort to rise, but lay on her outstretched arms spread out 
upon the settle. loi and pastor nolan look at each other for an 
instant. then loi said: 

'sir, you must go. my cousin has not been strong for some time, and 
doubtless she needs more quiet than she has had to-day.' 

pastor nolan bowed, and left the house; but in a moment he returned. 
half opening the door, but without entering, he said: 

'i come back to ask, if perchance i may call this evening to inquire 
how young mistress hickson find herself?' 

but faith did not hear this; she was sob loud than ever. 

'why did you send him away, lois? i should have been better directly, 
and it is so long since i have seen him.' 

she had her face hidden as she utter these words, and loi could not 
hear them distinctly. she bent her head down by her cousin's on the 
settle, meaning to ask her to repeat what she had said. but in the 
irritation of the moment, and prompt possibly by some incipient 
jealousy, faith pushed loi away so violent that the latter was hurt 
against the hard, sharp corner of the wooden settle. tear came into 
her eyes; not so much because her cheek was bruised, as because of the 
surprise pain she felt at this repulse from the cousin towards whom 
she was feeling so warm and kindly. just for the moment, loi was as 
angry as any child could have been; but some of the words of pastor 
nolan's prayer yet rang in her ears, and she thought it would be a 
shame if she did not let them sink into her heart. she dare not, 
however, stoop again to caress faith, but stood quiet by her, 
sorrowful waiting, until a step at the outer door cause faith to 
rise quickly, and rush into the kitchen, leave loi to bear the brunt 
of the new-comer. it was manasseh, return from hunting. he had been 
two days away, in company with other young men belong to salem. it 
was almost the only occupation which could draw him out of his secluded 
habits. he stop sudden at the door on seeing lois, and alone, for 
she had avoid him of late in every possible way. 

'where is my mother?' 

'at a prayer meeting at pastor tappau's. she has taken prudence. faith 
has left the room this minute. i will call her.' and loi was go 
towards the kitchen, when he placed himself between her and the door. 

'lois,' said he, 'the time is go by, and i cannot wait much longer. 
the vision come thick upon me, and my sight grow clear and clearer. 
only this last night, camp out in the woods, i saw in my soul, 
between sleep and waking, the spirit come and offer thee two lots, 
and the colour of the one was white, like a bride's, and the other was 
black and red, which is, being interpreted, a violent death. and when 
thou didst choose the latter the spirit said unto me, 'come!' and i 
came, and did as i was bidden. i put it on thee with mine own hands, as 
it is preordained, if thou wilt not hearken unto the voice and be my 
wife. and when the black and red dress fell to the ground, thou wert 
even as a corpse three days old. now, be advised, lois, in time. lois, 
my cousin, i have seen it in a vision, and my soul cleaveth unto 
thee--i would fain spare thee.' 

he was really in earnest--in passionate earnest; whatever his visions, 
as he call them, might be, he believe in them, and this belief gave 
something of unselfish to his love for lois. this she felt at this 
moment, if she had never done so before, and it seem like a contrast 
to the repulse she had just met with from his sister. he had drawn near 
her, and now he took hold of her hand, repeating in his wild, pathetic, 
dreamy way: 

'and the voice said unto me, "marry lois!"' and loi was more inclined 
to soothe and reason with him than she had ever been before, since the 
first time of his speak to her on the subject,--when grace 
hickson--and prudence enter the room from the passage. they had 
return from the prayer meeting by the back way, which had prevent 
the sound of their approach from being heard. 

but manasseh did not stir or look round; he kept his eyes fixed on 
lois, as if to note the effect of his words. grace came hasty 
forwards, and lift up her strong right arm, smote their join hand 
in twain, in spite of the fervour of manasseh's grasp. 

'what means this?' said she, address herself more to loi than to 
her son, anger flash out of her deep-set eyes. 

loi wait for manasseh to speak. he seemed, but a few minutes before, 
to be more gentle and less threaten than he had been of late on this 
subject, and she did not wish to irritate him. but he did not speak, 
and her aunt stood angry wait for an answer. 

'at any rate,' thought lois, 'it will put an end to the thought in his 
mind when my aunt speak out about it.' 

'my cousin seek me in marriage,' said lois. 

'thee!' and grace struck out in the direction of her niece with a 
gesture of supreme contempt. but now manasseh spoke forth: 

'yea! it is preordained. the voice has said it, and the spirit has 
brought her to me as my bride.' 

'spirit! an evil spirit then. a good spirit would have chosen out for 
thee a godly maiden of thine own people, and not a prelatist and a 
stranger like this girl. a pretty return, mistress lois, for all our 
kindness.' 

'indeed, aunt hickson, i have done all i could--cousin manasseh know 
it--to show him i can be none of his. i have told him,' said she, 
blushing, but determined to say the whole out at once, 'that i am all 
but troth-plight to a young man of our own village at home; and, even 
putt all that on one side, i wish not for marriage at present.' 

'wish rather for conversion and regeneration. marriage is an unseemly 
word in the mouth of a maiden. as for manasseh, i will take reason with 
him in private; and, meanwhile, if thou hast spoken truly, throw not 
thyself in his path, as i have notice thou hast done but too often of 
late.' 

lois's heart burnt within her at this unjust accusation, for she knew 
how much she had dread and avoid her cousin, and she almost look 
to him to give evidence that her aunt's last words were not true. but, 
instead, he recur to his one fixed idea, and said: 

'mother, listen! if i wed not lois, both she and i die within the year. 
i care not for life; before this, as you know, i have sought for death' 
(grace shuddered, and was for a moment subdued by some recollection of 
past horror); 'but if loi were my wife i should live, and she would be 
spare from what is the other lot. that whole vision grow clear to 
me day by day. yet, when i try to know whether i am one of the elect, 
all is dark. the mystery of free-will and fore-knowledge is a mystery 
of satan's devising, not of god's.' 

'alas, my son! satan is abroad among the brethren even now; but let the 
old vex topic rest. soon than fret thyself again, thou shalt have 
loi to be thy wife, though my heart was set far different for thee.' 

'no, manasseh,' said lois. 'i love you well as a cousin, but wife of 
yours i can never be. aunt hickson, it is not well to delude him so. i 
say, if ever i marry man, i am troth-plight to one in england.' 

'tush, child! i am your guardian in my dead husband's place. thou 
thinkest thyself so great a prize that i would clutch at thee whether 
or no, i doubt not. i value thee not, save as a medicine for manasseh, 
if his mind get disturbed again, as i have noted sign of late.' 

this, then, was the secret explanation of much that had alarm her in 
her cousin's manner: and if loi had been a physician of modern times, 
she might have trace somewhat of the same temperament in his sister 
as well--in prudence's lack of natural feeling and impish delight in 
mischief, in faith's vehemence of unrequited love. but as yet loi did 
not know, any more than faith, that the attachment of the latter to mr. 
nolan was not merely unreturned, but even unperceived, by the young 
minister. 

he came, it is true--came often to the house, sat long with the family, 
and watch them narrowly, but took no especial notice of faith. loi 
perceive this, and grieve over it; nattee perceive it, and was 
indignant at it, long before faith slow acknowledge it to herself, 
and went to nattee the indian woman, rather than to loi her cousin, 
for sympathy and counsel. 

'he care not for me,' said faith. 'he care more for lois's little 
finger than for my whole body,' the girl moan out in the bitter pain 
of jealousy. 

'hush thee, hush thee, prairie bird! how can he build a nest, when the 
old bird has got all the moss and the feathers? wait till the indian 
has found means to send the old bird flying far away.' this was the 
mysterious comfort nattee gave. 

grace hickson took some kind of charge over manasseh that relieved loi 
of much of her distress at his strange behaviour. yet at times he 
escape from his mother's watchfulness, and in such opportunity he 
would always seek lois, entreat her, as of old, to marry 
him--sometimes pleading his love for her, often speak wildly of 
his vision and the voice which he heard foretell a terrible 
futurity. 

we have now to do with event which were taking place in salem, beyond 
the narrow circle of the hickson family; but as they only concern us in 
as far as they bore down in their consequence on the future of those 
who form part of it, i shall go over the narrative very briefly. the 
town of salem had lost by death, within a very short time preceding the 
commencement of my story, nearly all its venerable men and leading 
citizens--men of ripe wisdom and sound counsel. the people had hardly 
yet recover from the shock of their loss, as one by one the 
patriarch of the primitive little community had rapid follow each 
other to the grave. they had been beloved as fathers, and look up to 
as judge in the land. the first bad effect of their loss was seen in 
the heated dissension which sprang up between pastor tappau and the 
candidate nolan. it had been apparently heal over; but mr. nolan had 
not been many week in salem, after his second coming, before the 
strife broke out afresh, and alienate many for life who had till then 
been bound together by the tie of friendship or relationship. even in 
the hickson family something of this feeling soon sprang up; grace 
being a vehement partisan of the elder pastor's more gloomy doctrines, 
while faith was a passionate, if a powerless, advocate of mr. nolan. 
manasseh's grow absorption in his own fancies, and imagine gift of 
prophecy, making him comparatively indifferent to all outward events, 
did not tend to either the fulfilment of his visions, or the 
elucidate of the dark mysterious doctrine over which he had ponder 
too long for the health either of his mind or body; while prudence 
delight in irritate every one by her advocacy of the view of 
thinking to which they were most opposed, and retail every gossip 
story to the person most likely to disbelieve, and be indignant at what 
she told, with an assume unconscious of any such effect to be 
produced. there was much talk of the congregational difficulty and 
dissension being carry up to the general court, and each party 
naturally hope that, if such were the course of events, the oppose 
pastor and that portion of the congregation which adhere to him might 
be worsted in the struggle. 

such was the state of things in the township when, one day towards the 
end of the month of february, grace hickson return from the weekly 
prayer meeting; which it was her custom to attend at pastor tappau's 
house, in a state of extreme excitement. on her entrance into her own 
house she sat down, rock her body backwards and forwards, and 
pray to herself: both faith and loi stop their spinning, in 
wonder at her agitation, before either of them venture to address her. 
at length faith rose, and spoke: 

'mother, what is it? hath anything happen of an evil nature?' 

the brave, stern, old woman's face was blenched, and her eyes were 
almost set in horror, as she prayed; the great drops running down her 
cheeks. 

it seem almost as if she had to make a struggle to recover her sense 
of the present homely accustomed life, before she could find words to 
answer: 

'evil nature! daughters, satan is abroad,--is close to us. i have this 
very hour seen him afflict two innocent children, as of old he trouble 
those who were possessed by him in judea. hester and abigail tappau 
have been contort and convulse by him and his servant into such 
shape as i am afeard to think on; and when their father, godly mr. 
tappau, began to exhort and to pray, their howling were like the wild 
beast of the field. satan is of a truth let loose amongst us. the 
girl kept calling upon him as if he were even then present among us. 
abigail screech out that he stood at my very back in the guise of a 
black man; and truly, as i turn round at her words, i saw a creature 
like a shadow vanishing, and turn all of a cold sweat. who know 
where he is now? faith, lay straw across on the door-sill.' 

'but if he be already enter in,' ask prudence, 'may not that make 
it difficult for him to depart?' 

her mother, taking no notice of her question, went on rock herself, 
and praying, till again she broke out into narration: 

'reverend mr. tappau says, that only last night he heard a sound as of 
a heavy body drag all through the house by some strong power; once 
it was thrown against his bedroom door, and would, doubtless, have 
broken it in, if he had not pray fervent and aloud at that very 
time; and a shriek went up at his prayer that made his hair stand on 
end; and this morning all the crockery in the house was found broken 
and pile up in the middle of the kitchen floor; and pastor tappau 
says, that as soon as he began to ask a blessing on the morning's meal, 
abigail and hester cry out, as if some one was pinch them. lord, 
have mercy upon us all! satan is of a truth let loose.' 

'they sound like the old story i use to hear in barford,' said lois, 
breathless with affright. 

faith seem less alarmed; but then her dislike to pastor tappau was so 
great, that she could hardly sympathise with any misfortune that 
befell him or his family. 

towards evening mr. nolan came in. in general, so high did party spirit 
run, grace hickson only tolerate his visits, finding herself often 
engaged at such hours, and being too much abstracted in thought to show 
him the ready hospitality which was one of her most prominent virtues. 
but to-day, both as bring the latest intelligence of the new horrors 
sprung up in salem, and as being one of the church militant (or what 
the puritan considered as equivalent to the church militant) against 
satan, he was welcome by her in an unusual manner. 

he seem oppress with the occurrence of the day: at first it 
appear to be almost a relief to him to sit still, and cogitate upon 
them, and his host were becoming almost impatient for him to say 
something more than mere monosyllables, when he began: 

'such a day as this, i pray that i may never see again. it is as if the 
devil whom our lord banish into the herd of swine, had been 
permit to come again upon the earth. and i would it were only the 
lost spirits who were torment us; but i much fear, that certain of 
those whom we have esteem as god's people have sold their soul to 
satan, for the sake of a little of his evil power, whereby they may 
afflict other for a time. elder sherringham hath lost this very day a 
good and valuable horse, wherewith he use to drive his family to 
meeting, his wife being bedridden.' 

'perchance,' said lois, 'the horse die of some natural disease.' 

'true,' said pastor nolan; 'but i was go on to say, that as he 
enter into his house, full of dolour at the loss of his beast, a 
mouse ran in before him so sudden that it almost trip him up, though 
an instant before there was no such thing to be seen; and he caught at 
it with his shoe and hit it, and it cry out like a human creature in 
pain, and straight ran up the chimney, care nothing for the hot flame 
and smoke.' 

manasseh listen greedy to all this story, and when it was end he 
smote upon his breast, and pray aloud for deliverance from the power 
of the evil one; and he continual went on pray at interval 
through the evening, with every mark of abject terror on his face and 
in his manner--he, the bravest, most daring hunter in all the 
settlement. indeed, all the family huddle together in silent fear, 
scarcely finding any interest in the usual household occupations. faith 
and loi sat with arms entwined, as in days before the former had 
become jealous of the latter; prudence ask low, fearful question of 
her mother and of the pastor as to the creature that were abroad, and 
the ways in which they afflict others; and when grace besought the 
minister to pray for her and her household, he made a long and 
passionate supplicate that none of that little flock might ever so 
far fall away into hopeless perdition as to be guilty of the sin 
without forgiveness--the sin of witchcraft. 




chapter 3 


'the sin of witchcraft.' we read about it, we look on it from the 
outside; but we can hardly realize the terror it induced. every 
impulsive or unaccustomed action, every little nervous affection, every 
ache or pain was noticed, not merely by those around the sufferer, but 
by the person himself, whoever he might be, that was acting, or being 
act upon, in any but the most simple and ordinary manner. he or she 
(for it was most frequent a woman or girl that was the supposed 
subject) felt a desire for some unusual kind of food--some unusual 
motion or rest her hand twitched, her foot was asleep, or her leg had 
the cramp; and the dreadful question immediately suggest itself, 'is 
any one possess an evil power over me, by the help of satan?' and 
perhaps they went on to think, 'it is bad enough to feel that my body 
can be made to suffer through the power of some unknown evil-wisher to 
me, but what if satan give them still further power, and they can 
touch my soul, and inspire me with loathful thought leading me into 
crime which at present i abhor?' and so on, till the very dread of 
what might happen, and the constant dwelling of the thoughts, even with 
horror, upon certain possibilities, or what were esteem such, really 
brought about the corruption of imagination at least, which at first 
they had shudder at. moreover, there was a sort of uncertainty as to 
who might be infected--not unlike the overpowering dread of the plague, 
which made some shrink from their best-beloved with irrepressible fear. 
the brother or sister, who was the dearest friend of their childhood 
and youth, might now be bound in some mysterious deadly pact with evil 
spirits of the most horrible kind--who could tell? and in such a case 
it became a duty, a sacred duty, to give up the earthly body which had 
been once so loved, but which was now the habitation of a soul corrupt 
and horrible in its evil inclinations. possibly, terror of death might 
bring on confession and repentance, and purification. or if it did not, 
why away with the evil creature, the witch, out of the world, down to 
the kingdom of the master, whose bidding was done on earth in all 
manner of corruption and torture of god's creatures! there were other 
who, to these more simple, if more ignorant, feelings of horror at 
witch and witchcraft, add the desire, conscious or unconscious, of 
revenge on those whose conduct had been in any way displease to them. 
where evidence take a supernatural character, there is no disprove 
it. this argument come up: 'you have only the natural powers; i have 
supernatural. you admit the existence of the supernatural by the 
condemnation of this very crime of witchcraft. you hardly know the 
limit of the natural powers; how then can you define the supernatural? 
i say that in the dead of night, when my body seem to all present to 
be lie in quiet sleep, i was, in the most complete and wakeful 
consciousness, present in my body at an assembly of witch and wizard 
with satan at their head; that i was by them torture in my body, 
because my soul would not acknowledge him as its king; and that i 
witness such and such deeds. what the nature of the appearance was 
that took the semblance of myself, sleep quiet in my bed, i know 
not; but admitting, as you do, the possibility of witchcraft, you 
cannot disprove my evidence.' this evidence might be given truly or 
falsely, as the person witness believe it or not; but every one 
must see what immense and terrible power was abroad for revenge. then, 
again, the accused themselves minister to the horrible panic abroad. 
some, in dread of death, confessed from cowardice to the imaginary 
crime of which they were accused, and of which they were promise a 
pardon on confession. some, weak and terrified, came honestly to 
believe in their own guilt, through the disease of imagination which 
were sure to be engender at such a time as this. 

loi sat spin with faith. both were silent, ponder over the 
story that were abroad. loi spoke first. 

'oh, faith! this country is worse than ever england was, even in the 
days of master matthew hopkins, the witch-finder. i grow frightened of 
every one, i think. i even get afeard sometimes of nattee!' 

faith coloured a little. then she asked, 

'why? what should make you distrust the indian woman?' 

'oh! i am ashamed of my fear as soon as it arise in my mind. but, you 
know, her look and colour were strange to me when first i came; and she 
is not a christen woman; and they tell story of indian wizards; and 
i know not what the mixture are which she is sometimes stirring over 
the fire, nor the meaning of the strange chant she sing to herself. 
and once i met her in the dusk, just close by pastor tappau's house, in 
company with hota, his servant--it was just before we heard of the sore 
disturbance in his house--and i have wonder if she had aught to do 
with it.' 

faith sat very still, as if thinking. at last she said: 

'if nattee has powers beyond what you and i have, she will not use them 
for evil; at least not evil to those whom she loves.' 

'that comfort me but little,' said lois. 'if she has powers beyond 
what she ought to have, i dread her, though i have done her no evil; 
nay, though i could almost say she bore me a kindly feeling. but such 
powers are only given by the evil one; and the proof thereof is, that, 
as you imply, nattee would use them on those who offend her.' 

'and why should she not?' ask faith, lift her eyes, and flash 
heavy fire out of them at the question. 

'because,' said lois, not seeing faith's glance, 'we are told to pray 
for them that despiteful use us, and to do good to them that 
persecute us. but poor nattee is not a christen woman. i would that 
mr. nolan would baptize her; it would, maybe, take her out of the power 
of satan's temptations.' 

'are you never tempted?' ask faith, half scornfully; 'and yet i doubt 
not you were well baptized!' 

'true,' said lois, sadly; 'i often do very wrong, but, perhaps, i might 
have done worse, if the holy form had not been observed.' 

they were again silent for a time. 

'lois,' said faith, 'i did not mean any offence. but do you never feel 
as if you would give up all that future life, of which the parson 
talk, and which seem so vague and so distant, for a few years of real, 
vivid blessed to begin to-morrow--this hour, this minute? oh! i 
could think of happiness for which i would willing give up all those 
misty chance of heaven----' 

'faith, faith!' cry lois, in terror, holding her hand before her 
cousin's mouth, and look around in fright. 'hush! you know not who 
may be listening; you are putt yourself in his power.' 

but faith pushed her hand away, and said, 'lois, i believe in him no 
more than i believe in heaven. both may exist, but they are so far away 
that i defy them. why, all this ado about mr. tappau's house--promise 
me never to tell living creature, and i will tell you a secret.' 

'no!' said lois, terrified. 'i dread all secrets. i will hear none. i 
will do all that i can for you, cousin faith, in any way; but just at 
this time, i strive to keep my life and thought within the strictest 
bounds of godly simplicity, and i dread pledge myself to aught that 
is hidden and secret.' 

'as you will, cowardly girl, full of terrors, which, if you had 
listen to me, might have been lessened, if not entire done away 
with.' and faith would not utter another word, though loi try meek 
to entice her into conversation on some other subject. 

the rumour of witchcraft was like the echo of thunder among the hills. 
it had broken out in mr. tappau's house, and his two little daughter 
were the first supposed to be bewitched; but round about, from every 
quarter of the town, came in account of sufferer by witchcraft. there 
was hardly a family without one of these supposed victims. then arose a 
growl and menace of vengeance from many a household--menaces deepened, 
not daunt by the terror and mystery of the suffering that gave rise 
to them. 

at length a day was appoint when, after solemn fast and prayer, 
mr. tappau invite the neighbouring minister and all godly people to 
assemble at his house, and unite with him in devote a day to solemn 
religious services, and to supplicate for the deliverance of his 
children, and those similarly afflicted, from the power of the evil 
one. all salem pour out towards the house of the minister. there was 
a look of excitement on all their faces; eager and horror were 
depict on many, while stern resolution, amount to determined 
cruelty, if the occasion arose, was seen on others. 

in the midst of the prayer, hester tappau, the younger girl, fell into 
convulsions; fit after fit came on, and her scream mingle with the 
shriek and cries of the assemble congregation. in the first pause, 
when the child was partially recovered, when the people stood around 
exhaust and breathless, her father, the pastor tappau, lift his 
right hand, and adjure her, in the name of the trinity, to say who 
torment her. there was a dead silence; not a creature stir of all 
those hundreds. hester turn weary and uneasily, and moan out the 
name of hota, her father's indian servant. hota was present, apparently 
as much interested as any one; indeed, she had been busy herself 
much in bring remedy to the suffering child. but now she stood 
aghast, transfixed, while her name was caught up and shout out in 
tone of reprobate and hatred by all the crowd around her. another 
moment and they would have fallen upon the tremble creature and torn 
her limb from limb--pale, dusky, shiver hota, half guilty-looking 
from her very bewilderment. but pastor tappau, that gaunt, grey man, 
lift himself to his utmost height, sign to them to go back, to 
keep still while he address them; and then he told them, that instant 
vengeance was not just, deliberate punishment; that there would be need 
of conviction, perchance of confession--he hope for some redress for 
his suffering children from her revelations, if she were brought to 
confession. they must leave the culprit in his hands, and in those of 
his brother ministers, that they might wrestle with satan before 
deliver her up to the civil power. he spoke well, for he spoke from 
the heart of a father seeing his children expose to dreadful and 
mysterious suffering, and firm believe that he now held the clue in 
his hand which should ultimately release them and their 
fellow-sufferers. and the congregation moan themselves into 
unsatisfy submission, and listen to his long, passionate prayer, 
which he uplift even while the hapless hota stood there, guarded and 
bound by two men, who glare at her like bloodhound ready to slip, 
even while the prayer end in the words of the merciful saviour. 

loi sicken and shudder at the whole scene; and this was no 
intellectual shudder at the folly and superstition of the people, 
but tender moral shudder at the sight of guilt which she believe 
in, and at the evidence of men's hatred and abhorrence, which, when 
shown even to the guilty, trouble and distress her merciful heart. 
she follow her aunt and cousin out into the open air, with downcast 
eyes and pale face. grace hickson was go home with a feeling of 
triumphant relief at the detection of the guilty one. faith alone 
seem uneasy and disturbed beyond her wont, for manasseh received the 
whole transaction as the fulfilment of a prophecy, and prudence was 
excited by the novel scene into a state of discordant high spirits. 

'i am quite as old as hester tappau,' said she; 'her birthday is in 
september and mine in october.' 

'what has that to do with it?' said faith, sharply. 

'nothing, only she seem such a little thing for all those grave 
minister to be pray for, and so many folk come from a 
distance--some from boston they said--all for her sake, as it were. 
why, didst thou see, it was godly mr. henwick that held her head when 
he wriggle so, and old madam holbrook had herself help upon a chair 
to see the better. i wonder how long i might wriggle, before great and 
godly folk would take so much notice of me? but, i suppose, that come 
of being a pastor's daughter. she'll be so set up there'll be no 
speak to her now. faith! thinkest thou that hota really had 
bewitch her? she gave me corn-cakes, the last time i was at pastor 
tappau's, just like any other woman, only, perchance, a trifle more 
good-natured; and to think of her being a witch after all!' 

but faith seem in a hurry to reach home, paid no attention to 
prudence's talking. loi hasten on with faith, for manasseh was 
walking alongside of his mother, and she kept steady to her plan of 
avoid him, even though she pressed her company upon faith, who had 
seem of late desirous of avoid her. 

that evening the news spread through salem, that hota had confessed her 
sin--had acknowledge that she was a witch. nattee was the first to 
hear the intelligence. she broke into the room where the girl were 
sitting with grace hickson, solemn do nothing, because of the 
great prayer-meeting in the morning, and cry out, 'mercy, mercy, 
mistress, everybody! take care of poor indian nattee, who never do 
wrong, but for mistress and the family! hota one bad wicked witch, she 
say so herself; oh, me! oh me!' and stoop over faith, she said 
something in a low, miserable tone of voice, of which loi only heard 
the word 'torture.' but faith heard all, and turning very pale, half 
accompanied, half led nattee back to her kitchen. 

presently, grace hickson came in. she had been out to see a neighbour; 
it will not do to say that so godly a woman had been gossiping; and, 
indeed, the subject of the conversation she had held was of too serious 
and momentous a nature for me to employ a light word to designate it. 
there was all the listen to and repeating of small detail and 
rumours, in which the speaker have no concern, that constitute 
gossiping; but in this instance, all trivial fact and speech might 
be considered to bear such dreadful significance, and might have so 
ghastly an ending, that such whisper were occasional raise to a 
tragic importance. every fragment of intelligence that related to mr. 
tappau's household was eager snatch at; how his dog howl all one 
long night through, and could not be stilled; how his cow sudden 
fail in her milk only two month after she had calved; how his memory 
had forsaken him one morning, for a minute or two, in repeating the 
lord's prayer, and he had even omit a clause thereof in his sudden 
perturbation; and how all these forerunner of his children's strange 
illness might now be interpret and understood--this had form the 
staple of the conversation between grace hickson and her friends. there 
had arisen a dispute among them at last, as to how far these 
subjection to the power of the evil one were to be considered as a 
judgment upon pastor tappau for some sin on his part; and if so, what? 
it was not an unpleasant discussion, although there was considerable 
difference of opinion; for as none of the speaker had had their 
family so troubled, it was rather a proof that they had none of them 
committed any sin. in the midst of this talk, one, enter in from the 
street, brought the news that hota had confessed all--had own to 
sign a certain little red book which satan had present to her--had 
been present at impious sacraments--had ridden through the air to 
newbury falls--and, in fact, had assent to all the question which 
the elder and magistrates, careful reading over the confession of 
the witch who had formerly been try in england, in order that they 
might not omit a single inquiry, had ask of her. more she had own 
to, but things of inferior importance, and partake more of the nature 
of earthly trick than of spiritual power. she had spoken of careful 
adjust strings, by which all the crockery in pastor tappau's house 
could be pull down or disturbed; but of such intelligible 
malpractice the gossip of salem took little heed. one of them said 
that such an action show satan's prompting, but they all prefer to 
listen to the grand guilt of the blasphemous sacrament and 
supernatural rides. the narrator end with saying that hota was to be 
hung the next morning, in spite of her confession, even although her 
life had been promise to her if she acknowledge her sin; for it was 
well to make an example of the first-discovered witch, and it was also 
well that she was an indian, a heathen, whose life would be no great 
loss to the community. grace hickson on this spoke out. it was well 
that witch should perish off the face of the earth, indian or 
english, heathen or, worse, a baptize christian who had betray the 
lord, even as judas did, and had gone over to satan. for her part, she 
wish that the first-discovered witch had been a member of a godly 
english household, that it might be seen of all men that religious folk 
were willing to cut off the right hand, and pluck out the right eye, if 
taint with this devilish sin. she spoke stern and well. the last 
comer said, that her words might be brought to the proof, for it had 
been whisper that hota had name others, and some from the most 
religious family of salem, whom she had seen among the unholy 
communicant at the sacrament of the evil one. and grace reply that 
she would answer for it, all godly folk would stand the proof, and 
quench all natural affection rather than that such a sin should grow 
and spread among them. she herself had a weak bodily dread of 
witness the violent death even of an animal; but she would not let 
that deter her from standing amidst those who cast the accursed 
creature out from among them on the morrow morning. 

contrary to her wont, grace hickson told her family much of this 
conversation. it was a sign of her excitement on the subject that she 
thus spoke, and the excitement spread in different form through her 
family. faith was flushed and restless, wandering between the 
keeping-room and the kitchen, and questioning her mother particularly 
as to the more extraordinary parts of hota's confession, as if she 
wish to satisfy herself that the indian witch had really done those 
horrible and mysterious deeds. 

loi shiver and tremble with affright at the narration, and the idea 
that such things were possible. occasional she found herself 
wandering off into sympathetic thought for the woman who was to die, 
abhor of all men, and unpardoned by god, to whom she had been so 
fearful a traitor, and who was now, at this very time--when loi sat 
among her kindred by the warm and cheerful firelight, anticipate many 
peaceful, perchance happy, morrows--solitary, shivering, 
panic-stricken, guilty, with none to stand by her and exhort her, shut 
up in dark between the cold wall of the town prison. but loi 
almost shrank from sympathise with so loathsome an accomplice of 
satan, and pray for forgiveness for her charitable thought; and yet, 
again, she remember the tender spirit of the saviour, and allow 
herself to fall into pity, till at last her sense of right and wrong 
became so bewilder that she could only leave all to god's disposal, 
and just ask that he would take all creature and all event into his 
hands. 

prudence was as bright as if she were listen to some merry 
story--curious as to more than her mother would tell her--seeming to 
have no particular terror of witch or witchcraft, and yet to be 
especially desirous to accompany her mother the next morning to the 
hanging. loi shrank from the cruel, eager face of the young girl as 
she beg her mother to allow her to go. even grace was disturbed and 
perplexed by her daughter's pertinacity. 

'no!' said she. 'ask me no more. thou shalt not go. such sight are not 
for the young. i go, and i sicken at the thought of it. but i go to 
show that i, a christian woman, take god's part against the devil's. 
thou shalt not go, i tell thee. i could whip thee for thinking of it.' 

'manasseh say hota was well whip by pastor tappau ere she was 
brought to confession,' said prudence, as if anxious to change the 
subject of discussion. 

manasseh lift up his head from the great folio bible, brought by his 
father from england, which he was studying. he had not heard what 
prudence said, but he look up at the sound of his name. all present 
were startle at his wild eyes, his bloodless face. but he was 
evidently annoy at the expression of their countenances. 

'why look ye at me in that manner?' ask he. and his manner was 
anxious and agitated. his mother made haste to speak: 

'it was but that prudence said something that thou hast told her--that 
pastor tappau defile his hand by whipping the witch hota. what evil 
thought has got hold of thee? talk to us, and crack not thy skull 
against the learning of man.' 

'it is not the learning of man that i study: it is the word of god. i 
would fain know more of the nature of this sin of witchcraft, and 
whether it be, indeed, the unpardonable sin against the holy ghost. at 
times i feel a creep influence coming over me, prompt all evil 
thought and unheard-of deeds, and i question within myself, "is not 
this the power of witchcraft?" and i sicken, and loathe all that i do 
or say, and yet some evil creature hath the mastery over me, and i must 
needs do and say what i loathe and dread. why wonder you, mother, that 
i, of all men, strive to learn the exact nature of witchcraft, and for 
that end study the word of god? have you not seen me when i was, as it 
were, possessed with a devil?' 

he spoke calmly, sadly, but as under deep conviction. his mother rose 
to comfort him. 

'my son,' she said, 'no one ever saw thee do deeds, or heard thee utter 
words, which any one could say were prompt by devils. we have seen 
thee, poor lad, with thy wit gone astray for a time, but all thy 
thought sought rather god's will in forbidden places, than lost the 
clue to them for one moment in hankering after the powers of darkness. 
those days are long past; a future lie before thee. think not of 
witch or of being subject to the power of witchcraft. i did evil to 
speak of it before thee. let loi come and sit by thee, and talk to 
thee.' 

loi went to her cousin, grieve at heart for his depressed state of 
mind, anxious to soothe and comfort him, and yet recoil more than 
ever from the idea of ultimately becoming his wife--an idea to which 
she saw her aunt reconcile herself unconscious day by day, as she 
perceive the english girl's power of soothe and comfort her 
cousin, even by the very tone of her sweet coo voice. 

he took lois's hand. 

'let me hold it. it do me good,' said he. 'ah, lois, when i am by you 
i forget all my troubles--will the day never come when you will listen 
to the voice that speak to me continually?' 

'i never hear it, cousin manasseh,' she said, softly; 'but do not think 
of the voices. tell me of the land you hope to enclose from the 
forest--what manner of tree grow on it?' 

thus, by simple question on practical affairs, she led him back, in 
her unconscious wisdom, to the subject on which he had always shown 
strong practical sense. he talk on these with all due discretion till 
the hour for family prayer came round, which was early in those days. 
it was manasseh's place to conduct it, as head of the family; a post 
which his mother had always been anxious to assign to him since her 
husband's death. he pray extempore; and to-night his supplicate 
wander off into wild, unconnected fragment of prayer, which all 
those kneel around began, each according to her anxiety for the 
speaker, to think would never end. minutes elapsed, and grew to 
quarters of an hour, and his words only became more emphatic and 
wilder, pray for himself alone, and lay bare the recess of his 
heart. at length his mother rose, and took loi by the hand, for she 
had faith in lois's power over her son, as being akin to that which the 
shepherd david, play on his harp, had over king saul sitting on his 
throne. she drew her towards him, where he knelt facing into the 
circle, with his eyes upturned, and the trance agony of his face 
depict the struggle of the trouble soul within. 

'here is lois,' said grace, almost tenderly; 'she would fain go to her 
chamber.' (down the girl's face the tear were streaming.) 'rise, and 
finish thy prayer in thy closet.' 

but at lois's approach he sprang to his feet,--sprang aside. 

'take her away, mother! lead me not into temptation. she bring me evil 
and sinful thoughts. she overshadow me, even in the presence of my 
god. she is no angel of light, or she would not do this. she trouble 
me with the sound of a voice bidding me marry her, even when i am at my 
prayers. avaunt! take her away!' 

he would have struck at loi if she had not shrunk back, dismay and 
affrighted. his mother, although equally dismayed, was not affrighted. 
she had seen him thus before; and understood the management of his 
paroxysm. 

'go, lois! the sight of thee irritate him, as once that of faith did. 
leave him to me.' 

and loi rush away to her room, and threw herself on her bed, like a 
panting, hunt creature. faith came after her slow and heavily. 

'lois,' said she, 'wilt thou do me a favour? it is not much to ask. 
wilt thou arise before daylight, and bear this letter from me to pastor 
nolan's lodgings? i would have done it myself, but mother has bidden me 
to come to her, and i may be detain until the time when hota is to be 
hung; and the letter tell of matter pertain to life and death. 
seek out pastor nolan wherever he may be, and have speech of him after 
he has read the letter.' 

'cannot nattee take it?' ask lois. 

'no!' faith answered, fiercely. 'why should she?' 

but loi did not reply. a quick suspicion dart through faith's mind, 
sudden as lightning. it had never enter there before. 

'speak, lois. i read thy thoughts. thou wouldst fain not be the bearer 
of this letter?' 

'i will take it,' said lois, meekly. 'it concern life and death, you 
say?' 

'yes!' said faith, in quite a different tone of voice. but, after a 
pause of thought, she added: 'then, as soon as the house is still, i 
will write what i have to say, and leave it here, on this chest; and 
thou wilt promise me to take it before the day is fully up, while there 
is yet time for action.' 

'yes! i promise,' said lois. and faith knew enough of her to feel sure 
that the deed would be done, however reluctantly. 

the letter was written--laid on the chest; and, ere day dawned, loi 
was astir, faith watch her from between her half-closed 
eyelids--eyelids that had never been fully closed in sleep the livelong 
night. the instant lois, cloak and hooded, left the room, faith 
sprang up, and prepared to go to her mother, whom she heard already 
stirring. nearly every one in salem was awake and up on this awful 
morning, though few were out of doors, as loi pass along the 
streets. here was the hasty erect gallows, the black shadow of 
which fell across the street with ghastly significance; now she had to 
pass the iron-barred gaol, through the unglazed window of which she 
heard the fearful cry of a woman, and the sound of many footsteps. on 
she sped, sick almost to faintness, to the widow woman's where mr. 
nolan lodged. he was already up and abroad, gone, his hostess believed, 
to the gaol. thither lois, repeating the words 'for life and for 
death!' was forced to go. retrace her steps, she was thankful to see 
him come out of those dismal portals, render more dismal for being in 
heavy shadow, just as she approached. what his errand had been she knew 
not; but he look grave and sad, as she put faith's letter into his 
hands, and stood before him quiet waiting, until he should read it, 
and deliver the expect answer. but, instead of opening it, he held it 
in his hand, apparently absorb in thought. at last he spoke aloud, 
but more to himself than to her: 

'my god! and is she then to die in this fearful delirium? it must 
be--can be--only delirium, that prompt such wild and horrible 
confessions. mistress barclay, i come from the presence of the indian 
woman appoint to die. it seems, she considered herself betray last 
evening by her sentence not being respited, even after she had made 
confession of sin enough to bring down fire from heaven; and, it seem 
to me, the passionate, impotent anger of this helpless creature has 
turn to madness, for she appall me by the additional revelation she 
has made to the keeper during the night--to me this morning. i could 
almost fancy that she thinks, by deepen the guilt she confesses, to 
escape this last dread punishment of all, as if, were a tithe of what 
she say true, one could suffer such a sinner to live. yet to send her 
to death in such a state of mad terror! what is to be done?' 

'yet scripture say that we are not to suffer witch in the land,' 
said lois, slowly. 

'true; i would but ask for a respite till the prayer of god's people 
had gone up for his mercy. some would pray for her, poor wretch as she 
is. you would, mistress barclay, i am sure?' but he said it in a 
questioning tone. 

'i have been pray for her in the night many a time,' said lois, in a 
low voice. 'i pray for her in my heart at this moment; i suppose; they 
are bidden to put her out of the land, but i would not have her 
entire god-forsaken. but, sir, you have not read my cousin's letter. 
and she bade me bring back an answer with much urgency.' 

still he delayed. he was thinking of the dreadful confession he came 
from hearing. if it were true, the beautiful earth was a pollute 
place, and he almost wish to die, to escape from such pollution, into 
the white innocence of those who stood in the presence of god. 

sudden his eyes fell on lois's pure, grave face, upturned and 
watch his. faith in earthly goodness came over his soul in that 
instant, 'and he blessed her unaware.' 

he put his hand on her shoulder, with an action half paternal--although 
the difference in their age was not above a dozen years--and, bend 
a little towards her, whispered, half to himself, 'mistress barclay, 
you have done me good.' 

'i!' said lois, half affrighted--'i done you good! how?' 

'by being what you are. but, perhaps, i should rather thank god, who 
sent you at the very moment when my soul was so disquieted.' 

at this instant, they were aware of faith standing in front of them, 
with a countenance of thunder. her angry look made loi feel guilty. 
she had not enough urge the pastor to read his letter, she thought; 
and it was indignation at this delay in what she had been commission 
to do with the urgent of life or death, that made her cousin lower at 
her so from beneath her straight black brows. loi explain how she 
had not found mr. nolan at his lodgings, and had had to follow him to 
the door of the gaol. but faith replied, with obdurate contempt: 

'spare thy breath, cousin lois. it is easy seeing on what pleasant 
matter thou and the pastor nolan were talking. i marvel not at thy 
forgetfulness. my mind is changed. give me back my letter, sir; it was 
about a poor matter--an old woman's life. and what is that compare to 
a young girl's love?' 

loi heard but for an instant; did not understand that her cousin, in 
her jealous anger, could suspect the existence of such a feeling as 
love between her and mr. nolan. no imagination as to its possibility 
had ever enter her mind; she had respect him, almost revere 
him--nay, had like him as the probable husband of faith. at the 
thought that her cousin could believe her guilty of such treachery, her 
grave eyes dilated, and fixed themselves on the flaming countenance of 
faith. that serious, unprotest manner of perfect innocence must have 
told on her accuser, had it not been that, at the same instant, the 
latter caught sight of the crimson and disturbed countenance of the 
pastor, who felt the veil rent off the unconscious secret of his heart. 
faith snatch her letter out of his hands, and said: 

'let the witch hang! what care i? she has done harm enough with her 
charm and her sorcery on pastor tappau's girls. let her die, and let 
all other witch look to themselves; for there be many kind of 
witchcraft abroad. cousin lois, thou wilt like best to stop with pastor 
nolan, or i would pray thee to come back with me to breakfast.' 

loi was not to be daunt by jealous sarcasm. she held out her hand to 
pastor nolan, determined to take no heed of her cousin's mad words, but 
to bid him farewell in her accustomed manner. he hesitate before 
taking it, and when he did, it was with a convulsive squeeze that 
almost made her start. faith wait and watch all, with set lip and 
vengeful eyes. she bade no farewell; she spake no word; but grasping 
loi tight by the back of the arm, she almost drove her before her 
down the street till they reach their home. 

the arrangement for the morning was this: grace hickson and her son 
manasseh were to be present at the hanging of the first witch execute 
in salem, as pious and godly head of a family. all the other member 
were strict forbidden to stir out, until such time as the low-tolling 
bell announce that all was over in this world for hota, the indian 
witch. when the execution was ended, there was to be a solemn 
prayer-meeting of all the inhabitant of salem; minister had come from 
a distance to aid by the efficacy of their prayer in these effort to 
purge the land of the devil and his servants. there was reason to think 
that the great old meeting-house would be crowded, and when faith and 
loi reach home, grace hickson was give her directions to prudence, 
urge her to be ready for an early start to that place. the stern old 
woman was trouble in her mind at the anticipation of the sight she was 
to see, before many minutes were over, and spoke in a more hurried and 
incoherent manner than was her wont. she was dress in her sunday 
best; but her face was very grey and colourless, and she seem afraid 
to cease speak about household affairs, for fear she should have 
time to think. manasseh stood by her, perfectly, rigid still; he also 
was in his sunday clothes. his face, too, was pale than its wont, but 
it wore a kind of absent, rapt expression, almost like that of a man 
who see a vision. as faith entered, still holding loi in her fierce 
grasp, manasseh start and smiled; but still dreamily. his manner was 
so peculiar, that even his mother stay her talk to observe him 
more closely; he was in that state of excitement which usually end in 
what his mother and certain of her friends esteem a prophetic 
revelation. he began to speak, at first very low, and then his voice 
increase in power: 

'how beautiful is the land of beulah, far over the sea, beyond the 
mountains! thither the angel carry her, lie back in their arms like 
one fainting. they shall kiss away the black circle of death, and lay 
her down at the feet of the lamb. i hear her pleading there for those 
on earth who consent to her death. o lois! pray also for me, pray for 
me, miserable!' 

when he utter his cousin's name all their eyes turn towards her. it 
was to her that his vision related! she stood among them, amazed, 
awe-stricken, but not like one affrighte or dismayed. she was the 
first to speak: 

'dear friends, do not think of me; his words may or may not be true. i 
am in god's hand all the same, whether he have the gift of prophecy or 
not. besides, hear you not that i end where all would fain end? think 
of him, and of his needs. such times as these always leave him 
exhaust and weary, when he come out of them.' 

and she busy herself in care for his refreshment, aid her aunt's 
tremble hand to set before him the requisite food, as he now sat 
tired and bewildered, gathering together with difficulty his scattered 
senses. 

prudence did all she could to assist and speed their departure. but 
faith stood apart, watch in silence with her passionate, angry eyes. 

as soon as they had set out on their solemn, fatal errand, faith left 
the room. she had not taste food or touched drink. indeed, they all 
felt sick at heart. the moment her sister had gone up stairs, prudence 
sprang to the settle on which loi had thrown down her cloak and hood: 

'lend me your muffle and mantle, cousin lois. i never yet saw a woman 
hanged, and i see not why i should not go. i will stand on the edge of 
the crowd; no one will know me, and i will be home long before my 
mother.' 

'no!' said lois, 'that may not be. my aunt would be sore displeased. i 
wonder at you, prudence, seek to witness such a sight.' and as she 
spoke she held fast her cloak, which prudence vehement struggle for. 

faith returned, brought back possibly by the sound of the struggle. she 
smiled--a deadly smile. 

'give it up, prudence. strive no more with her. she has bought success 
in this world, and we are but her slaves.' 

'oh, faith!' said lois, relinquish her hold of the cloak, and 
turning round with passionate reproach in her look and voice, 'what 
have i done that you should speak so of me; you, that have love as i 
think one love a sister?' 

prudence did not lose her opportunity, but hasty array herself in 
the mantle, which was too large for her, and which she had, therefore, 
considered as well adapt for concealment; but, as she went towards 
the door, her feet became entangle in the unusual length, and she 
fell, bruising her arm pretty sharply. 

'take care, another time, how you meddle with a witch's things,' said 
faith, as one scarcely believe her own words, but at enmity with all 
the world in her bitter jealousy of heart. prudence rub her arm and 
look stealthy at lois. 

'witch lois! witch lois!' said she at last, softly, pull a childish 
face of spite at her. 

'oh, hush, prudence! do not bandy such terrible words. let me look at 
thine arm. i am sorry for thy hurt, only glad that it has kept thee 
from disobey thy mother.' 

'away, away!' said prudence, spring from her. 'i am afeard of her in 
very truth, faith. keep between me and the witch, or i will throw a 
stool at her.' 

faith smiled--it was a bad and wicked smile--but she did not stir to 
calm the fear she had call up in her young sister. just at this 
moment, the bell began to toll. hota, the indian witch, was dead. loi 
cover her face with her hands. even faith went a deadly pale than 
she had been, and said, sighing, 'poor hota! but death is best.' 

prudence alone seem unmoved by any thought connected with the 
solemn, monotonous sound. her only consideration was, that now she 
might go out into the street and see the sights, and hear the news, and 
escape from the terror which she felt at the presence of her cousin. 
she flew up stairs to find her own mantle, ran down again, and past 
lois, before the english girl had finished her prayer, and was speedy 
mingle among the crowd go to the meetinghouse. there also faith and 
loi came in due course of time, but separately, not together. faith so 
evidently avoid lois, that she, humble and grieved, could not force 
her company upon her cousin, but loiter a little behind,--the quiet 
tear steal down her face, shed for the many cause that had 
occur this morning. 

the meeting-house was full to suffocation; and, as it sometimes happen 
on such occasions, the greatest crowd was close about the doors, from 
the fact that few saw, on their first entrance, where there might be 
possible space into which they could wedge themselves. yet they were 
impatient of any arrival from the outside, and pushed and hustle 
faith, and after her lois, till the two were forced on to a conspicuous 
place in the very centre of the building, where there was no chance of 
a seat, but still space to stand in. several stood around, the pulpit 
being in the middle, and already occupy by two minister in geneva 
band and gowns, while other ministers, similarly attired, stood 
holding on to it, almost as if they were give support instead of 
receiving it. grace hickson and her son sat decorous in their own 
pew, thereby showing that they had arrive early from the execution. 
you might almost have trace out the number of those who had been at 
the hanging of the indian witch, by the expression of their 
countenances. they were awestricken into terrible repose; while the 
crowd pour in, still pour in, of those who had not attend the 
execution, look all restless, and excited, and fierce. a buzz went 
round the meeting, that the stranger minister who stood along with 
pastor tappau in the pulpit was no other than dr. cotton mather 
himself, come all the way from boston to assist in purge salem of 
witches. 

and now pastor tappau began his prayer, extempore, as was the custom. 
his words were wild and incoherent, as might be expect from a man who 
had just been consent to the bloody death of one who was, but a few 
days ago, a member of his own family; violent and passionate, as was to 
be look for in the father of children, whom he believe to suffer so 
fearful from the crime he would denounce before the lord. he sat down 
at length from pure exhaustion. then dr. cotton mather stood forward: 
he did not utter more than a few words of prayer, calm in comparison 
with what had gone before, and then he went on to address the great 
crowd before him in a quiet, argumentative way, but arrange what he 
had to say with something of the same kind of skill which antony use 
in his speech to the roman after cæsar's murder. some of dr. mather's 
words have been preserve to us, as he afterwards wrote them down in
