one of his works. speak of those 'unbelieving sadducees' who doubt 
the existence of such a crime, he said: 'instead of their apish shout 
and jeer at blessed scripture, and history which have such undoubted 
confirmation as that no man that has breeding enough to regard the 
common law of human society will offer to doubt of them, it become us 
rather to adore the goodness of god, who from the mouth of babe and 
suckling has ordain truth, and by the means of the sore-afflicted 
children of your godly pastor, has reveal the fact that the devil 
have with most horrid operation broken in upon your neighbourhood. let 
us beseech him that their power may be restrained, and that they go not 
so far in their evil machination as they did but four years ago in the 
city of boston, where i was the humble means, under god, of loose 
from the power of satan the four children of that religious and blessed 
man, mr. goodwin. these four babe of grace were bewitch by an ireland 
witch; there is no end to the narration of the torment they had to 
submit to. at one time they would bark like dogs, at another purr like 
cats; yea, they would fly like geese, and be carry with an incredible 
swiftness, have but just their toe now and then upon the ground, 
sometimes not once in twenty feet, and their arms wave like those of a 
bird. yet at other times, by the hellish device of the woman who had 
bewitch them, they could not stir without limping, for, by means of 
an invisible chain, she hamper their limbs, or, sometimes, by means 
of a noose, almost choke them. one in especial was subject by this 
woman of satan to such heat as of an oven, that i myself have seen the 
sweat drop from off her, while all around were moderately cold and well 
at ease. but not to trouble you with more of my stories, i will go on 
to prove that it was satan himself that held power over her. for a very 
remarkable thing it was, that she was not permit by that evil spirit 
to read any godly or religious book, speak the truth as it is in 
jesus. she could read popish books well enough, while both sight and 
speech seem to fail her when i gave her the assembly's catechism. 
again, she was fond of that prelate book of common prayer, which is 
but the roman mass-book in an english and ungodly shape. in the midst 
of her sufferings, if one put the prayer-book into her hand it 
relieved her. yet mark you, she could never be brought to read the 
lord's prayer, whatever book she met with it in, prove thereby 
distinct that she was in league with the devil. i took her into my 
own house, that i, even as dr. martin luther did, might wrestle with 
the devil and have my fling at him. but when i call my household to 
prayer, the devil that possessed her cause her to whistle, and sing, 
and yell in a discordant and hellish fashion.' 

at this very instant, a shrill, clear whistle pierce all ears. dr. 
mather stop for a moment: 

'satan is among you!' he cried. 'look to yourselves!' and he pray 
with fervour, as if against a present and threaten enemy; but no one 
heed him. whence came that ominous, unearthly whistle? every man 
watch his neighbour. again the whistle, out of their very midst! and 
then a bustle in a corner of the building, three or four people 
stirring, without any cause immediately perceptible to those at a 
distance, the movement spread, and, directly after, a passage even in 
that dense mass of people was clear for two men, who bore forward 
prudence hickson, lie rigid as a log of wood, in the convulsive 
position of one who suffer from an epileptic fit. they laid her down 
among the minister who were gather round the pulpit. her mother came 
to her, send up a wail cry at the sight of her distort child. 
dr. mather came down from the pulpit and stood over her, exorcise the 
devil in possession, as one accustomed to such scenes. the crowd 
pressed forward in mute horror. at length, her rigidity of form and 
feature gave way, and she was terribly convulsed--torn by the devil, as 
they call it. by-and-by the violence of the attack was over, and the 
spectator began to breathe once more, though still the former horror 
brood over them, and they listen as if for the sudden ominous 
whistle again, and glance fearful around, as if satan were at their 
back pick out his next victim. 

meanwhile, dr. mather, pastor tappau, and one or two other were 
exhort prudence to reveal, if she could, the name of the person, the 
witch, who, by influence over satan, had subject the child to such 
torture as that which they had just witnessed. they bade her speak in 
the name of the lord. she whisper a name in the low voice of 
exhaustion. none of the congregation could hear what it was. but the 
pastor tappau, when he heard it, drew back in dismay, while dr. mather, 
knowing not to whom the name belonged, cry out, in a clear, cold 
voice, 

'know ye one loi barclay; for it is she who hath bewitch this poor 
child?' 

the answer was given rather by action than by word, although a low 
murmur went up from many. but all fell back, as far as fall back in 
such a crowd was possible, from loi barclay, where she stood,--and 
look on her with surprise and horror. a space of some feet, where no 
possibility of space had seem to be not a minute before, left loi 
standing alone, with every eye fixed upon her in hatred and dread. she 
stood like one speechless, tongue-tied, as if in a dream. she a witch! 
accursed as witch were in the sight of god and man! her smooth, 
healthy face became contract into shrivel and pallor, but she utter 
not a word, only look at dr. mather with her dilated, terrify eyes. 

some one said, 'she is of the household of grace hickson, a god-fearing 
woman.' loi did not know if the words were in her favour or not. she 
did not think about them, even; they told less on her than on any 
person present. she a witch! and the silver glittering avon, and the 
drown woman she had seen in her childhood at barford,--at home in 
england,--were before her, and her eyes fell before her doom. there was 
some commotion--some rustling of papers; the magistrate of the town 
were drawing near the pulpit and consulting with the ministers. dr. 
mather spoke again: 

'the indian woman, who was hung this morning, name certain people, 
whom she depose to have seen at the horrible meeting for the 
worship of satan; but there is no name of loi barclay down upon the 
paper, although we are stricken at the sight of the name of some----' 

an interruption--a consultation. again dr. mather spoke: 

'bring the accused witch, loi barclay, near to this poor suffering 
child of christ.' 

they rush forward to force loi to the place where prudence lay. but 
loi walk forward of herself. 

'prudence,' she said, in such a sweet, touching voice, that, long 
afterwards, those who heard it that day, spoke of it to their children, 
'have i ever said an unkind word to you, much less done you an ill 
turn? speak, dear child. you did not know what you said just now, did 
you?' 

but prudence writhe away from her approach, and scream out, as if 
stricken with fresh agony. 

'take her away! take her away! witch lois, witch lois, who threw me 
down only this morning, and turn my arm black and blue.' and she 
bare her arm, as if in confirmation of her words. it was sorely 
bruised. 

'i was not near you, prudence!' said lois, sadly. but that was only 
reckon fresh evidence of her diabolical power. 

lois's brain began to get bewildered. witch lois! she a witch, abhor 
of all men! yet she would try to think, and make one more effort. 

'aunt hickson,' she said, and grace came forwards--'am i a witch, aunt 
hickson?' she asked; for her aunt, stern, harsh, unlove as she might 
be, was truth itself, and loi thought--so near to delirium had she 
come--if her aunt condemn her, it was possible she might indeed be a 
witch. 

grace hickson face her unwillingly. 

'it is a stain upon our family for ever,' was the thought in her mind. 

'it is for god to judge whether thou art a witch, or not. not for me.' 

'alas, alas!' moan lois; for she had look at faith, and learnt that 
no good word was to be expect from her gloomy face and avert eyes. 
the meeting-house was full of eager voices, repressed, out of reverence 
for the place, into tone of earnest murmur that seem to fill the 
air with gathering sound of anger, and those who had at first fallen 
back from the place where loi stood were now pressing forward and 
round about her, ready to seize the young friendless girl, and bear her 
off to prison. those who might have been, who ought to have been, her 
friends, were either averse or indifferent to her; though only prudence 
made any open outcry upon her. that evil child cry out perpetual 
that loi had cast a devilish spell upon her, and bade them keep the 
witch away from her; and, indeed, prudence was strange convulse when 
once or twice lois's perplexed and wistful eyes were turn in her 
direction. here and there girls, women utter strange cries, and 
apparently suffering from the same kind of convulsive fit as that which 
had attack prudence, were centre of a group of agitate friends, who 
mutter much and savage of witchcraft, and the list which had been 
taken down only the night before from hota's own lips. they demand to 
have it made public, and object to the slow form of the law. others, 
not so much or so immediately interested in the sufferers, were 
kneel around, and pray aloud for themselves and their own safety, 
until the excitement should be so much quell as to enable dr. cotton 
mather to be again heard in prayer and exhortation. 

and where was manasseh? what said he? you must remember, that the stir 
of the outcry, the accusation, the appeal of the accused, all seem 
to go on at once amid the buzz and din of the people who had come to 
worship god, but remain to judge and upbraid their fellow-creature. 
till now loi had only caught a glimpse of manasseh, who was apparently 
try to push forwards, but whom his mother was holding back with word 
and action, as loi knew she would hold him back; for it was not for 
the first time that she was made aware how careful her aunt had 
always shroud his decent reputation among his fellow-citizens from 
the least suspicion of his season of excitement and incipient 
insanity. on such days, when he himself imagine that he heard 
prophetic voices, and saw prophetic visions, his mother would do much 
to prevent any besides his own family from seeing him; and now lois, by 
a process swift than reasoning, felt certain, from her one look at 
his face, when she saw it, colourless and deform by intensity of 
expression, among a number of other all simply ruddy and angry, that 
he was in such a state that his mother would in vain do her utmost to 
prevent his making himself conspicuous. whatever force or argument 
grace used, it was of no avail. in another moment he was by lois's 
side, stammer with excitement, and give vague testimony, which 
would have been of little value in a calm court of justice, and was 
only oil to the smoulder fire of that audience. 

'away with her to gaol!' 'seek out the witches!' 'the sin has spread 
into all households!' 'satan is in the very midst of us!' 'strike and 
spare not!' in vain dr. cotton mather raise his voice in loud prayers, 
in which he assume the guilt of the accused girl; no one listened, all 
were anxious to secure lois, as if they fear she would vanish from 
before their very eyes; she, white, trembling, standing quite still in 
the tight grasp of strange, fierce men, her dilate eyes only wandering 
a little now and then in search of some pitiful face--some pitiful face 
that among all those hundred was not to be found. while some fetch 
cords to bind her, and others, by low questions, suggest new 
accusation to the distempered brain of prudence, manasseh obtain a 
hearing once more. address dr. cotton mather, he said, evidently 
anxious to make clear some new argument that had just suggest itself 
to him: 'sir, in this matter, be she witch or not, the end has been 
foreshown to me by the spirit of prophecy. now, reverend sir, if the 
event be known to the spirit, it must have been foredoomed in the 
council of god. if so, why punish her for do that in which she had 
no free will?' 

'young man,' said dr. mather, bend down from the pulpit and look 
very severe upon manasseh, 'take care! you are trench on 
blasphemy.' 

'i do not care. i say it again. either loi barclay is a witch, or she 
is not. if she is, it has been foredoomed for her, for i have seen a 
vision of her death as a condemn witch for many month past--and the 
voice has told me there was but one escape for her, lois--the voice you 
know--' in his excitement he began to wander a little, but it was 
touching to see how conscious he was that by give way he would lose 
the thread of the logical argument by which he hope to prove that loi 
ought not to be punished, and with what an effort he wrench his 
imagination away from the old ideas, and strove to concentrate all his 
mind upon the plea that, if loi was a witch, it had been shown him by 
prophecy; and if there was prophecy there must be foreknowledge; if 
foreknowledge, foredoom; if foredoom, no exercise of free will, and, 
therefore, that loi was not just amenable to punishment. 

on he went, plunge into heresy, care not--growing more and more 
passionate every instant, but direct his passion into keen argument, 
desperate sarcasm, instead of allow it to excite his imagination. 
even dr. mather felt himself on the point of being worsted in the very 
presence of this congregation, who, but a short half-hour ago, look 
upon him as all but infallible. keep a good heart, cotton mather! your 
opponent's eye begin to glare and flicker with a terrible yet 
uncertain light--his speech grow less coherent, and his argument are 
mixed up with wild glimpse at wild revelation made to himself 
alone. he has touched on the limits,--he has enter the border of 
blasphemy, and with an awful cry of horror and reprobate the 
congregation rise up, as one man, against the blasphemer. dr. mather 
smile a grim smile, and the people were ready to stone manasseh, who 
went on, regardless, talk and raving. 

'stay, stay!' said grace hickson--all the decent family shame which 
prompt her to conceal the mysterious misfortune of her only son from 
public knowledge done away with by the sense of the immediate danger to 
his life. 'touch him not. he know not what he is saying. the fit is 
upon him. i tell you the truth before god. my son, my only son, is 
mad.' 

they stood aghast at the intelligence. the grave young citizen, who had 
silent taken his part in life close by them in their daily lives--not 
mix much with them, it was true, but look up to, perhaps, all the 
more--the student of abstruse books on theology, fit to converse with 
the most learned minister that ever came about those parts--was he the 
same with the man now pour out wild words to loi the witch, as if 
he and she were the only two present! a solution of it all occur to 
them. he was another victim. great was the power of satan! through the 
arts of the devil, that white statue of a girl had master the soul of 
manasseh hickson. so the word spread from mouth to mouth. and grace 
heard it. it seem a heal balsam for her shame. with wilful, 
dishonest blindness, she would not see--not even in her secret heart 
would she acknowledge, that manasseh had been strange, and moody, and 
violent long before the english girl had reach salem. she even found 
some specious reason for his attempt at suicide long ago. he was 
recover from a fever--and though tolerably well in health, the 
delirium had not finally left him. but since loi came, how headstrong 
he had been at times! how unreasonable! how moody! what a strange 
delusion was that which he was under, of being bidden by some voice to 
marry her! how he follow her about, and clung to her, as under some 
compulsion of affection! and over all reign the idea that, if he were 
indeed suffering from being bewitched, he was not mad, and might again 
assume the honourable position he had held in the congregation and in 
the town, when the spell by which he was held was destroyed. so grace 
yield to the notion herself, and encourage it in others, that loi 
barclay had bewitch both manasseh and prudence. and the consequence 
of this belief was, that loi was to be tried, with little chance in 
her favour, to see whether she was a witch or no; and if a witch, 
whether she would confess, implicate others, repent, and live a life of 
bitter shame, avoid by all men, and cruel treat by most; or die 
impenitent, hardened, deny her crime upon the gallows. 

and so they drag loi away from the congregation of christian to 
the gaol, to await her trial. i say 'dragged her,' because, although 
she was docile enough to have follow them whither they would, she was 
now so faint as to require extraneous force--poor lois! who should have 
been carry and tend loving in her state of exhaustion, but, 
instead, was so detest by the multitude, who look upon her as an 
accomplice of satan in all his evil doings, that they care no more how 
they treat her than a careless boy mind how he handle the toad that 
he is go to throw over the wall. 

when loi came to her full senses, she found herself lie on a short 
hard bed in a dark square room, which she at once knew must be a part 
of the city gaol. it was about eight feet square, it had stone wall on 
every side, and a grate opening high above her head, letting in all 
the light and air that could enter through about a square foot of 
aperture. it was so lonely, so dark to that poor girl, when she came 
slow and painful out of her long faint. she did so want human help 
in that struggle which always supervene after a swoon; when the effort 
is to clutch at life, and the effort seem too much for the will. she 
did not at first understand where she was; did not understand how she 
came to be there, nor did she care to understand. her physical instinct 
was to lie still and let the hurry pulse have time to calm. so she 
shut her eyes once more. slowly, slow the recollection of the scene 
in the meeting-house shaped itself into a kind of picture before her. 
she saw within her eyelids, as it were, that sea of loathing face all 
turn towards her, as towards something unclean and hateful. and you 
must remember, you who in the nineteenth century read this account, 
that witchcraft was a real terrible sin to her, loi barclay, two 
hundred years ago. the look on their faces, stamp on heart and brain, 
excited in her a sort of strange sympathy. could it, oh god!--could it 
be true, that satan had obtain the terrific power over her and her 
will, of which she had heard and read? could she indeed be possessed by 
a demon and be indeed a witch, and yet till now have been unconscious 
of it? and her excited imagination recalled, with singular vividness, 
all she had ever heard on the subject--the horrible midnight sacrament, 
the very presence and power of satan. then remember every angry 
thought against her neighbour, against the impertinence of prudence, 
against the overbearing authority of her aunt, against the persevering 
crazy suit of manasseh, the indignation--only that morning, but such 
age off in real time--at faith's injustice; oh, could such evil 
thought have had devilish power given to them by the father of evil, 
and, all unconscious to herself, have gone forth as active curse 
into the world? and so, on the idea went career wildly through the 
poor girl's brain--the girl thrown inward upon herself. at length, the 
sting of her imagination forced her to start up impatiently. what was 
this? a weight of iron on her legs--a weight state afterwards, by the 
gaoler of salem prison, to have been 'not more than eight pounds.' it 
was well for loi it was a tangible ill, bring her back from the 
wild illimitable desert in which her imagination was wandering. she 
took hold of the iron, and saw her torn stocking,--her bruise ankle, 
and began to cry pitifully, out of strange compassion with herself. 
they feared, then, that even in that cell she would find a way to 
escape. why, the utter, ridiculous impossible of the thing convinced 
her of her own innocence, and ignorance of all supernatural power; and 
the heavy iron brought her strange round from the delusion that 
seem to be gathering about her. 

no! she never could fly out of that deep dungeon; there was no escape, 
natural or supernatural, for her, unless by man's mercy. and what was 
man's mercy in such times of panic? loi knew that it was nothing; 
instinct more than reason taught her, that panic call out cowardice, 
and cowardice cruelty. yet she cried, cry freely, and for the first 
time, when she found herself iron and chained. it seem so cruel, so 
much as if her fellow-creatures had really learnt to hate and dread 
her--her, who had had a few angry thoughts, which god forgive! but 
whose thought had never gone into words, far less into actions. why, 
even now she could love all the household at home, if they would but 
let her; yes, even yet, though she felt that it was the open accusation 
of prudence and the withheld justification of her aunt and faith that 
had brought her to her present strait. would they ever come and see 
her? would kind thought of her,--who had share their daily bread 
for month and months,--bring them to see her, and ask her whether it 
were really she who had brought on the illness of prudence, the 
derange of manasseh's mind? 

no one came. bread and water were pushed in by some one, who hasty 
lock and unlock the door, and care not to see if he put them 
within his prisoner's reach, or perhaps thought that physical fact 
matter little to a witch. it was long before loi could reach them; 
and she had something of the natural hunger of youth left in her still, 
which prompt her, lie her length on the floor, to weary herself 
with effort to obtain the bread. after she had eaten some of it, the 
day began to wane, and she thought she would lay her down and try to 
sleep. but before she did so, the gaoler heard her singing the evening 
hymn: 

glory to thee, my god, this night, 
for all the blessing of the light. 

and a dull thought came into his dull mind, that she was thankful for 
few blessings, if she could tune up her voice to sing praises after 
this day of what, if she were a witch, was shameful detection in 
abominable practices, and if not--. well, his mind stop short at 
this point in his wonder contemplation. loi knelt down and said the 
lord's prayer, pause just a little before one clause, that she might 
be sure that in her heart of heart she did forgive. then she look at 
her ankle, and the tear came into her eyes once again, but not so much 
because she was hurt, as because men must have hate her so bitter 
before they could have treat her thus. then she lay down, and fell 
asleep. 

the next day, she was led before mr. hathorn and mr. curwin, justice 
of salem, to be accused legal and public of witchcraft. other were 
with her, under the same charge. and when the prisoner were brought 
in, they were cry out at by the abhorrent crowd. the two tappaus, 
prudence, and one or two other girl of the same age were there, in the 
character of victim of the spell of the accused. the prisoner were 
placed about seven or eight feet from the justice and the accuse 
between the justice and them; the former were then ordered to stand 
right before the justices. all this loi did at their bidding, with 
something of the wonder docile of a child, but not with any hope 
of soften the hard, stony look of detest that was on all the 
countenance around her, save those that were distort by more 
passionate anger. then an officer was bidden to hold each of her hands, 
and justice hathorn bade her keep her eyes continual fixed on him, 
for this reason--which, however, was not told to her--lest, if she 
look on prudence, the girl might either fall into a fit, or cry out 
that she was sudden and violent hurt. if any heart could have been 
touched of that cruel multitude, they would have felt some compassion 
for the sweet young face of the english girl, try so meek to do 
all that she was ordered, her face quite white, yet so full of sad 
gentleness, her grey eyes, a little dilate by the very solemnity of 
her position, fixed with the intent look of innocent maidenhood on the 
stern face of justice hathorn. and thus they stood in silence, one 
breathless minute. then they were bidden to say the lord's prayer. loi 
went through it as if alone in her cell; but, as she had done alone in 
her cell the night before, she made a little pause, before the prayer 
to be forgiven as she forgave. and at this instant of hesitation--as if 
they had been on the watch for it--they all cry out upon her for a 
witch, and when the clamour end the justice bade prudence hickson 
come forwards. then loi turn a little to one side, wish to see at 
least one familiar face; but when her eyes fell upon prudence, the girl 
stood stock-still, and answer no questions, nor spoke a word, and the 
justice declared that she was struck dumb by witchcraft. then some 
behind took prudence under the arms, and would have forced her forward 
to touch lois, possibly esteem that as a cure for her being 
bewitched. but prudence had hardly been made to take three steps before 
she struggle out of their arms, and fell down writhe as in a fit, 
calling out with shrieks, and entreat loi to help her, and save her 
from her torment. then all the girl began 'to tumble down like swine' 
(to use the words of an eye-witness) and to cry out upon loi and her 
fellow-prisoners. these last were now ordered to stand with their hand 
stretch out, it being imagine that if the body of the witch were 
arrange in the form of a cross they would lose their evil power. 
by-and-by loi felt her strength going, from the unwonted fatigue of 
such a position, which she had borne patient until the pain and 
weary had forced both tear and sweat down her face, and she ask 
in a low, plaintive voice, if she might not rest her head for a few 
moments against the wooden partition. but justice hathorn told her she 
had strength enough to torment others, and should have strength enough 
to stand. she sigh a little, and bore on, the clamour against her and 
the other accused increase every moment; the only way she could keep 
herself from utterly lose consciousness was by distract herself 
from present pain and danger, and saying to herself verse of the 
psalms as she could remember them, expressive of trust in god. at 
length she was ordered back to gaol, and dim understood that she and 
other were sentence to be hang for witchcraft. many people now 
look eager at lois, to see if she would weep at this doom. if she 
had had strength to cry, it might--it was just possible that it 
might--have been considered a plea in her favour, for witch could not 
shed tears, but she was too exhaust and dead. all she want was to 
lie down once more on her prison-bed, out of the reach of men's cries 
of abhorrence, and out of shot of their cruel eyes. so they led her 
back to prison, speechless and tearless. 

but rest gave her back her power of thought and suffering. was it, 
indeed, true that she was to die? she, loi barclay, only eighteen, so 
well, so young, so full of love and hope as she had been, till but 
these little days past! what would they think of it at home--real, dear 
home at barford, in england? there they had love her; there she had 
gone about, singing and rejoicing all the day long in the pleasant 
meadow by the avon side. oh, why did father and mother die, and leave 
her their bidding to come here to this cruel new england shore, where 
no one had want her, no one had care for her, and where now they 
were go to put her to a shameful death as a witch? and there would 
be no one to send kindly message by to those she should never see 
more. never more! young lucy was living, and joyful--probably thinking 
of her, and of his declared intention of coming to fetch her home to be 
his wife this very spring. possibly he had forgotten her; no one knew. 
a week before, she would have been indignant at her own distrust in 
thinking for a minute that he could forget. now, she doubt all men's 
goodness for a time; for those around her were deadly, and cruel, and 
relentless. 

then she turn round, and beat herself with angry blow (to speak in 
images), for ever doubt her lover. oh! if she were but with him! oh! 
if she might but be with him! he would not let her die; but would hide 
her in his bosom from the wrath of this people, and carry her back to 
the old home at barford. and he might even now be sailing on the wide 
blue sea, coming nearer, near every moment, and yet be too late after 
all. 

so the thought chase each other through her head all that feverish 
night, till she clung almost delirious to life, and wildly pray 
that she might not die; at least, not just yet, and she so young! 

pastor tappau and certain elder rouse her up from a heavy sleep, late 
on the morning of the following day. all night long she had tremble 
and cried, till morning had come peer in through the square grating 
up above. it soothe her, and she fell asleep, to be awakened, as i 
have said, by pastor tappau. 

'arise!' said he, scruple to touch her, from his superstitious idea 
of her evil powers. 'it is noonday.' 

'where am i?' said she, bewilder at this unusual wakening, and the 
array of severe face all gaze upon her with reprobation. 

'you are in salem gaol, condemn for a witch.' 

'alas! i had forgotten for an instant,' said she, drop her head 
upon her breast. 

'she has been out on a devilish ride all night long, doubtless, and is 
weary and perplexed this morning,' whisper one, in so low a voice 
that he did not think she could hear; but she lift up her eyes, and 
look at him, with mute reproach. 

'we are come' said pastor tappau, 'to exhort you to confess your great 
and manifold sin.' 

'my great and manifold sin!' repeated loi to herself, shake her 
head. 

'yea, your sin of witchcraft. if you will confess, there may yet be 
balm in gilead.' 

one of the elders, struck with pity at the young girl's wan, shrunken 
look, said, that if she confessed, and repented, and did penance, 
possibly her life might yet be spared. 

a sudden flash of light came into her sunk, dull eye. might she yet 
live? was it yet in her power? 

why, no one knew how soon ralph lucy might be here, to take her away 
for ever into the peace of a new home! life! oh, then, all hope was not 
over--perhaps she might still live, and not die. yet the truth came 
once more out of her lips, almost without any exercise of her will. 

'i am not a witch,' she said. 

then pastor tappau blindfold her, all unresisting, but with languid 
wonder in her heart as to what was to come next. she heard people enter 
the dungeon softly, and heard whisper voices; then her hand were 
lift up and made to touch some one near, and in an instant she heard 
a noise of struggling, and the well-known voice of prudence shriek 
out in one of her hysterical fits, and scream to be taken away and 
out of that place. it seem to loi as if some of her judge must have 
doubt of her guilt, and demand yet another test. she sat down 
heavy on her bed, thinking she must be in a horrible dream, so 
compass about with danger and enemy did she seem. those in the 
dungeon--and by the oppression of the air she perceive that they were 
many--kept on eager talk in low voices. she did not try to make out 
the sense of the fragment of sentence that reach her dull brain, 
till, all at once, a word or two made her understand they were 
discuss the desirable of apply the whip or the torture to 
make her confess, and reveal by what means the spell she had cast upon 
those whom she had bewitch could be dissolved. a thrill of affright 
ran through her; and she cry out, beseechingly: 

'i beg you, sirs, for god's mercy sake, that you do not use such awful 
means. i may say anything--nay, i may accuse any one if i am subject 
to such torment as i have heard tell about. for i am but a young girl, 
and not very brave, or very good, as some are.' 

it touched the heart of one or two to see her standing there; the 
tear stream down from below the coarse handkerchief tight bound 
over her eyes; the clank chain fastening the heavy weight to the 
slight ankle; the two hand held together as if to keep down a 
convulsive motion. 

'look!' said one of these. 'she is weeping. they say no witch can weep 
tears.' 

but another scoff at this test, and bade the first remember how those 
of her own family, the hickson even, bore witness against her. 

once more she was bidden to confess. the charges, esteem by all men 
(as they said) to have been proven against her, were read over to her, 
with all the testimony borne against her in proof thereof. they told 
her that, considering the godly family to which she belonged, it had 
been decided by the magistrate and minister of salem that he should 
have her life spared, if she would own her guilt, make reparation, and 
submit to penance; but that if not, she, and other convict of 
witchcraft along with her, were to be hung in salem market-place on the 
next thursday morning (thursday being market day). and when they had 
thus spoken, they wait silent for her answer. it was a minute or 
two before she spoke. she had sat down again upon the bed meanwhile, 
for indeed she was very weak. she asked, 'may i have this handkerchief 
unbound from my eyes, for indeed, sirs, it hurt me?' 

the occasion for which she was blindfold being over, the bandage was 
taken off, and she was allow to see. she look pitiful at the 
stern face around her, in grim suspense as to what her answer would 
be. then she spoke: 

'sirs, i must choose death with a quiet conscience, rather than life to 
be gain by a lie. i am not a witch. i know not hardly what you mean 
when you say i am. i have done many, many things very wrong in my life; 
but i think god will forgive me them for my saviour's sake.' 

'take not his name on your wicked lips,' said pastor tappau, enrage at 
her resolution of not confessing, and scarcely able to keep himself 
from striking her. she saw the desire he had, and shrank away in timid 
fear. then justice hathorn solemn read the legal condemnation of loi 
barclay to death by hanging, as a convict witch. she murmur 
something which nobody heard fully, but which sound like a prayer for 
pity and compassion on her tender years and friendless estate. then 
they left her to all the horrors of that solitary, loathsome dungeon, 
and the strange terror of approach death. 

outside the prison walls, the dread of the witches, and the excitement 
against witchcraft, grew with fearful rapidity. numbers of women, and 
men, too, were accused, no matter what their station of life and their 
former character had been. on the other side, it is allege that 
upwards of fifty person were grievous vex by the devil, and those 
to whom he had impart of his power for vile and wicked 
considerations. how much of malice, distinct, unmistakable personal 
malice, was mixed up with these accusations, no one can now tell. the 
dire statistics of this time tell us, that fifty-five escape death by 
confess themselves guilty, one hundred and fifty were in prison, 
more than two hundred accused, and upwards of twenty suffer death, 
among whom was the minister i have call nolan, who was traditional 
esteem to have suffer through hatred of his co-pastor. one old man, 
scorn the accusation, and refuse to plead at his trial, was, 
according to the law, pressed to death for his contumacy. nay, even 
dogs were accused of witchcraft, suffer the legal penalties, and are 
record among the subject of capital punishment. one young man found 
means to effect his mother's escape from confinement, flee with her on 
horseback, and secrete her in the blueberry swamp, not far from 
taplay's brook, in the great pasture; he conceal her here in a wigwam 
which he built for her shelter, provided her with food and clothing, 
and comfort and sustain her until after the delusion had pass 
away. the poor creature must, however, have suffer dreadfully, for 
one of her arms was fracture in the all but desperate effort of 
get her out of prison. 

but there was no one to try and save lois. grace hickson would fain 
have ignore her altogether. such a taint did witchcraft bring upon a 
whole family, that generation of blameless life were not at that day 
esteem sufficient to wash it out. besides, you must remember that 
grace, along with most people of her time, believe most firm in the 
reality of the crime of witchcraft. poor, forsaken lois, believe in it 
herself, and it add to her terror, for the gaoler, in an unusually 
communicative mood, told her that nearly every cell was now full of 
witches; and it was possible he might have to put one, if more came, in 
with her. loi knew that she was no witch herself; but not the less did 
she believe that the crime was abroad, and largely share in by 
evil-minded person who had chosen to give up their soul to satan; and 
she shudder with terror at what the gaoler said, and would have ask 
him to spare her this companionship if it were possible. but, somehow, 
her senses were leave her, and she could not remember the right words 
in which to form her request, until he had left the place. 

the only person who yearn after lois--who would have befriend her 
if he could--was manasseh: poor, mad manasseh. but he was so wild and 
outrageous in his talk, that it was all his mother could do to keep his 
state conceal from public observation. she had for this purpose given 
him a sleep potion; and, while he lay heavy and inert under the 
influence of the poppy-tea, his mother bound him with cords to the 
ponderous, antique bed in which he slept. she look broken-hearted 
while she did this office, and thus acknowledge the degrade of her 
first-born--him of whom she had ever been so proud. 

late that evening, grace hickson stood in lois's cell, hooded and 
cloak up to her eyes. loi was sitting quite still, play idle with 
a bit of string which one of the magistrate had drop out of his 
pocket that morning. her aunt was standing by her for an instant or two 
in silence, before loi seem aware of her presence. sudden she 
look up, and utter a little cry, shrink away from the dark 
figure. then, as if her cry had loosen grace's tongue, she began: 

'lois barclay, did i ever do you any harm?' grace did not know how 
often her want of loving-kindness had pierce the tender heart of the 
stranger under her roof; nor did loi remember it against her now. 
instead, lois's memory was fill with grateful thought of how much 
that might have been left undone, by a less conscientious person, her 
aunt had done for her, and she half stretch out her arms as to a 
friend in that desolate place, while she answered: 

'oh no, no you were very good! very kind!' 

but grace stood immovable. 

'i did you no harm, although i never rightly knew why you came to us.' 

'i was sent by my mother on her death-bed,' moan lois, covering her 
face. it grew dark every instant. her aunt stood, still and silent. 

'did any of mine ever wrong you?' she asked, after a time. 

'no, no; never, till prudence said--oh, aunt, do you think i am a 
witch?' and now loi was standing up, holding by grace's cloak, and 
try to read her face. grace drew herself, ever so little, away from 
the girl, whom she dreaded, and yet sought to propitiate. 

'wiser than i, godly than i, have said it. but oh, lois, lois! he was 
my first-born. loose him from the demon, for the sake of him whose name 
i dare not name in this terrible building, fill with them who have 
renounce the hope of their baptism; loose manasseh from his awful 
state, if ever i or mine did you a kindness!' 

'you ask me for christ's sake,' said lois. 'i can name that holy 
name--for oh, aunt! indeed, and in holy truth, i am no witch; and yet i 
am to die--to be hanged! aunt, do not let them kill me! i am so young, 
and i never did any one any harm that i know of.' 

'hush! for very shame! this afternoon i have bound my first-born with 
strong cords, to keep him from do himself or us a mischief--he is so 
frenzied. loi barclay, look here!' and grace knelt down at her niece's 
feet, and join her hand as if in prayer--'i am a proud woman, god 
forgive me! and i never thought to kneel to any save to him. and now i 
kneel at your feet, to pray you to release my children, more especially 
my son manasseh, from the spell you have put upon them. lois, hearken 
to me, and i will pray to the almighty for you, if yet there may be 
mercy.' 

'i cannot do it; i never did you or yours any wrong. how can i undo it? 
how can i?' and she wrung her hand in intensity of conviction of the 
inutle of aught she could do. 

here grace got up, slowly, stiffly, and sternly. she stood aloof from 
the chain girl, in the remote corner of the prison cell near the 
door, ready to make her escape as soon as she had cursed the witch, who 
would not, or could not, undo the evil she had wrought. grace lift up 
her right hand, and held it up on high, as she doom loi to be 
accursed for ever, for her deadly sin, and her want of mercy even at 
this final hour. and, lastly, she summon her to meet her at the 
judgment-seat, and answer for this deadly injury done to both soul and 
body of those who had taken her in, and received her when she came to 
them an orphan and a stranger. 

until this last summons, loi had stood as one who hear her sentence 
and can say nothing against it, for she know all would be in vain. but 
she raise her head when she heard her aunt speak of the judgment-seat, 
and at the end of grace's speech she, too, lift up her right hand, as 
if solemn pledge herself by that action, and replied: 

'aunt! i will meet you there. and there you will know my innocence of 
this deadly thing. god have mercy on you and yours!' 

her calm voice madden grace, and making a gesture as if she pluck 
up a handful of dust of the floor, and threw it at lois, she cried: 

'witch! witch! ask mercy for thyself--i need not your prayers. witches' 
prayer are read backwards. i spit at thee, and defy thee!' and so she 
went away. 

loi sat moan that whole night through. 'god comfort me! god 
strengthen me!' was all she could remember to say. she just felt that 
want, nothing more,--all other fear and wants seem dead within her. 
and when the gaoler brought in her breakfast the next morning, he 
report her as 'gone silly;' for, indeed, she did not seem to know 
him, but kept rock herself to and fro, and whisper soft to 
herself, smile a little from time to time. 

but god did comfort her, and strengthen her too late on that wednesday 
afternoon, they thrust another 'witch' into her cell, bidding the two, 
with opprobrious words, keep company together. the new comer fell 
prostrate with the push given her from without; and lois, not 
recognize anything but an old ragged woman lie helpless on her face 
on the ground, lift her up; and lo! it was nattee--dirty, filthy 
indeed, mud-pelted, stone-bruised, beaten, and all astray in her wit 
with the treatment she had received from the mob outside. loi held her 
in her arms, and soft wipe the old brown wrinkle face with her 
apron, crying over it, as she had hardly yet cry over her own 
sorrows. for hour she tend the old indian woman--tended her bodily 
woes; and as the poor scattered senses of the savage creature came 
slow back, loi gather her infinite dread of the morrow, when she 
too, as well as lois, was to be led out to die, in face of all that 
infuriate crowd. loi sought in her own mind for some source of 
comfort for the old woman, who shook like one in the shake palsy at 
the dread of death--and such a death. 

when all was quiet through the prison, in the deep dead midnight, the 
gaoler outside the door heard loi telling, as if to a young child, the 
marvellous and sorrowful story of one who die on the cross for us and 
for our sakes. as long as she spoke, the indian woman's terror seem 
lulled; but the instant she paused, for weariness, nattee cry out 
afresh, as if some wild beast were following her close through the 
dense forest in which she had dwelt in her youth. and then loi went 
on, saying all the blessed words she could remember, and comfort the 
helpless indian woman with the sense of the presence of a heavenly 
friend. and in comfort her, loi was comforted; in strengthen 
her, loi was strengthened. 

the morning came, and the summons to come forth and die came. they who 
enter the cell found loi asleep, her face rest on the slumber 
old woman, whose head she still held in her lap. she did not seem 
clearly to recognize where she was, when she awakened; the 'silly' look 
had return to her wan face; all she appear to know was, that 
somehow or another, through some peril or another, she had to protect 
the poor indian woman. she smile faint when she saw the bright light 
of the april day; and put her arm round nattee, and try to keep the 
indian quiet with hushing, soothe words of broken meaning, and holy 
fragment of the psalms. nattee tighten her hold upon loi as they 
drew near the gallows, and the outrageous crowd below began to hoot and 
yell. loi redouble her effort to calm and encourage nattee, 
apparently unconscious that any of the opprobrium, the hootings, the 
stones, the mud, was direct towards her herself. but when they took 
nattee from her arms, and led her out to suffer first, loi seem all 
at once to recover her sense of the present terror. she gaze wildly 
around, stretch out her arms as if to some person in the distance, 
who was yet visible to her, and cry out once with a voice that 
thrill through all who heard it, 'mother!' directly afterwards, the 
body of loi the witch swung in the air, and every one stood, with 
hush breath, with a sudden wonder, like a fear of deadly crime, 
fallen upon them. 

the still and the silence were broken by one craze and mad, who 
came rush up the steps of the ladder, and caught lois's body in his 
arms, and kiss her lip with wild passion. and then, as if it were 
true what the people believed, that he was possessed by a demon, he 
sprang down, and rush through the crowd, out of the bounds of the 
city, and into the dark dense forest, and manasseh hickson was no more 
seen of christian man. 

the people of salem had awaken from their frightful delusion before 
the autumn, when captain holdernesse and ralph lucy came to find out 
lois, and bring her home to peaceful barford, in the pleasant country 
of england. instead, they led them to the grassy grave where she lay at 
rest, done to death by mistaken men. ralph lucy shook the dust off his 
feet in quit salem, with a heavy, heavy heart; and live a bachelor 
all his life long for her sake. 

long years afterwards, captain holdernesse sought him out, to tell him 
some news that he thought might interest the grave miller of the 
avonside. captain holdernesse told him that in the previous year, it 
was then 1713, the sentence of excommunication against the witch of 
salem was ordered, in godly sacramental meeting of the church, to be 
erase and blot out, and that those who met together for this 
purpose 'humbly request the merciful god would pardon whatsoever sin, 
error, or mistake was in the application of justice, through our 
merciful high priest, who knoweth how to have compassion on the 
ignorant, and those that are out of the way.' he also said that 
prudence hickson--now woman grown--had made a most touching and pungent 
declaration of sorrow and repentance before the whole church, for the 
false and mistaken testimony she had given in several instances, among 
which she particularly mention that of her cousin loi barclay. to 
all which ralph lucy only answered: 

'no repentance of theirs can bring her back to life.' 

then captain holdernesse took out a paper, and read the following 
humble and solemn declaration of regret on the part of those who sign 
it, among whom grace hickson was one: 

'we, whose name are undersigned, being, in the year 1692, call 
to serve as juror in court of salem, on trial of many who were by 
some suspect guilty of do acts of witchcraft upon the body 
of sundry persons; we confess that we ourselves were not capable to 
understand, nor able to withstand, the mysterious delusion of the 
powers of darkness, and prince of the air, but were, for want of 
knowledge in ourselves, and better information from others, 
prevail with to take up with such evidence against the accused, 
as, on further consideration, and better information, we just 
fear was insufficient for the touching the lives of any (deut. 
xvii. 6), whereby we fear we have been instrumental, with others, 
though ignorant and unwittingly, to bring upon ourselves and this 
people of the lord the guilt of innocent blood; which sin, the lord 
saith in scripture, he would not pardon (2 kings, xxiv. 4), that 
is, we suppose, in regard of his temporal judgments. we do, 
therefore, signify to all in general (and to the survive 
sufferer in special) our deep sense of, and sorrow for, our 
errors, in acting on such evidence to the condemn of any person; 
and do hereby declare, that we just fear that we were sadly 
delude and mistaken, for which we are much disquiet and 
distress in our minds, and do therefore humble beg forgiveness, 
first of god for christ's sake, for this our error; and pray that 
god would not impute the guilt of it to ourselves nor others; and 
we also pray that we may be considered candidly and aright by the 
living sufferers, as being then under the power of a strong and 
general delusion, utterly unacquainted with, and not experienced 
in, matter of that nature. 

'we do heartily ask forgiveness of you all, whom we have just 
offended; and do declare, according to our present minds, we would 
none of us do such things again on such grounds for the whole 
world; pray you to accept of this in way of satisfaction for our 
offence, and that you would bless the inheritance of the lord, that 
he may be entreat for the land. 

'foreman, thomas fisk, &c.' 

to the reading of this paper ralph lucy made no reply save this, even 
more gloomy than before: 

'all their repentance will avail nothing to my lois, nor will it bring 
back her life.' 

then captain holdernesse spoke once more, and said that on the day of 
the general fast, appoint to be held all through new england, when 
the meeting-houses were crowded, an old, old man with white hair had 
stood up in the place in which he was accustomed to worship, and had 
hand up into the pulpit a written confession, which he had once or 
twice essay to read for himself, acknowledge his great and grievous 
error in the matter of the witch of salem, and pray for the 
forgiveness of god and of his people, ending with an entreaty that all 
then present would join with him in prayer that his past conduct might 
not bring down the displeasure of the most high upon his country, his 
family, or himself. that old man, who was no other than justice sewall, 
remain standing all the time that his confession was read; and at the 
end he said, 'the good and gracious god be pleased to save new england 
and me and my family.' and then it came out that, for years past, judge 
sewall had set apart a day for humiliate and prayer, to keep fresh in 
his mind a sense of repentance and sorrow for the part he had borne in 
these trials, and that this solemn anniversary he was pledge to keep 
as long as he lived, to show his feeling of deep humiliation. 

ralph lucy's voice tremble as he spoke: 

'all this will not bring my loi to life again, or give me back the 
hope of my youth.' 

but--as captain holdernesse shook his head (for what word could he say, 
or how dispute what was so evidently true?)--ralph added, 'what is the 
day, know you, that this justice has set apart?' 

'the twenty-ninth of april.' 

'then on that day will i, here at barford in england, join my prayer as 
long as i live with the repentant judge, that his sin may be blot 
out and no more had in remembrance. she would have will it so.' 




the grey woman 

portion 1 


there is a mill by the neckar-side, to which many people resort for 
coffee, according to the fashion which is almost national in germany. 
there is nothing particularly attractive in the situation of this mill; 
it is on the mannheim (the flat and unromantic) side of heidelberg. the 
river turn the mill-wheel with a plenteous gushing sound; the 
out-buildings and the dwelling-house of the miller form a well-kept 
dusty quadrangle. again, further from the river, there is a garden full 
of willows, and arbours, and flower-beds not well kept, but very 
profuse in flower and luxuriant creepers, knot and loop the 
arbour together. in each of these arbour is a stationary table of 
white paint wood, and light moveable chair of the same colour and 
material. 

i went to drink coffee there with some friends in 184--. the stately 
old miller came out to greet us, as some of the party were known to him 
of old. he was of a grand build of a man, and his loud musical voice, 
with its tone friendly and familiar, his rolling laugh of welcome, went 
well with the keen bright eye, the fine cloth of his coat, and the 
general look of substance about the place. poultry of all kind 
abound in the mill-yard, where there were ample means of livelihood 
for them strew on the ground; but not content with this, the miller 
took out handful of corn from the sacks, and threw liberally to the 
cock and hen that ran almost under his feet in their eagerness. and 
all the time he was do this, as it were habitually, he was talk 
to us, and ever and anon calling to his daughter and the serving-maids, 
to bid them hasten the coffee we had ordered. he follow us to an 
arbour, and saw us serve to his satisfaction with the best of 
everything we could ask for; and then left us to go round to the 
different arbour and see that each party was properly attend to; 
and, as he went, this great, prosperous, happy-looking man whistle 
soft one of the most plaintive airs i ever heard. 

'his family have held this mill ever since the old palatinate days; or 
rather, i should say, have possessed the ground ever since then, for 
two successive mill of theirs have been burnt down by the french. if 
you want to see scherer in a passion, just talk to him of the 
possibility of a france invasion.' 

but at this moment, still whistle that mournful air, we saw the 
miller go down the steps that led from the somewhat raise garden 
into the mill-yard; and so i seem to have lost my chance of putt 
him in a passion. 

we had nearly finished our coffee, and our 'kucken,' and our cinnamon 
cake, when heavy splash fell on our thick leafy covering; quick and 
quick they came, coming through the tender leaves as if they were 
tear them asunder; all the people in the garden were hurry under 
shelter, or seek for their carriage standing outside. up the steps 
the miller came hastening, with a crimson umbrella, fit to cover every 
one left in the garden, and follow by his daughter, and one or two 
maidens, each bearing an umbrella. 

'come into the house--come in, i say. it is a summer-storm, and will 
flood the place for an hour or two, till the river carry it away. 
here, here.' 

and we follow him back into his own house. we went into the kitchen 
first. such an array of bright copper and tin vessel i never saw; and 
all the wooden things were as thorough scoured. the red tile floor 
was spotless when we went in, but in two minutes it was all over slop 
and dirt with the tread of many feet; for the kitchen was filled, and 
still the worthy miller kept bring in more people under his great 
crimson umbrella. he even call the dogs in, and made them lie down 
under the tables. 

his daughter said something to him in german, and he shook his head 
merry at her. everybody laughed. 

'what did she say?' i asked. 

'she told him to bring the ducks in next; but indeed if more people 
come we shall be suffocated. what with the thundery weather, and the 
stove, and all these steam clothes, i really think we must ask leave 
to pass on. perhaps we might go in and see frau scherer.' 

my friend ask the daughter of the house for permission to go into an 
inner chamber and see her mother. it was granted, and we went into a 
sort of saloon, over-looking the neckar; very small, very bright, and 
very close. the floor was slippery with polish; long narrow pieces of 
looking-glass against the wall reflect the perpetual motion of the 
river opposite; a white porcelain stove, with some old-fashioned 
ornament of brass about it; a sofa, cover with utrecht velvet, a 
table before it, and a piece of worsted-worked carpet under it; a vase 
of artificial flowers; and, lastly, an alcove with a bed in it, on 
which lay the paralyse wife of the good miller, knitting busily, 
form the furniture. i spoke as if this was all that was to be seen in 
the room; but, sitting quietly, while my friend kept up a brisk 
conversation in a language which i but half understood, my eye was 
caught by a picture in a dark corner of the room, and i got up to 
examine it more nearly. 

it was that of a young girl of extreme beauty; evidently of middle 
rank. there was a sensitive refinement in her face, as if she almost 
shrank from the gaze which, of necessity, the painter must have fixed 
upon her. it was not over-well painted, but i felt that it must have 
been a good likeness, from this strong impress of peculiar character 
which i have try to describe. from the dress, i should guess it to 
have been paint in the latter half of the last century. and i 
afterwards heard that i was right. 

there was a little pause in the conversation. 

'will you ask frau scherer who this is?' 

my friend repeated my question, and received a long reply in german. 
then she turn round and translate it to me. 

'it is the likeness of a great-aunt of her husband's.' (my friend was 
standing by me, and look at the picture with sympathetic curiosity.) 
'see! here is the name on the open page of this bible, "anna scherer, 
1778." frau scherer say there is a tradition in the family that this 
pretty girl, with her complexion of lily and roses, lost her colour 
so entire through fright, that she was known by the name of the grey 
woman. she speak as if this anna scherer live in some state of 
life-long terror. but she do not know details; refer me to her 
husband for them. she think he has some papers which were written by 
the original of that picture for her daughter, who die in this very 
house not long after our friend there was married. we can ask herr 
scherer for the whole story if you like.' 

'oh yes, pray do!' said i. and, as our host came in at this moment to 
ask how we were faring, and to tell us that he had sent to heidelberg 
for carriage to convey us home, seeing no chance of the heavy rain 
abating, my friend, after thank him, pass on to my request. 

'ah!' said he, his face changing, 'the aunt anna had a sad history. it 
was all owe to one of those hellish frenchmen; and her daughter 
suffer for it--the cousin ursula, as we all call her when i was a 
child. to be sure, the good cousin ursula was his child as well. the 
sin of the father are visit on their children. the lady would like 
to know all about it, would she? well, there are papers--a kind of 
apology the aunt anna wrote for putt an end to her daughter's 
engagement--or rather fact which she revealed, that prevent cousin 
ursula from marry the man she loved; and so she would never have any 
other good fellow, else i have heard say my father would have been 
thankful to have made her his wife.' all this time he was rummage in 
the drawer of an old-fashioned bureau, and now he turn round, with a 
bundle of yellow mss. in his hand, which he gave to my friend, saying, 
'take it home, take it home, and if you care to make out our crabbed 
germany writing, you may keep it as long as you like, and read it at 
your leisure. only i must have it back again when you have done with 
it, that's all.' 

and so we became possessed of the manuscript of the following letter, 
which it was our employment, during many a long evening that ensue 
winter, to translate, and in some parts to abbreviate. the letter began 
with some reference to the pain which she had already inflict upon 
her daughter by some unexplained opposition to a project of marriage; 
but i doubt if, without the clue with which the good miller had 
furnish us, we could have made out even this much from the 
passionate, broken sentence that made us fancy that some scene between 
the mother and daughter--and possibly a third person--had occur just 
before the mother had begun to write. 

* * * * * 

'thou dost not love thy child, mother! thou dost not care if her heart 
is broken!' ah, god! and these words of my heart-beloved ursula ring in 
my ear as if the sound of them would fill them when i lie a-dying. and 
her poor tear-stained face come between me and everything else. child! 
heart do not break; life is very tough as well as very terrible. but i 
will not decide for thee. i will tell thee all; and thou shalt bear the 
burden of choice. i may be wrong; i have little wit left, and never had 
much, i think; but an instinct serve me in place of judgement, and 
that instinct tell me that thou and thy henri must never be married. 
yet i may be in error. i would fain make my child happy. lay this paper 
before the good priest schriesheim; if, after reading it, thou hast 
doubt which make thee uncertain. only i will tell thee all now, on 
condition that no spoken word ever pass between us on the subject. it 
would kill me to be questioned. i should have to see all present again. 

my father held, as thou knowest, the mill on the neckar, where thy 
new-found uncle, scherer, now lives. thou rememberest the surprise with 
which we were received there last vintage twelvemonth. how thy uncle 
disbelieve me when i said that i was his sister anna, whom he had long 
believe to be dead, and how i had to lead thee underneath the picture, 
paint of me long ago, and point out, feature by feature, the likeness 
between it and thee; and how, as i spoke, i recall first to my own 
mind, and then by speech to his, the detail of the time when it was 
painted; the merry words that pass between us then, a happy boy and 
girl; the position of the articles of furniture in the room; our 
father's habits; the cherry-tree, now cut down, that shade the window 
of my bedroom, through which my brother was wont to squeeze himself, in 
order to spring on to the topmost bough that would bear his weight; and 
thence would pass me back his cap laden with fruit to where i sat on 
the window-sill, too sick with fright for him to care much for eat 
the cherries. 

and at length fritz gave way, and believe me to be his sister anna, 
even as though i were risen from the dead. and thou rememberest how he 
fetch in his wife, and told her that i was not dead, but was come 
back to the old home once more, change as i was. and she would scarce 
believe him, and scan me with a cold, distrustful eye, till at 
length--for i knew her of old as babette müller--i said that i was 
well-to-do, and need not to seek out friends for what they had to 
give. and then she asked--not me, but her husband--why i had kept 
silent so long, leading all--father, brother, every one that love me 
in my own dear home--to esteem me dead. and then thine uncle (thou 
rememberest?) said he care not to know more than i care to tell; that 
i was his anna, found again, to be a blessing to him in his old age, as 
i had been in his boyhood. i thank him in my heart for his trust; for 
were the need for telling all less than it seem to me now i could not 
speak of my past life. but she, who was my sister-in-law still, held 
back her welcome, and, for want of that, i did not go to live in 
heidelberg as i had plan beforehand, in order to be near my brother 
fritz, but contented myself with his promise to be a father to my 
ursula when i should die and leave this weary world. 

that babette müller was, as i may say, the cause of all my life's 
suffering. she was a baker's daughter in heidelberg--a great beauty, as 
people said, and, indeed, as i could see for myself i, too--thou sawest 
my picture--was reckon a beauty, and i believe i was so. babette 
müller look upon me as a rival. she like to be admired, and had no 
one much to love her. i had several people to love me--thy grandfather, 
fritz, the old servant kätchen, karl, the head apprentice at the 
mill--and i fear admiration and notice, and the being stare at as 
the 'schöne müllerin,' whenever i went to make my purchase in 
heidelberg. 

those were happy, peaceful days. i had kätchen to help me in the 
housework, and whatever we did pleased my brave old father, who was 
always gentle and indulgent towards us women, though he was stern 
enough with the apprentice in the mill. karl, the oldest of these, was 
his favourite; and i can see now that my father wish him to marry me, 
and that karl himself was desirous to do so. but karl was rough-spoken, 
and passionate--not with me, but with the others--and i shrank from him 
in a way which, i fear, gave him pain. and then came thy uncle fritz's 
marriage; and babette was brought to the mill to be its mistress. not 
that i care much for give up my post, for, in spite of my father's 
great kindness, i always fear that i did not manage well for so large 
a family (with the men, and a girl under kätchen, we sat down eleven 
each night to supper). but when babette began to find fault with 
kätchen, i was unhappy at the blame that fell on faithful servants; and 
by-and-by i began to see that babette was egg on karl to make more 
open love to me, and, as she once said, to get done with it, and take 
me off to a home of my own. my father was grow old, and did not 
perceive all my daily discomfort. the more karl advanced, the more i 
dislike him. he was good in the main, but i had no notion of being 
married, and could not bear any one who talk to me about it. 

things were in this way when i had an invitation to go to carlsruhe to 
visit a schoolfellow, of whom i had been very fond. babette was all for 
my going; i don't think i want to leave home, and yet i had been very 
fond of sophie rupprecht. but i was always shy among strangers. somehow 
the affair was settled for me, but not until both fritz and my father 
had made inquiry as to the character and position of the rupprechts. 
they learned that the father had held some kind of inferior position 
about the grand-duke's court, and was now dead, leave a widow, a 
noble lady, and two daughters, the elder of whom was sophie, my friend. 
madame rupprecht was not rich, but more than respectable--genteel. when 
this was ascertained, my father made no opposition to my going; babette 
forward it by all the means in her power, and even my dear fritz had 
his word to say in its favour. only kätchen was against it--kätchen and 
karl. the opposition of karl did more to send me to carlsruhe than 
anything. for i could have object to go; but when he took upon 
himself to ask what was the good of go a-gadding, visiting stranger 
of whom no one knew anything, i yield to circumstances--to the 
pull of sophie and the push of babette. i was silent vexed, i 
remember, at babette's inspection of my clothes; at the way in which 
she settled that this gown was too old-fashioned, or that too common, 
to go with me on my visit to a noble lady; and at the way in which she 
took upon herself to spend the money my father had given me to buy what 
was requisite for the occasion. and yet i blame myself, for every one 
else thought her so kind for do all this; and she herself meant 
kindly, too. 

at last i quit the mill by the neckar-side. it was a long day's 
journey, and fritz went with me to carlsruhe. the rupprecht live on 
the third floor of a house a little behind one of the principal 
streets, in a cramped-up court, to which we gain admittance through a 
doorway in the street. i remember how pinched their rooms look after 
the large space we had at the mill, and yet they had an air of grandeur 
about them which was new to me, and which gave me pleasure, fade as 
some of it was. madame rupprecht was too formal a lady for me; i was 
never at my ease with her; but sophie was all that i had recollect 
her at school: kind, affectionate, and only rather too ready with her 
expression of admiration and regard. the little sister kept out of our 
way; and that was all we needed, in the first enthusiast renewal of 
our early friendship. the one great object of madame rupprecht's life 
was to retain her position in society; and as her means were much 
diminish since her husband's death, there was not much comfort, 
though there was a great deal of show, in their way of living; just the 
opposite of what it was at my father's house. i believe that my coming 
was not too much desire by madame rupprecht, as i brought with me 
another mouth to be fed; but sophie had spent a year or more in 
entreat for permission to invite me, and her mother, have once 
consented, was too well bred not to give me a stately welcome. 

the life in carlsruhe was very different from what it was at home. the 
hour were later, the coffee was weak in the morning, the pottage was 
weaker, the boil beef less relieved by other diet, the dress finer, 
the evening engagement constant. i did not find these visit pleasant. 
we might not knit, which would have relieved the tedium a little; but 
we sat in a circle, talk together, only interrupt occasional by 
a gentleman, who, break out of the knot of men who stood near the 
door, talk eager together, stole across the room on tiptoe, his 
hat under his arm, and, bring his feet together in the position we 
call the first at the dancing-school, made a low bow to the lady he 
was go to address. the first time i saw these manners i could not 
help smiling; but madame rupprecht saw me, and spoke to me next morning 
rather severely, telling me that, of course, in my country breeding i 
could have seen nothing of court manners, or france fashions, but that 
that was no reason for my laugh at them. of course i try never to 
smile again in company. this visit to carlsruhe took place in '89, just 
when every one was full of the event taking place at paris; and yet at 
carlsruhe france fashion were more talk of than france politics. 
madame rupprecht, especially, thought a great deal of all france 
people. and this again was quite different to us at home. fritz could 
hardly bear the name of a frenchman; and it had nearly been an obstacle 
to my visit to sophie that her mother prefer being call madame to 
her proper title of frau. 

one night i was sitting next to sophie, and longing for the time when 
we might have supper and go home, so as to be able to speak together, a 
thing forbidden by madame rupprecht's rule of etiquette, which 
strict prohibit any but the most necessary conversation passing 
between member of the same family when in society. i was sitting, i 
say, scarcely keeping back my inclination to yawn, when two gentlemen 
came in, one of whom was evidently a stranger to the whole party, from 
the formal manner in which the host led him up, and present him to 
the hostess. i thought i had never seen any one so handsome or so 
elegant. his hair was powdered, of course, but one could see from his 
complexion that it was fair in its natural state. his features were as 
delicate as a girl's, and set off by two little 'mouches,' as we call 
patch in those days, one at the left corner of his mouth, the other 
prolonging, as it were, the right eye. his dress was blue and silver. i 
was so lost in admiration of this beautiful young man, that i was as 
much surprise as if the angel gabriel had spoken to me, when the lady 
of the house brought him forward to present him to me. she call him 
monsieur de la tourelle, and he began to speak to me in french; but 
though i understood him perfectly, i dare not trust myself to reply to 
him in that language. then he try german, speak it with a kind of 
soft lisp that i thought charming. but, before the end of the evening, 
i became a little tired of the affected soft and effeminacy of his 
manners, and the exaggerate compliments he paid me, which had the 
effect of making all the company turn round and look at me. madame 
rupprecht was, however, pleased with the precise thing that displease 
me. she like either sophie or me to create a sensation; of course she 
would have prefer that it should have been her daughter, but her 
daughter's friend was next best. as we went away, i heard madame 
rupprecht and monsieur de la tourelle reciprocate civil speech with 
might and main, from which i found out that the france gentleman was 
coming to call on us the next day. i do not know whether i was more 
glad or frightened, for i had been kept upon stilt of good manners all 
the evening. but still i was flatter when madame rupprecht spoke as 
if she had invite him, because he had shown pleasure in my society, 
and even more gratify by sophie's ungrudging delight at the evident 
interest i had excited in so fine and agreeable a gentleman. yet, with 
all this, they had hard work to keep me from running out of the salon 
the next day, when we heard his voice inquiring at the gate on the 
stairs for madame rupprecht. they had made me put on my sunday gown, 
and they themselves were dress as for a reception. 

when he was gone away, madame rupprecht congratulate me on the 
conquest i had made; for, indeed, he had scarcely spoken to anyone 
else, beyond what mere civility required, and had almost invite 
himself to come in the evening to bring some new song, which was all 
the fashion in paris, he said. madame rupprecht had been out all 
morning, as she told me, to glean information about monsieur de la 
tourelle. he was a propriétaire, had a small château on the vosge 
mountains; he own land there, but had a large income from some 
source quite independent of this property. altogether, he was a good 
match, as she emphatically observed. she never seem to think that i 
could refuse him after this account of his wealth, nor do i believe she 
would have allow sophie a choice, even had he been as old and ugly as 
he was young and handsome. i do not quite know--so many event have 
come to pass since then, and blur the clear of my 
recollections--if i love him or not. he was very much devoted to me; 
he almost frightened me by the excess of his demonstration of love. 
and he was very charming to everybody around me, who all spoke of him 
as the most fascinating of men, and of me as the most fortunate of 
girls. and yet i never felt quite at my ease with him. i was always 
relieved when his visit were over, although i miss his presence when 
he did not come. he prolonged his visit to the friend with whom he was 
stay at carlsruhe, on purpose to woo me. he loaded me with presents, 
which i was unwill to take, only madame rupprecht seem to consider 
me an affected prude if i refuse them. many of these presents 
consist of articles of valuable old jewellery, evidently belong to 
his family; by accept these i double the tie which were form 
around me by circumstances even more than by my own consent. in those 
days we did not write letters to absent friends as frequent as is 
done now, and i had been unwill to name him in the few letters that 
i wrote home. at length, however, i learned from madame rupprecht that 
she had written to my father to announce the splendid conquest i had 
made, and to request his presence at my betrothal. i start with 
astonishment. i had not realize that affair had gone so far as this. 
but when she ask me, in a stern, offend manner, what i had meant by 
my conduct if i did not intend to marry monsieur de la tourelle--i had 
received his visits, his presents, all his various advances without 
showing any unwilling or repugnance--(and it was all true; i had 
shown no repugnance, though i did not wish to be married to him,--at 
least, not so soon)--what could i do but hang my head, and silent 
consent to the rapid enunciation of the only course which now remain 
for me if i would not be esteem a heartless coquette all the rest of 
my days? 

there was some difficulty, which i afterwards learnt that my 
sister-in-law had obviated, about my betrothal taking place from home. 
my father, and fritz especially, were for have me return to the mill, 
and there be betrothed, and from thence be married. but the rupprecht 
and monsieur de la tourelle were equally urgent on the other side; and 
babette was unwill to have the trouble of the commotion at the mill; 
and also, i think, a little dislike the idea of the contrast of my 
grand marriage with her own. 

so my father and fritz came over to the betrothal. they were to stay at 
an inn in carlsruhe for a fortnight, at the end of which time the 
marriage was to take place. monsieur de la tourelle told me he had 
business at home, which would oblige him to be absent during the 
interval between the two events; and i was very glad of it, for i did 
not think that he value my father and my brother as i could have 
wish him to do. he was very polite to them; put on all the soft, 
grand manner, which he had rather drop with me; and compliment us 
all round, beginning with my father and madame rupprecht, and ending 
with little alwina. but he a little scoff at the old-fashioned church 
ceremony which my father insist on; and i fancy fritz must have 
taken some of his compliments as satire, for i saw certain sign of 
manner by which i knew that my future husband, for all his civil words, 
had irritate and annoy my brother. but all the money arrangement 
were liberal in the extreme, and more than satisfied, almost surprised, 
my father. even fritz lift up his eyebrow and whistled. i alone did 
not care about anything. i was bewitched,--in a dream,--a kind of 
despair. i had got into a net through my own timid and weakness, and 
i did not see how to get out of it. i clung to my own home-people that 
fortnight as i had never done before. their voices, their ways were all 
so pleasant and familiar to me, after the constraint in which i had 
been living. i might speak and do as i like without being correct by 
madame rupprecht, or reprove in a delicate, complimentary way by 
monsieur de la tourelle. one day i said to my father that i did not 
want to be married, that i would rather go back to the dear old mill; 
but he seem to feel this speech of mine as a dereliction of duty as 
great as if i had committed perjury; as if, after the ceremony of 
betrothal, no one had any right over me but my future husband. and yet 
he ask me some solemn questions; but my answer were not such as to 
do me any good. 

'dost thou know any fault or crime in this man that should prevent 
god's blessing from rest on thy marriage with him? dost thou feel 
aversion or repugnance to him in any way?' 

and to all this what could i say? i could only stammer out that i did 
not think i love him enough; and my poor old father saw in this 
reluctance only the fancy of a silly girl who did not know her own 
mind, but who had now gone too far to recede. 

so we were married, in the court chapel, a privilege which madame 
rupprecht had use no end of effort to obtain for us, and which she 
must have thought was to secure us all possible happiness, both at the
