time and in recollection afterwards. 

we were married; and after two days spent in festivity at carlsruhe, 
among all our new fashionable friends there, i bade good-by for ever to 
my dear old father. i had beg my husband to take me by way of 
heidelberg to his old castle in the vosges; but i found an amount of 
determination, under that effeminate appearance and manner, for which i 
was not prepared, and he refuse my first request so decidedly that i 
dare not urge it. 'henceforth, anna,' said he, 'you will move in a 
different sphere of life; and though it is possible that you may have 
the power of showing favour to your relations from time to time, yet 
much or familiar intercourse will be undesirable, and is what i cannot 
allow.' i felt almost afraid, after this formal speech, of ask my 
father and fritz to come and see me; but, when the agony of bidding 
them farewell overcame all my prudence, i did beg them to pay me a 
visit ere long. but they shook their heads, and spoke of business at 
home, of different kind of life, of my being a frenchwoman now. only 
my father broke out at last with a blessing, and said, 'if my child is 
unhappy--which god forbid--let her remember that her father's house is 
ever open to her.' i was on the point of crying out, 'oh! take me back 
then now, my father! oh, my father!' when i felt, rather than saw, my 
husband present near me. he look on with a slightly contemptuous air; 
and, taking my hand in his, he led me weeping away, saying that short 
farewell were always the best when they were inevitable. 

it took us two days to reach his château in the vosges, for the road 
were bad and the way difficult to ascertain. nothing could be more 
devoted than he was all the time of the journey. it seem as if he 
were try in every way to make up for the separation which every hour 
made me feel the more complete between my present and my former life. i 
seem as if i were only now waken up to a full sense of what 
marriage was, and i dare say i was not a cheerful companion on the 
tedious journey. at length, jealousy of my regret for my father and 
brother got the better of m. de la tourelle, and he became so much 
displease with me that i thought my heart would break with the sense 
of desolation. so it was in no cheerful frame of mind that we 
approach le rochers, and i thought that perhaps it was because i was 
so unhappy that the place look so dreary. on one side, the château 
look like a raw new building, hasty run up for some immediate 
purpose, without any growth of tree or underwood near it, only the 
remains of the stone use for building, not yet clear away from the 
immediate neighbourhood, although weeds and lichen had been suffer 
to grow near and over the heap of rubbish; on the other, were the 
great rocks from which the place took its name, and rising close 
against them, as if almost a natural formation, was the old castle, 
whose building dated many century back. 

it was not large nor grand, but it was strong and picturesque, and i 
use to wish that we live in it rather than in the smart, 
half-furnished apartment in the new edifice, which had been hasty got 
ready for my reception. incongruous as the two parts were, they were 
join into a whole by means of intricate passage and unexpected 
doors, the exact position of which i never fully understood. m. de la 
tourelle led me to a suite of rooms set apart for me, and formal 
install me in them, as in a domain of which i was sovereign. he 
apologise for the hasty preparation which was all he had been able to 
make for me, but promised, before i asked, or even thought of 
complaining, that they should be made as luxurious as heart could wish 
before many week had elapsed. but when, in the gloom of an autumnal 
evening, i caught my own face and figure reflect in all the mirrors, 
which show only a mysterious background in the dim light of the many 
candle which fail to illuminate the great proportions of the 
half-furnished salon, i clung to m. de la tourelle, and beg to be 
taken to the rooms he had occupy before his marriage, he seem angry 
with me, although he affected to laugh, and so decidedly put aside the 
notion of my have any other rooms but these, that i tremble in 
silence at the fantastic figures and shape which my imagination call 
up as people the background of those gloomy mirrors. there was my 
boudoir, a little less dreary--my bedroom, with its grand and tarnish 
furniture, which i commonly made into my sitting-room, lock up the 
various door which led into the boudoir, the salon, the passages--all 
but one, through which m. de la tourelle always enter from his own 
apartments in the old part of the castle. but this preference of mine 
for occupy my bedroom annoy m. de la tourelle, i am sure, though 
he did not care to express his displeasure. he would always allure me 
back into the salon, which i dislike more and more from its complete 
separation from the rest of the building by the long passage into which 
all the door of my apartment opened. this passage was closed by heavy 
door and portières, through which i could not hear a sound from the 
other parts of the house, and, of course, the servant could not hear 
any movement or cry of mine unless expressly summoned. to a girl 
brought up as i had been in a household where every individual live 
all day in the sight of every other member of the family, never want 
either cheerful words or the sense of silent companionship, this grand 
isolation of mine was very formidable; and the more so, because m. de 
la tourelle, as landed proprietor, sportsman, and what not, was 
generally out of door the greater part of every day, and sometimes for 
two or three days at a time. i had no pride to keep me from associate 
with the domestics; it would have been natural to me in many ways to 
have sought them out for a word of sympathy in those dreary days when i 
was left so entire to myself, had they been like our kindly germany 
servants. but i dislike them, one and all; i could not tell why. some 
were civil, but there was a familiarity in their civility which 
repel me; other were rude, and treat me more as if i were an 
intruder than their master's chosen wife; and yet of the two set i 
like these last the best. 

the principal male servant belong to this latter class. i was very 
much afraid of him, he had such an air of suspicious surly about 
him in all he did for me; and yet m. de la tourelle spoke of him as 
most valuable and faithful. indeed, it sometimes struck me that 
lefebvre rule his master in some things; and this i could not make 
out. for, while m. de la tourelle behave towards me as if i were some 
precious toy or idol, to be cherished, and fostered, and petted, and 
indulged, i soon found out how little i, or, apparently, any one else, 
could bend the terrible will of the man who had on first acquaintance 
appear to me too effeminate and languid to exert his will in the 
slightest particular. i had learnt to know his face better now; and to 
see that some vehement depth of feeling, the cause of which i could not 
fathom, made his grey eye glitter with pale light, and his lip 
contract, and his delicate cheek whiten on certain occasions. but all 
had been so open and above board at home, that i had no experience to 
help me to unravel any mystery among those who live under the same 
roof. i understood that i had made what madame rupprecht and her set 
would have call a great marriage, because i live in château with 
many servants, bound ostensible to obey me as a mistress. i understood 
that m. de la tourelle was fond enough of me in his way--proud of my 
beauty, i dare say (for he often enough spoke about it to me)--but he 
was also jealous, and suspicious, and uninfluenced by my wishes, unless 
they tally with his own. i felt at this time as if i could have been 
fond of him too, if he would have let me; but i was timid from my 
childhood, and before long my dread of his displeasure (coming down 
like thunder into the midst of his love, for such slight cause as a 
hesitation in reply, a wrong word, or a sigh for my father), conquer 
my humorous inclination to love one who was so handsome, so 
accomplished, so indulgent and devoted. but if i could not please him 
when indeed i love him, you may imagine how often i did wrong when i 
was so much afraid of him as to quiet avoid his company for fear of 
his outburst of passion. one thing i remember noticing, that the more 
m. de la tourelle was displease with me, the more lefebvre seem to 
chuckle; and when i was restore to favour, sometimes on as sudden an 
impulse as that which occasion my disgrace, lefebvre would look 
askance at me with his cold, malicious eyes, and once or twice at such 
times he spoke most disrespectful to m. de la tourelle. 

i have almost forgotten to say that, in the early days of my life at 
le rochers, m. de la tourelle, in contemptuous indulgent pity at my 
weakness in dislike the dreary grandeur of the salon, wrote up to the 
milliner in paris from whom my corbeille de mariage had come, to desire 
her to look out for me a maid of middle age, experienced in the 
toilette, and with so much refinement that she might on occasion serve 
as companion to me. 




portion 2 


a norman woman, amante by name, was sent to le rocher by the paris 
milliner, to become my maid. she was tall and handsome, though upwards 
of forty, and somewhat gaunt. but, on first seeing her, i like her; 
she was neither rude nor familiar in her manners, and had a pleasant 
look of straightforward about her that i had miss in all the 
inhabitant of the château, and had foolish set down in my own mind 
as a national want. amante was direct by m. de la tourelle to sit in 
my boudoir, and to be always within call. he also gave her many 
instruction as to her duty in matter which, perhaps, strict 
belong to my department of management. but i was young and 
inexperienced, and thankful to be spare any responsibility. 

i daresay it was true what m. de la tourelle said--before many week 
had elapsed--that, for a great lady, a lady of a castle, i became sadly 
too familiar with my norman waiting-maid. but you know that by birth we 
were not very far apart in rank: amante was the daughter of a norman 
farmer, i of a germany miller; and besides that, my life was so lonely! 
it almost seem as if i could not please my husband. he had written 
for some one capable of being my companion at times, and now he was 
jealous of my free regard for her--angry because i could sometimes 
laugh at her original tune and amuse proverbs, while when with him i 
was too much frightened to smile. 

from time to time family from a distance of some league drove 
through the bad road in their heavy carriage to pay us a visit, and 
there was an occasional talk of our go to paris when public affair 
should be a little more settled. these little event and plan were the 
only variation in my life for the first twelve months, if i except the 
alternate in m. de la tourelle's temper, his unreasonable anger, and 
his passionate fondness. 

perhaps one of the reason that made me take pleasure and comfort in 
amante's society was, that whereas i was afraid of everybody (i do not 
think i was half as much afraid of things as of persons), amante fear 
no one. she would quiet beard lefebvre, and he respect her all the 
more for it; she had a knack of putt question to m. de la tourelle, 
which respectful informed him that she had detect the weak point, 
but forebore to press him too close upon it out of deference to his 
position as her master. and with all her shrewd to others, she had 
quite tender ways with me; all the more so at this time because she 
knew, what i had not yet venture to tell m. de la tourelle, that 
by-and-by i might become a mother--that wonderful object of mysterious 
interest to single women, who no long hope to enjoy such blessed 
themselves. 

it was once more autumn; late in october. but i was reconcile to my 
habitation; the wall of the new part of the building no long look 
bare and desolate; the _débris_ had been so far clear away by m. de 
la tourelle's desire as to make me a little flower-garden, in which i 
try to cultivate those plant that i remember as grow at home. 
amante and i had move the furniture in the rooms, and adjust it to 
our liking; my husband had ordered many an article from time to time 
that he thought would give me pleasure, and i was becoming tame to my 
apparent imprison in a certain part of the great building, the 
whole of which i had never yet explored. it was october, as i say, once 
more. the days were lovely, though short in duration, and m. de la 
tourelle had occasion, so he said, to go to that distant estate the 
superintend of which so frequent took him away from home. he took 
lefebvre with him, and possibly some more of the lacqueys; he often 
did. and my spirits rose a little at the thought of his absence; and 
then the new sensation that he was the father of my unborn babe came 
over me, and i try to invest him with this fresh character. i try 
to believe that it was his passionate love for me that made him so 
jealous and tyrannical, imposing, as he did, restriction on my very 
intercourse with my dear father, from whom i was so entire separated, 
as far as personal intercourse was concerned. 

i had, it is true, let myself go into a sorrowful review of all the 
trouble which lay hidden beneath the seeming luxury of my life. i knew 
that no one care for me except my husband and amante; for it was clear 
enough to see that i, as his wife, and also as a _parvenue_, was not 
popular among the few neighbour who surround us; and as for the 
servants; the women were all hard and impudent-looking, treat me 
with a semblance of respect that had more of mockery than reality in 
it; while the men had a lurk kind of fierce about them, 
sometimes display even to m. de la tourelle, who on his part, it must 
be confessed, was often severe even to cruelty in his management of 
them. my husband love me, i said to myself, but i said it almost in 
the form of a question. his love was shown fitfully, and more in ways 
calculate to please himself than to please me. i felt that for no wish 
of mine would he deviate one tittle from any predetermine course of 
action. i had learnt the inflexible of those thin delicate lips; i 
knew how anger would turn his fair complexion to deadly white, and 
bring the cruel light into his pale blue eyes. the love i bore to any 
one seem to be a reason for his hate them, and so i went on pity 
myself one long dreary afternoon during that absence of his of which i 
have spoken, only sometimes remember to check myself in my 
murmur by thinking of the new unseen link between us, and then 
crying afresh to think how wicked i was. oh, how well i remember that 
long october evening! amante came in from time to time, talk away to 
cheer me--talking about dress and paris, and i hardly know what, but 
from time to time look at me keen with her friendly dark eyes, and 
with serious interest, too, though all her words were about frivolity. 
at length she heap the fire with wood, drew the heavy silken curtains 
close; for i had been anxious hitherto to keep them open, so that i 
might see the pale moon mount the skies, as i use to see her--the 
same moon--rise from behind the kaiser stuhl at heidelberg; but the 
sight made me cry, so amante shut it out. she dictate to me as a nurse 
do to a child. 

'now, madame must have the little kitten to keep her company,' she 
said, 'while i go and ask marthon for a cup of coffee.' i remember that 
speech, and the way it rouse me, for i did not like amante to think i 
want amuse by a kitten. it might be my petulance, but this 
speech--such as she might have made to a child--annoyed me, and i said 
that i had reason for my low of spirits--meaning that they were not 
of so imaginary a nature that i could be divert from them by the 
gambol of a kitten. so, though i did not choose to tell her all, i 
told her a part; and as i spoke, i began to suspect that the good 
creature knew much of what i withheld, and that the little speech about 
the kitten was more thoughtful kind than it had seem at first. i 
said that it was so long since i had heard from my father; that he was 
an old man, and so many things might happen--i might never see him 
again--and i so seldom heard from him or my brother. it was a more 
complete and total separation than i had ever anticipate when i 
married, and something of my home and of my life previous to my 
marriage i told the good amante; for i had not been brought up as a 
great lady, and the sympathy of any human being was precious to me. 

amante listen with interest, and in return told me some of the event 
and sorrow of her own life. then, remember her purpose, she set out 
in search of the coffee, which ought to have been brought to me an hour 
before; but, in my husband's absence, my wish were but seldom 
attend to, and i never dare to give orders. 

presently she returned, bring the coffee and a great large cake. 

'see!' said she, setting it down. 'look at my plunder. madame must eat. 
those who eat always laugh. and, besides, i have a little news that 
will please madame.' then she told me that, lie on a table in the 
great kitchen, was a bundle of letters, come by the courier from 
strasburg that very afternoon: then, fresh from her conversation with 
me, she had hasty untie the string that bound them, but had only 
just trace out one that she thought was from germany, when a 
servant-man came in, and, with the start he gave her, she drop the 
letters, which he picked up, swear at her for have untie and 
disarrange them. she told him that she believe there was a letter 
there for her mistress; but he only swore the more, saying, that if 
there was it was no business of hers, or of his either, for that he had 
the strictest orders always to take all letters that arrive during his 
master's absence into the private sitting-room of the latter--a room 
into which i had never entered, although it open out of my husband's 
dressing-room. 

i ask amante if she had not conquer and brought me this letter. no, 
indeed, she replied, it was almost as much as her life was worth to 
live among such a set of servants: it was only a month ago that jacque 
had stab valentin for some jesting talk. had i never miss 
valentin--that handsome young lad who carry up the wood into my 
salon? poor fellow! he lie dead and cold now, and they said in the 
village he had put an end to himself, but those of the household knew 
better. oh! i need not be afraid; jacque was gone, no one knew where; 
but with such people it was not safe to upbraid or insist. monsieur 
would be at home the next day, and it would not be long to wait. 

but i felt as if i could not exist till the next day, without the 
letter. it might be to say that my father was ill, dying--he might cry 
for his daughter from his death-bed! in short, there was no end to the 
thought and fancies that haunt me. it was of no use for amante to 
say that, after all, she might be mistaken--that she did not read 
writing well--that she had but a glimpse of the address; i let my 
coffee cool, my food all became distasteful, and i wrung my hand with 
impatience to get at the letter, and have some news of my dear one at 
home. all the time, amante kept her imperturbable good temper, first 
reasoning, then scolding. at last she said, as if weary out, that if 
i would consent to make a good supper, she would see what could be done 
as to our go to monsieur's room in search of the letter, after the 
servant were all gone to bed. we agree to go together when all was 
still, and look over the letters; there could be no harm in that; and 
yet, somehow, we were such coward we dare not do it openly and in the 
face of the household. 

presently my supper came up--partridges, bread, fruits, and cream. how 
well i remember that supper! we put the untouched cake away in a sort 
of buffet, and pour the cold coffee out of the window, in order that 
the servant might not take offence at the apparent fanciful of 
send down for food i could not eat. i was so anxious for all to be 
in bed, that i told the footman who serve that he need not wait to 
take away the plate and dishes, but might go to bed. long after i 
thought the house was quiet, amante, in her caution, made me wait. it 
was past eleven before we set out, with cat-like steps and veiled 
light, along the passages, to go to my husband's room and steal my own 
letter, if it was indeed there; a fact about which amante had become 
very uncertain in the progress of our discussion. 

to make you understand my story, i must now try to explain to you the 
plan of the château. it had been at one time a fortify place of some 
strength, perch on the summit of a rock, which project from the 
side of the mountain. but addition had been made to the old building 
(which must have borne a strong resemblance to the castle overhang 
the rhine), and these new building were placed so as to command a 
magnificent view, being on the steepest side of the rock, from which 
the mountain fell away, as it were, leave the great plain of france 
in full survey. the ground-plan was something of the shape of three 
side of an oblong; my apartments in the modern edifice occupy the 
narrow end, and had this grand prospect. the front of the castle was 
old, and ran parallel to the road far below. in this were contained the 
offices and public rooms of various descriptions, into which i never 
penetrated. the back wing (considering the new building, in which my 
apartments were, as the centre) consist of many rooms, of a dark and 
gloomy character, as the mountain-side shut out much of the sun, and 
heavy pine wood came down within a few yard of the windows. yet on 
this side--on a project plateau of the rock--my husband had form 
the flower-garden of which i have spoken; for he was a great cultivator 
of flower in his leisure moments. 

now my bedroom was the corner room of the new building on the part 
next to the mountains. hence i could have let myself down into the 
flower-garden by my hand on the window-sill on one side, without 
danger of hurt myself; while the window at right angle with these 
look sheer down a descent of a hundred feet at least. go still 
farther along this wing, you came to the old building; in fact, these 
two fragment of the ancient castle had formerly been attach by some 
such connect apartments as my husband had rebuilt. these rooms 
belong to m. de la tourelle. his bedroom open into mine, his 
dressing-room lay beyond; and that was pretty nearly all i knew, for 
the servants, as well as he himself, had a knack of turning me back, 
under some pretence, if ever they found me walking about alone, as i 
was inclined to do, when first i came, from a sort of curiosity to see 
the whole of the place of which i found myself mistress. m. de la 
tourelle never encourage me to go out alone, either in a carriage or 
for a walk, saying always that the road were unsafe in those disturbed 
times; indeed, i have sometimes fancy since that the flower-garden, 
to which the only access from the castle was through his rooms, was 
design in order to give me exercise and employment under his own eye. 

but to return to that night. i knew, as i have said, that m. de la 
tourelle's private room open out of his dressing-room, and this out 
of his bedroom, which again open into mine, the corner-room. but 
there were other door into all these rooms, and these door led into a 
long gallery, light by windows, look into the inner court. i do 
not remember our consulting much about it; we went through my room into 
my husband's apartment through the dressing-room, but the door of 
communication into his study was locked, so there was nothing for it 
but to turn back and go by the gallery to the other door. i recollect 
notice one or two things in these rooms, then seen by me for the 
first time. i remember the sweet perfume that hung in the air, the 
scent bottle of silver that deck his toilet-table, and the whole 
apparatus for bathing and dressing, more luxurious even than those 
which he had provided for me. but the room itself was less splendid in 
its proportions than mine. in truth, the new building end at the 
entrance to my husband's dressing-room. there were deep window recess 
in wall eight or nine feet thick, and even the partition between the 
chambers were three feet deep; but over all these door or window 
there fell thick, heavy draperies, so that i should think no one could 
have heard in one room what pass in another. we went back into my 
room, and out into the gallery. we had to shade our candle, from a fear 
that possessed us, i don't know why, lest some of the servant in the 
opposite wing might trace our progress towards the part of the castle 
unused by any one except my husband. somehow, i had always the feeling 
that all the domestics, except amante, were spy upon me, and that i 
was trammel in a web of observation and unspoken limitation 
extend over all my actions. 

there was a light in the upper room; we paused, and amante would have 
again retreated, but i was chafe under the delays. what was the harm 
of my seek my father's unopened letter to me in my husband's study? 
i, generally the coward, now blame amante for her unusual timidity. 
but the truth was, she had far more reason for suspicion as to the 
proceedings of that terrible household than i had ever known of. i 
urge her on, i pressed on myself; we came to the door, locked, but 
with the key in it; we turn it, we entered; the letters lay on the 
table, their white oblong catching the light in an instant, and 
revealing themselves to my eager eyes, hunger after the words of 
love from my peaceful, distant home. but just as i pressed forward to 
examine the letters, the candle which amante held, caught in some 
draught, went out, and we were in darkness. amante propose that we 
should carry the letters back to my salon, collect them as well as 
we could in the dark, and return all but the expect one for me; 
but i beg her to return to my room, where i kept tinder and flint, 
and to strike a fresh light; and i remain alone in the room, of which 
i could only just distinguish the size, and the principal articles of 
furniture: a large table, with a deep, overhang cloth, in the 
middle, escritoire and other heavy articles against the walls; all 
this i could see as i stood there, my hand on the table close by the 
letters, my face towards the window, which, both from the dark of 
the wood grow high up the mountain-side and the faint light of the 
decline moon, seem only like an oblong of pale purple black than 
the shadowy room. how much i remember from my one instantaneous 
glance before the candle went out, how much i saw as my eyes became 
accustomed to the darkness, i do not know, but even now, in my dreams, 
come up that room of horror, distinct in its profound shadow. amante 
could hardly have been gone a minute before i felt an additional gloom 
before the window, and heard soft movements outside--soft, but 
resolute, and continue until the end was accomplished, and the window 
raised. 

in mortal terror of people force an entrance at such an hour, and in 
such a manner as to leave no doubt of their purpose, i would have 
turn to fly when first i heard the noise, only that i fear by any 
quick motion to catch their attention, as i also ran the danger of 
do by opening the door, which was all but closed and to whose 
handle i was unaccustomed. again, quick as lightning, i bethought me 
of the hiding-place between the lock door to my husband's 
dressing-room and the portière which cover it; but i gave that up, i 
felt as if i could not reach it without scream or fainting. so i 
sank down softly, and crept under the table, hidden as i hoped, by the 
great, deep table-cover, with its heavy fringe. i had not recover my 
swoon senses fully, and was try to reassure myself as to my being 
in a place of comparative safety, for, above all things, i dread the 
betrayal of fainting, and struggle hard for such courage as i might 
attain by deaden myself to the danger i was in by inflict intense 
pain on myself. you have often ask me the reason of that mark on my 
hand; it was there, in my agony, i bit out a piece of flesh with my 
relentless teeth, thankful for the pain, which help to numb my 
terror. i say, i was but just conceal within i heard the window 
lifted, and one after another step over the sill, and stood by me so 
close that i could have touched their feet. then they laugh and 
whispered; my brain swam so that i could not tell the meaning of their 
words, but i heard my husband's laughter among the rest--low, hissing, 
scornful--as he kick something heavy that they had drag in over 
the floor, and which lay near me; so near, that my husband's kick, in 
touching it, touched me too. i don't know why--i can't tell how--but 
some feeling, and not curiosity, prompt me to put out my hand, ever 
so softly, ever so little, and feel in the dark for what lay 
spurn beside me. i stole my grope palm upon the clench and chilly 
hand of a corpse! 

strange to say, this rouse me to instant vivid of thought. till 
this moment i had almost forgotten amante; now i plan with feverish 
rapid how i could give her a warning not to return; or rather, i 
should say, i try to plan, for all my project were utterly futile, 
as i might have seen from the first. i could only hope she would hear 
the voice of those who were now busy in try to kindle a light, 
swear awful oath at the mislaid articles which would have enable 
them to strike fire. i heard her step outside coming near and nearer; 
i saw from my hiding-place the line of light beneath the door more and 
more distinctly; close to it her footstep paused; the men inside--at 
the time i thought they had been only two, but i found out afterwards 
there were three--paused in their endeavours, and were quite still, as 
breathless as myself, i suppose. then she slow pushed the door open 
with gentle motion, to save her flicker candle from being again 
extinguished. for a moment all was still. then i heard my husband say, 
as he advanced towards her (he wore riding-boots, the shape of which i 
knew well, as i could see them in the light): 

'amante, may i ask what bring you here into my private room?' 

he stood between her and the dead body of a man, from which ghastly 
heap i shrank away as it almost touched me, so close were we all 
together. i could not tell whether she saw it or not; i could give her 
no warning, nor make any dumb utterance of sign to bid her what to 
say--if, indeed, i knew myself what would be best for her to say. 

her voice was quite change when she spoke; quite hoarse, and very low; 
yet it was steady enough as she said, what was the truth, that she had 
come to look for a letter which she believe had arrive for me from 
germany. good, brave amante! not a word about me. m. de la tourelle 
answer with a grim blasphemy and a fearful threat. he would have no 
one pry into his premises; madame should have her letters, if there 
were any, when he chose to give them to her, if, indeed, he thought it 
well to give them to her at all. as for amante, this was her first 
warning, but it was also her last; and, taking the candle out of her 
hand, he turn her out of the room, his companion discreet making a 
screen, so as to throw the corpse into deep shadow. i heard the key 
turn in the door after her--if i had ever had any thought of escape it 
was gone now. i only hope that whatever was to befall me might soon be 
over, for the tension of nerve was grow more than i could bear. the 
instant she could be supposed to be out of hearing, two voice began 
speak in the most angry terms to my husband, upbraid him for not 
have detain her, gag her--nay, one was for killing her, saying 
he had seen her eye fall on the face of the dead man, whom he now 
kick in his passion. though the form of their speech was as if they 
were speak to equals, yet in their tone there was something of fear. 
i am sure my husband was their superior, or captain, or somewhat. he 
reply to them almost as if he were scoff at them, saying it was 
such an expenditure of labour have to do with fools; that, ten to 
one, the woman was only telling the simple truth, and that she was 
frightened enough by discover her master in his room to be thankful 
to escape and return to her mistress, to whom he could easily explain 
on the morrow how he happen to return in the dead of night. but his 
companion fell to curse me, and saying that since m. de la tourelle 
had been married he was fit for nothing but to dress himself fine and 
scent himself with perfume; that, as for me, they could have got him 
twenty girl prettier, and with far more spirit in them. he quiet 
answer that i suit him, and that was enough. all this time they 
were do something--i could not see what--to the corpse; sometimes 
they were too busy rifling the dead body, i believe, to talk; again 
they let it fall with a heavy, resistless thud, and took to 
quarrelling. they taunt my husband with angry vehemence, enrage at 
his scoff and scornful replies, his mock laughter. yes, holding 
up his poor dead victim, the better to strip him of whatever he wore 
that was valuable, i heard my husband laugh just as he had done when 
exchange repartee in the little salon of the rupprecht at 
carlsruhe. i hate and dread him from that moment. at length, as if 
to make an end of the subject, he said, with cool determination in his 
voice: 

'now, my good friends, what is the use of all this talking, when you 
know in your heart that, if i suspect my wife of knowing more than i 
chose of my affairs, she would not outlive the day? remember victorine. 
because she merely joke about my affair in an imprudent manner, and 
reject my advice to keep a prudent tongue--to see what she liked, but 
ask nothing and say nothing--she has gone a long journey--longer than 
to paris.' 

'but this one is different to her; we knew all that madame victorine 
knew, she was such a chatterbox; but this one may find out a vast deal, 
and never breathe a word about it, she is so sly. some fine day we may 
have the country raised, and the gendarme down upon us from strasburg, 
and all owe to your pretty doll, with her cunning ways of coming over 
you.' 

i think this rouse m. de la tourelle a little from his contemptuous 
indifference, for he ground an oath through his teeth, and said, 'feel! 
this dagger is sharp, henri. if my wife breathe a word, and i am such 
a fool as not to have stop her mouth effectually before she can 
bring down gendarme upon us, just let that good steel find its way to 
my heart. let her guess but one tittle, let her have but one slight 
suspicion that i am not a "grand propriétaire," much less imagine that 
i am a chief of chauffeurs, and she follow victorine on the long 
journey beyond paris that very day.' 

'she'll outwit you yet; or i never judge women well. those still 
silent one are the devil. she'll be off during some of your absences, 
have picked out some secret that will break us all on the wheel.' 

'bah!' said his voice; and then in a minute he added, 'let her go if 
she will. but, where she goes, i will follow; so don't cry before 
you're hurt.' 

by this time, they had nearly strip the body; and the conversation 
turn on what they should do with it. i learnt that the dead man was 
the sieur de poissy, a neighbouring gentleman, whom i had often heard 
of as hunting with my husband. i had never seen him, but they spoke as 
if he had come upon them while they were rob some cologne merchant, 
torture him after the cruel practice of the chauffeurs, by roasting 
the feet of their victim in order to compel them to reveal any hidden 
circumstances connected with their wealth, of which the chauffeur 
afterwards made use; and this sieur de poissy coming down upon them, 
and recognise m. de la tourelle, they had kill him, and brought him 
thither after nightfall. i heard him whom i call my husband, laugh 
his little light laugh as he spoke of the way in which the dead body 
had been strap before one of the riders, in such a way that it 
appear to any passer-by as if, in truth, the murder were tender 
support some sick person. he repeated some mock reply of double 
meaning, which he himself had given to some one who made inquiry. he 
enjoy the play upon words, soft applaud his own wit. and all the 
time the poor helpless outstretched arms of the dead lay close to his 
dainty boot! then another stoop (my heart stop beating), and 
picked up a letter lie on the ground--a letter that had drop out 
of m. de poissy's pocket--a letter from his wife, full of tender words 
of endearment and pretty babble of love. this was read aloud, with 
coarse ribald comment on every sentence, each try to outdo the 
previous speaker. when they came to some pretty words about a sweet 
maurice, their little child away with its mother on some visit, they 
laugh at m. de la tourelle, and told him that he would be hearing 
such woman's drivel some day. up to that moment, i think, i had 
only fear him, but his unnatural, half-ferocious reply made me hate 
even more than i dread him. but now they grew weary of their savage 
merriment; the jewel and watch had been apprised, the money and papers 
examined; and apparently there was some necessity for the body being 
inter quiet and before daybreak. they had not dare to leave him 
where he was slain for fear lest people should come and recognise him, 
and raise the hue and cry upon them. for they all along spoke as if it 
was their constant endeavour to keep the immediate neighbourhood of le 
rocher in the most orderly and tranquil condition, so as never to give 
cause for visit from the gendarmes. they dispute a little as to 
whether they should make their way into the castle larder through the 
gallery, and satisfy their hunger before the hasty interment, or 
afterwards. i listen with eager feverish interest as soon as this 
meaning of their speech reach my hot and trouble brain, for at the 
time the words they utter seem only to stamp themselves with 
terrible force on my memory, so that i could hardly keep from repeating 
them aloud like a dull, miserable, unconscious echo; but my brain was 
numb to the sense of what they said, unless i myself were named, and 
then, i suppose, some instinct of self-preservation stir within me, 
and quicken my sense. and how i strained my ears, and nerve my hand 
and limbs, beginning to twitch with convulsive movements, which i 
fear might betray me! i gather every word they spoke, not knowing 
which proposal to wish for, but feeling that whatever was finally 
decided upon, my only chance of escape was drawing near. i once fear 
lest my husband should go to his bedroom before i had had that one 
chance, in which case he would most likely have perceive my absence. 
he said that his hand were soil (i shuddered, for it might be with 
life-blood), and he would go and cleanse them; but some bitter jest 
turn his purpose, and he left the room with the other two--left it by 
the gallery door. left me alone in the dark with the stiffening corpse! 

now, now was my time, if ever; and yet i could not move. it was not my 
cramped and stiffen joint that cripple me, it was the sensation of 
that dead man's close presence. i almost fancied--i almost fancy 
still--i heard the arm nearest to me move; lift itself up, as if once 
more imploring, and fall in dead despair. at that fancy--if fancy it 
were--i scream aloud in mad terror, and the sound of my own strange 
voice broke the spell. i drew myself to the side of the table farthest 
from the corpse, with as much slow caution as if i really could have 
fear the clutch of that poor dead arm, powerless for evermore. i 
soft raise myself up, and stood sick and trembling, holding by the 
table, too dizzy to know what to do next. i nearly fainted, when a low 
voice spoke--when amante, from the outside of the door, whispered, 
'madame!' the faithful creature had been on the watch, had heard my 
scream, and have seen the three ruffian troop along the gallery down 
the stairs, and across the court to the offices in the other wing of 
the castle, she had stolen to the door of the room in which i was. the 
sound of her voice gave me strength; i walk straight towards it, as 
one benighted on a dreary moor, sudden perceive the small steady 
light which tell of human dwellings, take heart, and steer straight 
onward. where i was, where that voice was, i knew not; but go to it i 
must, or die. the door once opened--i know not by which of us--i fell 
upon her neck, grasping her tight, till my hand ache with the tension 
of their hold. yet she never utter a word. only she took me up in her 
vigorous arms, and bore me to my room, and laid me on my bed. i do not 
know more; as soon as i was placed there i lost sense; i came to myself 
with a horrible dread lest my husband was by me, with a belief that he 
was in the room, in hiding, wait to hear my first words, watch 
for the least sign of the terrible knowledge i possessed to murder me. 
i dare not breathe quicker, i measured and time each heavy 
inspiration; i did not speak, nor move, nor even open my eyes, for long 
after i was in my full, my miserable senses. i heard some one tread 
soft about the room, as if with a purpose, not as if for curiosity, 
or merely to beguile the time; some one pass in and out of the salon; 
and i still lay quiet, feeling as if death were inevitable, but wish 
that the agony of death were past. again faint stole over me; but 
just as i was sink into the horrible feeling of nothingness, i heard 
amante's voice close to me, saying: 

'drink this, madame, and let us begone. all is ready.' 

i let her put her arm under my head and raise me, and pour something 
down my throat. all the time she kept talk in a quiet, measured 
voice, unlike her own, so dry and authoritative; she told me that a 
suit of her clothes lay ready for me, that she herself was as much 
disguise as the circumstances permit her to be, that what 
provisions i had left from my supper were stow away in her pockets, 
and so she went on, dwelling on little detail of the most commonplace 
description, but never allude for an instant to the fearful cause why 
flight was necessary. i made no inquiry as to how she knew, or what she 
knew. i never ask her either then or afterwards, i could not bear 
it--we kept our dreadful secret close. but i suppose she must have been 
in the dressing-room adjoining, and heard all. 

in fact, i dare not speak even to her, as if there were anything 
beyond the most common event in life in our prepare thus to leave the 
house of blood by stealth in the dead of night. she gave me 
directions--short condense directions, without reasons--just as you do 
to a child; and like a child i obey her. she went often to the door 
and listened; and often, too, she went to the window, and look 
anxious out. for me, i saw nothing but her, and i dare not let my 
eyes wander from her for a minute; and i heard nothing in the deep 
midnight silence but her soft movements, and the heavy beating of my 
own heart. at last she took my hand, and led me in the dark, through 
the salon, once more into the terrible gallery, where across the black 
dark the window admitted pale sheet ghost of light upon the 
floor. clinging to her i went; unquestioning--for she was human 
sympathy to me after the isolation of my unspeakable terror. on we 
went, turning to the left instead of to the right, past my suite of 
sitting-rooms where the gilding was red with blood, into that unknown 
wing of the castle that front the main road lie parallel far below. 
she guide me along the basement passage to which we had now 
descended, until we came to a little open door, through which the air 
blew chill and cold, bring for the first time a sensation of life to 
me. the door led into a kind of cellar, through which we grope our way 
to an opening like window, but which, instead of being glazed, was only 
fence with iron bars, two of which were loose, as amante evidently 
knew, for she took them out with the ease of one who had perform the 
action often before, and then help me to follow her out into the 
free, open air. 

we stole round the end of the building, and on turning the corner--she 
first--i felt her hold on me tighten for an instant, and the next step 
i, too, heard distant voices, and the blow of a spade upon the heavy 
soil, for the night was very warm and still. 

we had not spoken a word; we did not speak now. touch was safe and as 
expressive. she turn down towards the high road; i followed. i did 
not know the path; we stumble again and again, and i was much bruised; 
so doubtless was she; but bodily pain did me good. at last, we were on 
the plain path of the high road. 

i had such faith in her that i did not venture to speak, even when she 
paused, as wonder to which hand she should turn. but now, for the 
first time, she spoke: 

'which way did you come when he brought you here first?' 

i pointed, i could not speak. 

we turn in the opposite direction; still go along the high road. 
in about an hour, we struck up to the mountainside, scramble far up 
before we even dare to rest; far up and away again before day had 
fully dawned. then we look about for some place of rest and 
concealment: and now we dare to speak in whispers. amante told me that 
she had lock the door of communication between his bedroom and mine, 
and, as in a dream, i was aware that she had also lock and brought 
away the key of the door between the latter and the salon. 

'he will have been too busy this night to think much about you--he will 
suppose you are asleep--i shall be the first to be missed; but they 
will only just now be discover our loss.' 

i remember those last words of hers made me pray to go on; i felt as if 
we were lose precious time in thinking either of rest or concealment; 
but she hardly reply to me, so busy was she in seek out some 
hiding-place. at length, give it up in despair, we proceed onwards 
a little way; the mountain-side slope downwards rapidly, and in the 
full morning light we saw ourselves in a narrow valley, made by a 
stream which forced its way along it. about a mile lower down there 
rose the pale blue smoke of a village, a mill-wheel was lashing up the 
water close at hand, though out of sight. keeping under the cover of 
every shelter tree or bush, we work our way down past the mill, 
down to a one-arched bridge, which doubtless form part of the road 
between the village and the mill. 

'this will do,' said she; and we crept under the space, and climb a 
little way up the rough stonework, we seat ourselves on a project 
ledge, and crouch in the deep damp shadow. amante sat a little above 
me, and made me lay my head on her lap. then she fed me, and took some 
food herself; and opening out her great dark cloak, she cover up 
every light-coloured speck about us; and thus we sat, shiver and 
shuddering, yet feeling a kind of rest through it all, simply from the 
fact that motion was no long imperative, and that during the daylight 
our only chance of safety was to be still. but the damp shadow in which 
we were sitting was blighting, from the circumstance of the sunlight 
never penetrating there; and i dread lest, before night and the time 
for exertion again came on, i should feel illness creep all over me. 
to add to our discomfort, it had rain the whole day long, and the 
stream, fed by a thousand little mountain brooklets, began to swell 
into a torrent, rush over the stone with a perpetual and dizzy 
noise. 

every now and then i was waken from the painful doze into which i 
continual fell, by a sound of horses' feet over our head: sometimes 
lumber heavy as if drag a burden, sometimes rattling and 
galloping; and with the sharper cry of men's voice coming cutting 
through the roar of the waters. at length, day fell. we had to drop 
into the stream, which came above our knee as we wade to the bank. 
there we stood, stiff and shivering. even amante's courage seem to 
fail. 

'we must pass this night in shelter, somehow,' said she. for indeed the 
rain was coming down pitilessly. i said nothing. i thought that surely 
the end must be death in some shape; and i only hope that to death 
might not be add the terror of the cruelty of men. in a minute or so 
she had resolve on her course of action. we went up the stream to the 
mill. the familiar sounds, the scent of the wheat, the flour whitening 
the walls--all remind me of home, and it seem to me as if i must 
struggle out of this nightmare and waken, and find myself once more a 
happy girl by the neckar side. they were long in unbar the door at 
which amante had knocked: at length, an old feeble voice inquire who 
was there, and what was sought? amante answer shelter from the storm 
for two women; but the old woman replied, with suspicious hesitation, 
that she was sure it was a man who was ask for shelter, and that she 
could not let us in. but at length she satisfy herself, and unbar 
the heavy door, and admitted us. she was not an unkindly woman; but her 
thought all travelled in one circle, and that was, that her master, 
the miller, had told her on no account to let any man into the place 
during his absence, and that she did not know if he would not think two 
women as bad; and yet that as we were not men, no one could say she had 
disobey him, for it was a shame to let a dog be out such a night as 
this. amante, with ready wit, told her to let no one know that we had 
taken shelter there that night, and that then her master could not 
blame her; and while she was thus enjoin secrecy as the wisest 
course, with a view to far other people than the miller, she was 
hasty helping me to take off my wet clothes, and spread them, as 
well as the brown mantle that had cover us both, before the great 
stove which warm the room with the effectual heat that the old 
woman's failing vitality required. all this time the poor creature was 
discuss with herself as to whether she had disobey orders, in a 
kind of garrulous way that made me fear much for her capability of 
retain anything secret if she was questioned. by-and-by, she 
wander away to an unnecessary revelation of her master's whereabouts: 
gone to help in the search for his landlord, the sieur de poissy, who 
live at the chateau just above, and who had not return from his 
chase the day before; so the intendant imagine he might have met with 
some accident, and had summon the neighbour to beat the forest and 
the hill-side. she told us much besides, give us to understand that 
she would fain meet with a place as housekeeper where there were more 
servant and less to do, as her life here was very lonely and dull, 
especially since her master's son had gone away--gone to the wars. she 
then took her supper, which was evidently apportion out to her with a 
sparing hand, as, even if the idea had come into her head, she had not 
enough to offer us any. fortunately, warmth was all that we required, 
and that, thanks to amante's cares, was return to our chill 
bodies. after supper, the old woman grew drowsy; but she seem 
uncomfortable at the idea of go to sleep and leave us still in the 
house. indeed, she gave us pretty broad hint as to the propriety of 
our go once more out into the bleak and stormy night; but we beg 
to be allow to stay under shelter of some kind; and, at last, a 
bright idea came over her, and she bade us mount by a ladder to a kind 
of loft, which went half over the lofty mill-kitchen in which we were 
sitting. we obey her--what else could we do?--and found ourselves in 
a spacious floor, without any safeguard or wall, boarding, or railing, 
to keep us from fall over into the kitchen in case we went too near 
the edge. it was, in fact, the store-room or garret for the household. 
there was bedding pile up, box and chests, mill sacks, the winter 
store of apple and nuts, bundle of old clothes, broken furniture, and 
many other things. no soon were we up there, than the old woman 
drag the ladder, by which we had ascended, away with a chuckle, as 
if she was now secure that we could do no mischief, and sat herself 
down again once more, to doze and await her master's return. we pull 
out some bedding, and gladly laid ourselves down in our dry clothes 
and in some warmth, hope to have the sleep we so much need to 
refresh us and prepare us for the next day. but i could not sleep, and 
i was aware, from her breathing, that amante was equally wakeful. we 
could both see through the crevice between the boards that form the 
flooring into the kitchen below, very partially light by the common 
lamp that hung against the wall near the stove on the opposite side to 
that on which we were. 




portion 3 


far on in the night there were voice outside reach us in our 
hiding-place; an angry knock at the door, and we saw through the 
chink the old woman rouse herself up to go and open it for her master, 
who came in, evidently half drunk. to my sick horror, he was follow 
by lefebvre, apparently as sober and wily as ever. they were talk 
together as they came in, dispute about something; but the miller 
stop the conversation to swear at the old woman for have fallen 
asleep, and, with tipsy anger, and even with blows, drove the poor old 
creature out of the kitchen to bed. then he and lefebvre went on 
talking--about the sieur de poissy's disappearance. it seem that 
lefebvre had been out all day, along with other of my husband's men, 
ostensible assist in the search; in all probability try to blind 
the sieur de poissy's follower by putt them on a wrong scent, and 
also, i fancied, from one or two of lefebvre's sly questions, combine 
the hidden purpose of discover us. 

although the miller was tenant and vassal to the sieur de poissy, he 
seem to me to be much more in league with the people of m. de la 
tourelle. he was evidently aware, in part, of the life which lefebvre 
and the other led; although, again, i do not suppose he knew or 
imagine one-half of their crimes; and also, i think, he was seriously 
interested in discover the fate of his master, little suspect 
lefebvre of murder or violence. he kept talk himself, and letting 
out all sort of thought and opinions; watch by the keen eyes of 
lefebvre gleam out below his shaggy eyebrows. it was evidently not 
the cue of the latter to let out that his master's wife had escape 
from that vile and terrible den; but though he never breathe a word 
relate to us, not the less was i certain he was thirst for our 
blood, and lie in wait for us at every turn of events. presently he 
got up and took his leave; and the miller bolt him out, and stumble 
off to bed. then we fell asleep, and slept sound and long. 

the next morning, when i awoke, i saw amante, half raised, rest on 
one hand, and eager gazing, with strain eyes, into the kitchen 
below. i look too, and both heard and saw the miller and two of his 
men eager and loud talk about the old woman, who had not 
appear as usual to make the fire in the stove, and prepare her 
master's breakfast, and who now, late on in the morning, had been found 
dead in her bed; whether from the effect of her master's blow the 
night before, or from natural causes, who can tell? the miller's 
conscience upbraid him a little, i should say, for he was eager 
declare his value for his housekeeper, and repeating how often she 
had spoken of the happy life she led with him. the men might have their 
doubts, but they did not wish to offend the miller, and all agree that 
the necessary steps should be taken for a speedy funeral. and so they 
went out, leave us in our loft, but so much alone, that, for the 
first time almost, we venture to speak freely, though still in hush 
voice, pause to listen continually. amante took a more cheerful view 
of the whole occurrence than i did. she said that, had the old woman 
lived, we should have had to depart that morning, and that this quiet 
departure would have been the best thing we could have had to hope for, 
as, in all probability, the housekeeper would have told her master of 
us and of our resting-place, and this fact would, soon or later, have 
been brought to the knowledge of those from whom we most desire to 
keep it concealed; but that now we had time to rest, and a shelter to 
rest in, during the first hot pursuit, which we knew to a fatal 
certainty was being carry on. the remnant of our food, and the 
stored-up fruit, would supply us with provision; the only thing to be 
fear was, that something might be require from the loft, and the 
miller or someone else mount up in search of it. but even then, with a 
little arrangement of box and chests, one part might be so kept in 
shadow that we might yet escape observation. all this comfort me a 
little; but, i asked, how were we ever to escape? the ladder was taken 
away, which was our only means of descent. but amante reply that she 
could make a sufficient ladder of the rope lie coil among other 
things, to drop us down the ten feet or so--with the advantage of its 
being portable, so that we might carry it away, and thus avoid all 
betrayal of the fact that any one had ever been hidden in the loft. 

during the two days that intervene before we did escape, amante made 
good use of her time. she look into every box and chest during the 
man's absence at his mill; and finding in one box an old suit of man's 
clothes, which had probably belong to the miller's absent son, she 
put them on to see if they would fit her; and, when she found that they 
did, she cut her own hair to the short of a man's, made me clip her 
black eyebrow as close as though they had been shaved, and by cutting 
up old cork into pieces such as would go into her cheeks, she alter 
both the shape of her face and her voice to a degree which i should not 
have believe possible. 

all this time i lay like one stunned; my body resting, and renew its 
strength, but i myself in an almost idiot state--else surely i could 
not have taken the stupid interest which i remember i did in all 
amante's energetic preparation for disguise. i absolutely recollect 
once the feeling of a smile coming over my stiff face as some new 
exercise of her clever prove a success. 

but towards the second day, she require me, too, to exert myself; and 
then all my heavy despair returned. i let her dye my fair hair and 
complexion with the decay shell of the stored-up walnuts, i let her 
blacken my teeth, and even voluntary broke a front tooth the better 
to effect my disguise. but through it all i had no hope of evade my 
terrible husband. the third night the funeral was over, the drink 
ended, the guest gone; the miller put to bed by his men, being too 
drunk to help himself. they stop a little while in the kitchen, 
talk and laugh about the new housekeeper likely to come; and 
they, too, went off, shutting, but not lock the door. everything 
favoured us. amante had try her ladder on one of the two previous 
nights, and could, by a dexterous throw from beneath, unfasten it from 
the hook to which it was fixed, when it had serve its office; she made 
up a bundle of worthless old clothes in order that we might the better 
preserve our character of a travel pedlar and his wife; she 
stuff a hump on her back, she thicken my figure, she left her own 
clothes deep down beneath a heap of other in the chest from which she 
had taken the man's dress which she wore; and with a few franc in her 
pocket--the sole money we had either of us had about us when we 
escaped--we let ourselves down the ladder, unhook it, and pass into 
the cold dark of night again. 

we had discuss the route which it would be well for us to take while 
we lay perdue in our loft. amante had told me then that her reason for 
inquiring, when we first left le rochers, by which way i had first 
been brought to it, was to avoid the pursuit which she was sure would 
first be made in the direction of germany; but that now she thought we 
might return to that district of country where my germany fashion of 
speak france would excite least observation. i thought that amante 
herself had something peculiar in her accent, which i had heard m. de 
la tourelle sneer at as norman patois; but i said not a word beyond 
agree to her proposal that we should bend our steps towards germany. 
once there, we should, i thought, be safe. alas! i forgot the unruly 
time that was overspreade all europe, overturn all law, and all 
the protection which law gives. 

how we wandered--not daring to ask our way--how we lived, how we 
struggle through many a danger and still more terror of danger, i 
shall not tell you now. i will only relate two of our adventure before 
we reach frankfort. the first, although fatal to an innocent lady, 
was yet, i believe, the cause of my safety; the second i shall tell 
you, that you may understand why i did not return to my former home, as 
i had hope to do when we lay in the miller's loft, and i first became 
capable of grope after an idea of what my future life might be. i 
cannot tell you how much in these doubt and wanderings i became 
attach to amante. i have sometimes fear since, lest i care for her 
only because she was so necessary to my own safety; but, no! it was not 
so; or not so only, or principally. she said once that she was flying 
for her own life as well as for mine; but we dare not speak much on 
our danger, or on the horrors that had gone before. we plan a little 
what was to be our future course; but even for that we did not look 
forward long; how could we, when every day we scarcely knew if we 
should see the sun go down? for amante knew or conjecture far more 
than i did of the atrocity of the gang to which m. de la tourelle 
belonged; and every now and then, just as we seem to be sink into 
the calm of security, we fell upon trace of a pursuit after us in all 
directions. once i remember--we must have been nearly three week 
weary walking through unfrequented ways, day after day, not daring to 
make inquiry as to our whereabouts, nor yet to seem purposeless in our 
wanderings--we came to a kind of lonely roadside farrier's and 
blacksmith's. i was so tired, that amante declared that, come what 
might, we would stay there all night; and accordingly she enter the 
house, and bold announce herself as a travel tailor, ready to do 
any odd job of work that might be required, for a night's lodging and 
food for herself and wife. she had adopt this plan once or twice 
before, and with good success; for her father had been a tailor in 
rouen, and as a girl she had often help him with his work, and knew 
the tailors' slang and habits, down to the particular whistle and cry 
which in france tell so much to those of a trade. at this 
blacksmith's, as at most other solitary house far away from a town, 
there was not only a store of men's clothes laid by as wanting mending 
when the housewife could afford time, but there was a natural craving 
after news from a distance, such news as a wandering tailor is bound to 
furnish. the early november afternoon was close into evening, as we 
sat down, she cross-legged on the great table in the blacksmith's 
kitchen, drawn close to the window, i close behind her, sewing at 
another part of the same garment, and from time to time well scold by 
my seeming husband. all at once she turn round to speak to me. it was 
only one word, 'courage!' i had seen nothing; i sat out of the light; 
but i turn sick for an instant, and then i brace myself up into a 
strange strength of endurance to go through i knew not what. 

the blacksmith's forge was in a shed beside the house, and front the 
road. i heard the hammer stop ply their continual rhythmic beat. 
she had seen why they ceased. a rider had come up to the forge and 
dismounted, leading his horse in to be re-shod. the broad red light of 
the forge-fire had reveal the face of the rider to amante, and she 
apprehend the consequence that really ensued. 

the rider, after some words with the blacksmith, was usher in by him 
into the house-place where we sat. 

'here, good wife, a cup of wine and some galette for this gentleman.' 

'anything, anything, madame, that i can eat and drink in my hand while 
my horse is being shod. i am in haste, and must get on to forbach 
to-night.' 

the blacksmith's wife light her lamp; amante had ask her for it 
five minutes before. how thankful we were that she had not more 
speedy comply with our request! as it was, we sat in dusk shadow, 
pretend to stitch away, but scarcely able to see. the lamp was 
placed on the stove, near which my husband, for it was he, stood and 
warm himself. by-and-by he turn round, and look all over the room, 
taking us in with about the same degree of interest as the inanimate 
furniture. amante, cross-legged, front him, stoop over her work, 
whistle soft all the while. he turn again to the stove, 
impatient rubbing his hands. he had finished his wine and galette, 
and want to be off. 

'i am in haste, my good woman. ask thy husband to get on more quickly. 
i will pay him double if he make haste.' 

the woman went out to do his bidding; and he once more turn round to 
face us. amante went on to the second part of the tune. he took it up, 
whistle a second for an instant or so, and then the blacksmith's wife 
re-entering, he move towards her, as if to receive her answer the more 
speedily. 

'one moment, monsieur--only one moment. there was a nail out of the 
off-foreshoe which my husband is replacing; it would delay monsieur 
again if that shoe also came off.' 

'madame is right,' said he, 'but my haste is urgent. if madame knew my 
reasons, she would pardon my impatience. once a happy husband, now a 
desert and betray man, i pursue a wife on whom i lavish all my 
love, but who has abuse my confidence, and flee from my house, 
doubtless to some paramour; carry off with her all the jewel and 
money on which she could lay her hands. it is possible madame may have 
heard or seen something of her; she was accompany in her flight by a 
base, profligate woman from paris, whom i, unhappy man, had myself 
engaged for my wife's waiting-maid, little dream what corruption i 
was bring into my house!' 

'is it possible?' said the good woman, throw up her hands. 

amante went on whistle a little lower, out of respect to the 
conversation. 

'however, i am tracing the wicked fugitives; i am on their track' (and 
the handsome, effeminate face look as ferocious as any demon's). 
'they will not escape me; but every minute is a minute of misery to me, 
till i meet my wife. madame has sympathy, has she not?' 

he drew his face into a hard, unnatural smile, and then both went out 
to the forge, as if once more to hasten the blacksmith over his work. 

amante stop her whistle for one instant. 

'go on as you are, without change of an eyelid even; in a few minutes 
he will be gone, and it will be over!' 

it was a necessary caution, for i was on the point of give way, and 
throw myself weak upon her neck. we went on; she whistle and 
stitching, i making semblance to sew. and it was well we did so; for 
almost directly he came back for his whip, which he had laid down and 
forgotten; and again i felt one of those sharp, quick-scanning glances, 
sent all round the room, and taking in all. 

then we heard him ride away; and then, it had been long too dark to see 
well, i drop my work, and gave way to my tremble and shuddering. 
the blacksmith's wife returned. she was a good creature. amante told 
her i was cold and weary, and she insist on my stopping my work, and 
go to sit near the stove; hastening, at the same time, her 
preparation for supper, which, in honour of us, and of monsieur's 
liberal payment, was to be a little less frugal than ordinary. it was 
well for me that she made me taste a little of the cider-soup she was 
preparing, or i could not have held up, in spite of amante's warning 
look, and the remembrance of her frequent exhortation to act 
resolute up to the character we had assumed, whatever befell. to 
cover my agitation, amante stop her whistling, and began to talk; 
and, by the time the blacksmith came in, she and the good woman of the 
house were in full flow. he began at once upon the handsome gentleman, 
who had paid him so well; all his sympathy was with him, and both he 
and his wife only wish he might overtake his wicked wife, and punish 
her as she deserved. and then the conversation took a turn, not 
uncommon to those whose lives are quiet and monotonous; every one 
seem to vie with each other in telling about some horror; and the 
savage and mysterious band of robber call the chauffeurs, who 
infest all the road leading to the rhine, with schinderhanne at 
their head, furnish many a tale which made the very marrow of my 
bone run cold, and quench even amante's power of talking. her eyes 
grew large and wild, her cheek blanched, and for once she sought by 
her looks help from me. the new call upon me rouse me. i rose and 
said, with their permission my husband and i would seek our bed, for 
that we had travelled far and were early risers. i add that we would 
get up betimes, and finish our piece of work. the blacksmith said we 
should be early bird if we rose before him; and the good wife second 
my proposal with kindly bustle. one other such story as those they had 
been relating, and i do believe amante would have fainted. 

as it was, a night's rest set her up; we arose and finished our work 
betimes, and share the plentiful breakfast of the family. then we had 
to set forth again; only knowing that to forbach we must not go, yet 
believing, as was indeed the case, that forbach lay between us and that 
germany to which we were direct our course. two days more we 
wander on, making a round, i suspect, and return upon the road to 
forbach, a league or two near to that town than the blacksmith's 
house. but as we never made inquiry i hardly knew where we were, when 
we came one night to a small town, with a good large rambling inn in 
the very centre of the principal street. we had begun to feel as if 
there were more safety in town than in the lonely of the country. 
as we had part with a ring of mine not many days before to a 
travel jeweller, who was too glad to purchase it far below its real 
value to make many inquiry as to how it came into the possession of a 
poor working tailor, such as amante seem to be, we resolve to stay 
at this inn all night, and gather such particulars and information as 
we could by which to direct our onward course. 

we took our supper in the darkest corner of the salle-à-manger, have 
previous bargain for a small bedroom across the court, and over the 
stables. we need food sorely; but we hurried on our meal from dread 
of any one enter that public room who might recognize us. just in 
the middle of our meal, the public diligence drove lumber up under 
the _porte-cochère_, and disgorge its passengers. most of them turn 
into the room where we sat, cower and fearful, for the door was 
opposite to the porter's lodge, and both open on to the wide-covered 
entrance from the street. among the passenger came in a young, 
fair-haired lady, attend by an elderly france maid. the poor young 
creature toss her head, and shrank away from the common room, full of 
evil smell and promiscuous company, and demanded, in germany french, to 
be taken to some private apartment. we heard that she and her maid had 
come in the coupé, and, probably from pride, poor young lady! she had 
avoid all association with her fellow-passengers, thereby exciting 
their dislike and ridicule. all these little pieces of hearsay had a 
significance to us afterwards, though, at the time, the only remark 
made that bore upon the future was amante's whisper to me that the 
young lady's hair was exactly the colour of mine, which she had cut off 
and burnt in the stove in the miller's kitchen in one of her descent 
from our hiding-place in the loft. 

as soon as we could, we struck round in the shadow, leave the 
boisterous and merry fellow-passengers to their supper. we cross the 
court, borrow a lantern from the ostler, and scramble up the rude 
step to our chamber above the stable. there was no door into it; the 
entrance was the hole into which the ladder fitted. the window look 
into the court. we were tired and soon fell asleep. i was waken by a 
noise in the stable below. one instant of listening, and i waken 
amante, place my hand on her mouth, to prevent any exclamation in her 
half-roused state. we heard my husband speak about his horse to the 
ostler. it was his voice. i am sure of it. amante said so too. we durst 
not move to rise and satisfy ourselves. for five minutes or so he went 
on give directions. then he left the stable, and, soft steal to 
our window, we saw him cross the court and re-enter the inn. we 
consult as to what we should do. we fear to excite remark or 
suspicion by descend and leave our chamber, or else immediate 
escape was our strongest idea. then the ostler left the stable, lock 
the door on the outside. 

'we must try and drop through the window--if, indeed, it is well to go 
at all,' said amante. 

with reflection came wisdom. we should excite suspicion by leave 
without pay our bill. we were on foot, and might easily be pursued. 
so we sat on our bed's edge, talk and shivering, while from across 
the court the laughter rang merrily, and the company slow disperse 
one by one, their lights flit past the window as they went 
upstairs and settled each one to his rest. 

we crept into our bed, holding each other tight, and listen to every 
sound, as if we thought we were tracked, and might meet our death at 
any moment. in the dead of night, just at the profound still 
preceding the turn into another day, we heard a soft, cautious step 
crossing the yard. the key into the stable was turned--some one came 
into the stable--we felt rather than heard him there. a horse start a 
little, and made a restless movement with his feet, then whinny 
recognition. he who had enter made two or three low sound to the 
animal, and then led him into the court. amante sprang to the window 
with the noiseless activity of a cat. she look out, but dare not 
speak a word. we heard the great door into the street open--a pause for 
mounting, and the horse's footstep were lost in distance. 

then amante came back to me. 'it was he! he is gone!' said she, and 
once more we lay down, tremble and shaking. 

this time we fell sound asleep. we slept long and late. we were waken 
by many hurry feet, and many confuse voices; all the world seem 
awake and astir. we rose and dress ourselves, and coming down we 
look around among the crowd collected in the court-yard, in order to 
assure ourselves _he_ was not there before we left the shelter of the 
stable. 

the instant we were seen, two or three people rush to us. 

'have you heard?--do you know?--that poor young lady--oh, come and 
see!' and so we were hurried, almost in spite of ourselves, across the 
court, and up the great open stairs of the main building of the inn, 
into a bed-chamber, where lay the beautiful young germany lady, so full 
of graceful pride the night before, now white and still in death. by 
her stood the france maid, crying and gesticulating. 

'oh, madame! if you had but suffer me to stay with you! oh! the 
baron, what will he say?' and so she went on. her state had but just 
been discovered; it had been supposed that she was fatigued, and was 
sleep late, until a few minutes before. the surgeon of the town had 
been sent for, and the landlord of the inn was try vain to enforce 
order until he came, and, from time to time, drink little cup of 
brandy, and offering them to the guests, who were all assemble there, 
pretty much as the servant were do in the court-yard. 

at last the surgeon came. all fell back, and hung on the words that 
were to fall from his lips. 

'see!' said the landlord. 'this lady came last night by the diligence 
with her maid. doubtless, a great lady, for she must have a private 
sitting-room--' 

'she was madame the baroness de roeder,' said the france maid. 

--'and was difficult to please in the matter of supper, and a 
sleeping-room. she went to bed well, though fatigued. her maid left 
her--' 

'i beg to be allow to sleep in her room, as we were in a strange 
inn, of the character of which we knew nothing; but she would not let 
me, my mistress was such a great lady.' 

--'and slept with my servants,' continue the landlord. 'this morning 
we thought madame was still slumbering; but when eight, nine, ten, and 
near eleven o'clock came, i bade her maid use my pass-key, and enter 
her room----' 

'the door was not locked, only closed. and here she was found--dead is 
she not, monsieur?--with her face down on her pillow, and her beautiful 
hair all scattered wild; she never would let me tie it up, saying it 
made her head ache. such hair!' said the waiting-maid, lift up a 
long golden tress, and letting it fall again. 

i remember amante's words the night before, and crept close up to 
her. 

meanwhile, the doctor was examine the body underneath the 
bed-clothes, which the landlord, until now, had not allow to be 
disarranged. the surgeon drew out his hand, all bath and stain with 
blood; and holding up a short, sharp knife, with a piece of paper 
fasten round it. 

'here has been foul play,' he said. 'the deceased lady has been 
murdered. this dagger was aim straight at her heart.' then putt on 
his spectacles, he read the writing on the bloody paper, dim and 
horrible obscure as it was: 

numÉro un. 
ainsi le chauffeur se vengent. 

'let us go!' said i to amante. 'oh, let us leave this horrible place!' 

'wait a little,' said she. 'only a few minutes more. it will be 
better.' 

immediately the voice of all proclaim their suspicion of the 
cavalier who had arrive last the night before. he had, they said, made 
so many inquiry about the young lady, whose supercilious conduct all 
in the _salle-à-manger_ had been discuss on his entrance. they were 
talk about her as we left the room; he must have come in directly 
afterwards, and not until he had learnt all about her, had he spoken of 
the business which necessitate his departure at dawn of day, and made 
his arrangement with both landlord and ostler for the possession of 
the key of the stable and _porte-cochère_. in short, there was no 
doubt as to the murderer, even before the arrival of the legal 
functionary who had been sent for by the surgeon; but the word on the 
paper chill every one with terror. le chauffeurs, who were they? no 
one knew, some of the gang might even then be in the room overhearing, 
and note down fresh object for vengeance. in germany, i had heard 
little of this terrible gang, and i had paid no greater heed to the 
story related once or twice about them in carlsruhe than one do to 
tale about ogres. but here in their very haunts, i learnt the full 
amount of the terror they inspired. no one would be legal responsible 
for any evidence criminate the murderer. the public prosecutor shrank 
from the duty of his office. what do i say? neither amante nor i, 
knowing far more of the actual guilt of the man who had kill that 
poor sleep young lady, durst breathe a word. we appear to be 
wholly ignorant of everything: we, who might have told so much. but how 
could we? we were broken down with terrific anxiety and fatigue, with 
the knowledge that we, above all, were doom victims; and that the
