blood, heavy dripping from the bed-clothes on to the floor, was 
dripping thus out of the poor dead body, because, when living, she had 
been mistaken for me. 

at length amante went up to the landlord, and ask permission to leave 
his inn, do all openly and humbly, so as to excite neither ill-will 
nor suspicion. indeed, suspicion was otherwise directed, and he 
willing gave us leave to depart. a few days afterwards we were across 
the rhine, in germany, making our way towards frankfort, but still 
keeping our disguises, and amante still working at her trade. 

on the way, we met a young man, a wandering journeyman from heidelberg. 
i knew him, although i did not choose that he should know me. i ask 
him, as careless as i could, how the old miller was now? he told me 
he was dead. this realization of the worst apprehension cause by his 
long silence shock me inexpressibly. it seem as though every prop 
gave way from under me. i had been talk to amante only that very day 
of the safety and comfort of the home that await her in my father's 
house; of the gratitude which the old man would feel towards her; and 
how there, in that peaceful dwelling, far away from the terrible land 
of france, she should find ease and security for all the rest of her 
life. all this i thought i had to promise, and even yet more had i 
look for, for myself. i look to the unburden of my heart and 
conscience by telling all i knew to my best and wisest friend. i look 
to his love as a sure guidance as well as a comfort stay, and, 
behold, he was gone away from me for ever! 

i had left the room hasty on hearing of this sad news from the 
heidelberger. presently, amante followed. 

'poor madame,' said she, console me to the best of her ability. and 
then she told me by degree what more she had learned respecting my 
home, about which she knew almost as much as i did, from my frequent 
talks on the subject both at le rocher and on the dreary, doleful 
road we had come along. she had continue the conversation after i 
left, by ask about my brother and his wife. of course, they live on 
at the mill, but the man said (with what truth i know not, but i 
believe it firm at the time) that babette had completely got the 
upper hand of my brother, who only saw through her eyes and heard with 
her ears. that there had been much heidelberg gossip of late days about 
her sudden intimacy with a grand france gentleman who had appear at 
the mill--a relation, by marriage--married, in fact, to the miller's 
sister, who, by all accounts, had behave abominable and ungratefully. 
but that was no reason for babette's extreme and sudden intimacy with 
him, go about everywhere with the france gentleman; and since he 
left (as the heidelberger said he knew for a fact) corresponding with 
him constantly. yet her husband saw no harm in it all, seemingly; 
though, to be sure, he was so out of spirits, what with his father's 
death and the news of his sister's infamy, that he hardly knew how to 
hold up his head. 

'now,' said amante, 'all this prove that m. de la tourelle has 
suspect that you would go back to the nest in which you were reared, 
and that he has been there, and found that you have not yet returned; 
but probably he still imagine that you will do so, and has accordingly 
engaged your sister-in-law as a kind of informant. madame has said that 
her sister-in-law bore her no extreme good-will; and the defamatory 
story he has got the start of us in spreading, will not tend to 
increase the favour in which your sister-in-law hold you. no doubt the 
assassin was retrace his steps when we met him near forbach, and 
have heard of the poor germany lady, with her france maid, and her 
pretty blonde complexion, he follow her. if madame will still be 
guide by me--and, my child, i beg of you still to trust me,' said 
amante, break out of her respectful formality into the way of 
talk more natural to those who had share and escape from common 
dangers--more natural, too, where the speaker was conscious of a power 
of protection which the other did not possess--'we will go on to 
frankfort, and lose ourselves, for a time, at least, in the numbers of 
people who throng a great town; and you have told me that frankfort is 
a great town. we will still be husband and wife; we will take a small 
lodging, and you shall house-keep and live in-doors. i, as the rough 
and the more alert, will continue my father's trade, and seek work at 
the tailors' shops.' 

i could think of no better plan, so we follow this out. in a back 
street at frankfort we found two furnish rooms to let on a sixth 
story. the one we enter had no light from day; a dingy lamp swung 
perpetual from the ceiling, and from that, or from the open door 
leading into the bedroom beyond, came our only light. the bedroom was 
more cheerful, but very small. such as it was, it almost exceed our 
possible means. the money from the sale of my ring was almost 
exhausted, and amante was a stranger in the place, speak only 
french, moreover, and the good germany were hate the france people 
right heartily. however, we succeed better than our hopes, and even 
laid by a little against the time of my confinement. i never stir 
abroad, and saw no one, and amante's want of knowledge of germany kept 
her in a state of comparative isolation. 

at length my child was born--my poor worse than fatherless child. it 
was a girl, as i had pray for. i had fear lest a boy might have 
something of the tiger nature of its father, but a girl seem all my 
own. and yet not all my own, for the faithful amante's delight and 
glory in the babe almost exceed mine; in outward show it certainly 
did. 

we had not been able to afford any attendance beyond what a 
neighbouring sage-femme could give, and she came frequently, bring 
in with her a little store of gossip, and wonderful tale cull out of 
her own experience, every time. one day she began to tell me about a 
great lady in whose service her daughter had live as scullion, or some 
such thing. such a beautiful lady! with such a handsome husband. but 
grief come to the palace as well as to the garret, and why or 
wherefore no one knew, but somehow the baron de roeder must have 
incur the vengeance of the terrible chauffeurs; for not many month 
ago, as madame was go to see her relations in alsace, she was 
stab dead as she lay in bed at some hotel on the road. had i not 
seen it in the _gazette_? had i not heard? why, she had been told that 
as far off as lyon there were placard offering a heavy reward on the 
part of the baron de roeder for information respecting the murder of 
his wife. but no one could help him, for all who could bear evidence 
were in such terror of the chauffeurs; there were hundred of them she 
had been told, rich and poor, great gentlemen and peasants, all league 
together by most frightful oath to hunt to the death any one who bore 
witness against them; so that even they who survive the torture to 
which the chauffeur subject many of the people whom they plundered, 
dare not to recognise them again, would not dare, even did they see 
them at the bar of a court of justice; for, if one were condemned, were 
there not hundred sworn to avenge his death? 

i told all this to amante, and we began to fear that if m. de la 
tourelle, or lefebvre, or any of the gang at le rochers, had seen 
these placards, they would know that the poor lady stab by the 
former was the baroness de roeder, and that they would set forth again 
in search of me. 

this fresh apprehension told on my health and impede my recovery. we 
had so little money we could not call in a physician, at least, not one 
in establish practice. but amante found out a young doctor for whom, 
indeed, she had sometimes worked; and offering to pay him in kind, she 
brought him to see me, her sick wife. he was very gentle and 
thoughtful, though, like ourselves, very poor. but he gave much time 
and consideration to the case, saying once to amante that he saw my 
constitution had experienced some severe shock from which it was 
probable that my nerves would never entire recover. by-and-by i shall 
name this doctor, and then you will know, better than i can describe, 
his character. 

i grew strong in time--stronger, at least. i was able to work a little 
at home, and to sun myself and my baby at the garret-window in the 
roof. it was all the air i dare to take. i constant wore the 
disguise i had first set out with; as constant had i renew the 
disfigure dye which change my hair and complexion. but the perpetual 
state of terror in which i had been during the whole month succeed 
my escape from le rocher made me loathe the idea of ever again 
walking in the open daylight, expose to the sight and recognition of 
every passer-by. in vain amante reasoned--in vain the doctor urged. 
docile in every other thing, in this i was obstinate. i would not stir 
out. one day amante return from her work, full of news--some of it 
good, some such as to cause us apprehension. the good news was this; 
the master for whom she work as journeyman was go to send her with 
some other to a great house at the other side of frankfort, where 
there were to be private theatricals, and where many new dress and 
much alteration of old one would be required. the tailor employ 
were all to stay at this house until the day of representation was 
over, as it was at some distance from the town, and no one could tell 
when their work would be ended. but the pay was to be proportionate 
good. 

the other thing she had to say was this: she had that day met the 
travel jeweller to whom she and i had sold my ring. it was rather a 
peculiar one, given to me by my husband; we had felt at the time that 
it might be the means of tracing us, but we were penniless and 
starving, and what else could we do? she had seen that this frenchman 
had recognise her at the same instant that she did him, and she 
thought at the same time that there was a gleam of more than common 
intelligence on his face as he did so. this idea had been confirmed by 
his following her for some way on the other side of the street; but she 
had evade him with her better knowledge of the town, and the 
increase dark of the night. still it was well that she was go 
to such a distance from our dwelling on the next day; and she had 
brought me in a stock of provisions, beg me to keep within doors, 
with a strange kind of fearful oblivion of the fact that i had never 
set foot beyond the threshold of the house since i had first enter 
it--scarce ever venture down the stairs. but, although my poor, my 
dear, very faithful amante was like one possessed that last night, she 
spoke continual of the dead, which is a bad sign for the living. she 
kiss you--yes! it was you, my daughter, my darling, whom i bore 
beneath my bosom away from the fearful castle of your father--i call 
him so for the first time, i must call him so once again before i have 
done--amante kiss you, sweet baby, blessed little comforter, as if 
she never could leave off. and then she went away, alive. 

two days, three days pass away. that third evening i was sitting 
within my bolt doors--you asleep on your pillow by my side--when a 
step came up the stair, and i knew it must be for me; for ours were the 
top-most rooms. some one knocked; i held my very breath. but some one 
spoke, and i knew it was the good doctor voss. then i crept to the 
door, and answered. 

'are you alone?' ask i. 

'yes,' said he, in a still lower voice. 'let me in.' i let him in, and 
he was as alert as i in bolt and barring the door. then he came and 
whisper to me his doleful tale. he had come from the hospital in the 
opposite quarter of the town, the hospital which he visited; he should 
have been with me sooner, but he had fear lest he should be watched. 
he had come from amante's death-bed. her fear of the jeweller were too 
well founded. she had left the house where she was employ that 
morning, to transact some errand connected with her work in the town; 
she must have been followed, and dogged on her way back through 
solitary wood-paths, for some of the wood-rangers belong to the 
great house had found her lie there, stab to death, but not dead; 
with the poniard again plunge through the fatal writing, once more; 
but this time with the word 'un' underlined, so as to show that the 
assassin was aware of his precious mistake. 

numéro _un_. 
ainsi le chauffeur se vengent. 

they had carry her to the house, and given her restorative till she 
had recover the feeble use of her speech. but, oh, faithful, dear 
friend and sister! even then she remember me, and refuse to tell 
(what no one else among her fellow workmen knew), where she live or 
with whom. life was ebb away fast, and they had no resource but to 
carry her to the nearest hospital, where, of course, the fact of her 
sex was made known. fortunately both for her and for me, the doctor in 
attendance was the very doctor voss whom we already knew. to him, while 
await her confessor, she told enough to enable him to understand the 
position in which i was left; before the priest had heard half her tale 
amante was dead. 

doctor voss told me he had made all sort of _détours_, and wait 
thus, late at night, for fear of being watch and followed. but i do 
not think he was. at any rate, as i afterwards learnt from him, the 
baron roeder, on hearing of the similitude of this murder with that of 
his wife in every particular, made such a search after the assassins, 
that, although they were not discovered, they were compel to take to 
flight for the time. 

i can hardly tell you now by what argument dr. voss, at first merely 
my benefactor, sparing me a portion of his small modicum, at length 
persuade me to become his wife. his wife he call it, i call it; 
for we went through the religious ceremony too much slight at the 
time, and as we were both lutherans, and m. de la tourelle had 
pretended to be of the reform religion, a divorce from the latter 
would have been easily procure by germany law both ecclesiastical and 
legal, could we have summon so fearful a man into any court. 

the good doctor took me and my child by stealth to his modest dwelling; 
and there i live in the same deep refinement, never seeing the full 
light of day, although when the dye had once pass away from my face 
my husband did not wish me to renew it. there was no need; my yellow 
hair was grey, my complexion was ashen-coloured, no creature could have 
recognize the fresh-coloured, bright-haired young woman of eighteen 
month before. the few people whom i saw knew me only as madame voss; a 
widow much old than himself, whom dr. voss had secret married. they 
call me the grey woman. 

he made me give you his surname. till now you have known no other 
father--while he live you need no father's love. once only, only 
once more, did the old terror come upon me. for some reason which i 
forget, i broke through my usual custom, and went to the window of my 
room for some purpose, either to shut or to open it. look out into 
the street for an instant, i was fascinate by the sight of m. de la 
tourelle, gay, young, elegant as ever, walking along on the opposite 
side of the street. the noise i had made with the window cause him to 
look up; he saw me, an old grey woman, and he did not recognize me! yet 
it was not three years since we had parted, and his eyes were keen and 
dreadful like those of the lynx. 

i told m. voss, on his return home, and he try to cheer me, but the 
shock of seeing m. de la tourelle had been too terrible for me. i was 
ill for long month afterwards. 

once again i saw him. dead. he and lefebvre were at last caught; hunt 
down by the baron de roeder in some of their crimes. dr. voss had heard 
of their arrest; their condemnation, their death; but he never said a 
word to me, until one day he bade me show him that i love him by my 
obedience and my trust. he took me a long carriage journey, where to i 
know not, for we never spoke of that day again; i was led through a 
prison, into a closed court-yard, where, decent drape in the last 
robe of death, conceal the mark of decapitation, lay m. de la 
tourelle, and two or three others, whom i had known at le rochers. 

after that conviction dr. voss try to persuade me to return to a more 
natural mode of life, and to go out more. but although i sometimes 
comply with his wish, yet the old terror was ever strong upon me, and 
he, seeing what an effort it was, gave up urge me at last. 

you know all the rest. how we both mourn bitter the loss of that 
dear husband and father--for such i will call him ever--and as such you 
must consider him, my child, after this one revelation is over. 

why has it been made, you ask. for this reason, my child. the lover, 
whom you have only known as m. lebrun, a france artist, told me but 
yesterday his real name, drop because the blood-thirsty republican 
might consider it as too aristocratic. it is maurice de poissy. 




curious, if true 

(extract from a letter from richard whittingham, esq.) 


you were formerly so much amuse at my pride in my descent from that 
sister of calvin's, who married a whittingham, dean of durham, that i 
doubt if you will be able to enter into the regard for my distinguished 
relation that has led me to france, in order to examine register and 
archives, which, i thought, might enable me to discover collateral 
descendant of the great reformer, with whom i might call cousins. i 
shall not tell you of my trouble and adventure in this research; you 
are not worthy to hear of them; but something so curious befell me one 
evening last august, that if i had not been perfectly certain i was 
wide awake, i might have taken it for a dream. 

for the purpose i have named, it was necessary that i should make tour 
my head-quarters for a time. i had trace descendant of the calvin 
family out of normandy into the centre of france; but i found it was 
necessary to have a kind of permission from the bishop of the diocese 
before i could see certain family papers, which had fallen into the 
possession of the church; and, as i had several english friends at 
tours, i await the answer to my request to monseigneur de----, at 
that town. i was ready to accept any invitation; but i received very 
few; and was sometimes a little at a loss what to do with my evenings. 
the _table d'hôte_ was at five o'clock; i did not wish to go to the 
expense of a private sitting-room, dislike the dinnery atmosphere of 
the _salle à manger_, could not play either at pool or billiards, and 
the aspect of my fellow guest was unprepossess enough to make me 
unwill to enter into any _tête-à-tête_ gamble with them. so i 
usually rose from table early, and try to make the most of the 
remain light of the august evenings in walking brisk off to 
explore the surrounding country; the middle of the day was too hot for 
this purpose, and better employ in lounge on a bench in the 
boulevards, lazy listen to the distant band, and notice with 
equal lazy the face and figures of the women who pass by. 

one thursday evening, the 18th of august it was, i think, i had gone 
further than usual in my walk, and i found that it was later than i had 
imagine when i pause to turn back. i fancy i could make a round; i 
had enough notion of the direction in which i was, to see that by 
turning up a narrow straight lane to my left i should shorten my way 
back to tours. and so i believe i should have done, could i have found 
an outlet at the right place, but field-paths are almost unknown in 
that part of france, and my lane, stiff and straight as any street, and 
marked into terribly vanish perspective by the regular row of 
poplar on each side, seem interminable. of course night came on, and 
i was in darkness. in england i might have had a chance of seeing a 
light in some cottage only a field or two off, and ask my way from 
the inhabitants; but here i could see no such welcome sight; indeed, i 
believe france peasant go to bed with the summer daylight, so if there 
were any habitation in the neighbourhood i never saw them. at last--i 
believe i must have walk two hour in the darkness,--i saw the dusky 
outline of a wood on one side of the weariful lane, and, impatient 
careless of all forest law and penalty for trespassers, i made my 
way to it, thinking that if the worst came to the worst, i could find 
some covert--some shelter where i could lie down and rest, until the 
morning light gave me a chance of finding my way back to tours. but the 
plantation, on the outskirts of what appear to me a dense wood, was 
of young trees, too close plant to be more than slender stem 
grow up to a good height, with scanty foliage on their summits. on i 
went towards the thick forest, and once there i slacken my pace, 
and began to look about me for a good lair. i was as dainty as 
lochiel's grandchild, who made his grandsire indignant at the luxury of 
his pillow of snow: this brake was too full of brambles, that felt damp 
with dew; there was no hurry, since i had given up all hope of passing 
the night between four walls; and i went leisurely grope about, and 
trust that there were no wolf to be poke up out of their summer 
drowsy by my stick, when all at once i saw a château before me, not 
a quarter of a mile off, at the end of what seem to be an ancient 
avenue (now overgrown and irregular), which i happen to be crossing, 
when i look to my right, and saw the welcome sight. large, stately, 
and dark was its outline against the dusky night-sky; there were 
pepper-boxes and tourelle and what-not fantastic go up into the 
dim starlight. and more to the purpose still, though i could not see 
the detail of the building that i was now facing, it was plain enough 
that there were lights in many windows, as if some great entertainment 
was go on. 

'they are hospitable people, at any rate,' thought i. 'perhaps they 
will give me a bed. i don't suppose france propriétaires have trap and 
horse quite as plentiful as english gentlemen; but they are evidently 
have a large party, and some of their guest may be from tours, and 
will give me a cast back to the lion d'or. i am not proud, and i am 
dog-tired. i am not above hanging on behind, if need be.' 

so, putt a little brisk and spirit into my walk, i went up to 
the door, which was standing open, most hospitably, and showing a large 
light hall, all hung round with spoils of the chase, armour, &c., the 
detail of which i had not time to notice, for the instant i stood on 
the threshold a huge porter appeared, in a strange, old-fashioned 
dress, a kind of livery which well befit the general appearance of 
the house. he ask me, in france (so curious pronounced that i 
thought i had hit upon a new kind of patois), my name, and whence i 
came. i thought he would not be much the wiser, still it was but civil 
to give it before i made my request for assistance; so, in reply, i 
said: 

'my name is whittingham--richard whittingham, an english gentleman, 
stay at ----.' to my infinite surprise, a light of pleased 
intelligence came over the giant's face; he made me a low bow, and said 
(still in the same curious dialect) that i was welcome, that i was long 
expected. 

'long expected!' what could the fellow mean? had i stumble on a nest 
of relations by john calvin's side, who had heard of my genealogy 
inquiries, and were gratify and interested by them? but i was too 
much pleased to be under shelter for the night to think it necessary to 
account for my agreeable reception before i enjoy it. just as he was 
opening the great heavy _battants_ of the door that led from the hall 
to the interior, he turn round and said: 

'apparently monsieur le géanquilleur is not come with you.' 

'no! i am all alone; i have lost my way,'--and i was go on with my 
explanation, when he, as if quite indifferent to it, led the way up a 
great stone staircase, as wide as many rooms, and have on each 
landing-place massive iron wickets, in a heavy framework; these the 
porter unlock with the solemn slow of age. indeed, a strange, 
mysterious awe of the century that had pass away since this château 
was built, came over me as i wait for the turning of the ponderous 
key in the ancient locks. i could almost have fancy that i heard a 
mighty rush murmur (like the ceaseless sound of a distant sea, 
ebb and flowing for ever and for ever), coming forth from the great 
vacant gallery that open out on each side of the broad staircase, 
and were to be dim perceive in the dark above us. it was as if 
the voice of generation of men yet echo and eddy in the silent 
air. it was strange, too, that my friend the porter go before me, 
ponderous infirm, with his feeble old hand strive in vain to keep 
the tall flambeau he held steady before him,--strange, i say, that he 
was the only domestic i saw in the vast hall and passages, or met with 
on the grand staircase. at length we stood before the gilded door that 
led into the saloon where the family--or it might be the company, so 
great was the buzz of voices--was assembled. i would have remonstrate 
when i found he was go to introduce me, dusty and travel-smeared, in 
a morning costume that was not even my best, into this grand _salon_, 
with nobody knew how many ladies and gentlemen assembled; but the 
obstinate old man was evidently bent upon taking me straight to his 
master, and paid no heed to my words. 

the door flew open, and i was usher into a saloon curious full of 
pale light, which did not culminate on any spot, nor proceed from any 
centre, nor flicker with any motion of the air, but fill every nook 
and corner, making all things delicious distinct; different from our 
light of gas or candle, as is the difference between a clear southern 
atmosphere and that of our misty england. 

at the first moment, my arrival excited no attention, the apartment was 
so full of people, all intent on their own conversation. but my friend 
the porter went up to a handsome lady of middle age, richly attire in 
that antique manner which fashion has brought round again of late 
years, and, wait first in an attitude of deep respect till her 
attention fell upon him, told her my name and something about me, as 
far as i could guess from the gesture of the one and the sudden glance 
of the eye of the other. 

she immediately came towards me with the most friendly action of 
greeting, even before she had advanced near enough to speak. then,--and 
was it not strange?--her words and accent were that of the commonest 
peasant of the country. yet she herself look highbred, and would have 
been dignified had she been a shade less restless, had her countenance 
worn a little less lively and inquisitive expression. i had been poke 
a good deal about the old parts of tours, and had had to understand the 
dialect of the people who dwelt in the marché au vendredi and similar 
places, or i really should not have understood my handsome hostess, as 
she offer to present me to her husband, a henpecked, gentlemanly man, 
who was more quaint attire than she in the very extreme of that 
style of dress. i thought to myself that in france, as in england, it 
is the provincial who carry fashion to such an excess as to become 
ridiculous. 

however, he spoke (still in the _patois_) of his pleasure in making my 
acquaintance, and led me to a strange uneasy easy-chair, much of a 
piece with the rest of the furniture, which might have taken its place 
without any anachronism by the side of that in the hôtel cluny. then 
again began the clatter of france voices, which my arrival had for an 
instant interrupted, and i had leisure to look about me. opposite to me 
sat a very sweet-looking lady, who must have been a great beauty in her 
youth, i should think, and would be charming in old age, from the 
sweet of her countenance. she was, however, extremely fat, and on 
seeing her feet laid up before her on a cushion, i at once perceive 
that they were so swollen as to render her incapable of walking, which 
probably brought on her excessive _embonpoint_. her hand were plump 
and small, but rather coarse-grained in texture, not quite so clean as 
they might have been, and altogether not so aristocratic-looking as the 
charming face. her dress was of superb black velvet, ermine-trimmed, 
with diamond thrown all abroad over it. 

not far from her stood the least little man i had ever seen; of such 
admirable proportions no one could call him a dwarf, because with that 
word we usually associate something of deformity; but yet with an elfin 
look of shrewd, hard, worldly wisdom in his face that mar the 
impression which his delicate regular little features would otherwise 
have conveyed. indeed, i do not think he was quite of equal rank with 
the rest of the company, for his dress was inappropriate to the 
occasion (and he apparently was an invited, while i was an involuntary 
guest); and one or two of his gesture and action were more like the 
trick of an uneducated rustic than anything else. to explain what i 
mean: his boots had evidently seen much service, and had been 
re-topped, re-heeled, re-soled to the extent of cobbler's powers. why 
should he have come in them if they were not his best--his only pair? 
and what can be more ungenteel than poverty? then again he had an 
uneasy trick of putt his hand up to his throat, as if he expect to 
find something the matter with it; and he had the awkward habit--which 
i do not think he could have copy from dr. johnson, because most 
probably he had never heard of him--of try always to retrace his 
steps on the exact boards on which he had trodden to arrive at any 
particular part of the room. besides, to settle the question, i once 
heard him address as monsieur poucet, without any aristocratic 'de' 
for a prefix; and nearly every one else in the room was a marquis, at 
any rate. 

i say, 'nearly every one;' for some strange people had the entrée; 
unless, indeed, they were, like me, benighted. one of the guest i 
should have taken for a servant, but for the extraordinary influence he 
seem to have over the man i took for his master, and who never did 
anything without, apparently, being urge thereto by this follower. the 
master, magnificent dressed, but ill at ease in his clothes as if 
they had been made for some one else, was a weak-looking, handsome man, 
continual saunter about, and i almost guess an object of 
suspicion to some of the gentlemen present, which, perhaps, drove him 
on the companionship of his follower, who was dress something in the 
style of an ambassador's chasseur; yet it was not a chasseur's dress 
after all; it was something more thorough old-world; boots half way 
up his ridiculous small legs, which clatter as he walk along, as 
if they were too large for his little feet; and a great quantity of 
grey fur, as trimming to coat, court mantle, boots, cap--everything. 
you know the way in which certain countenance remind you perpetual 
of some animal, be it bird or beast! well, this chasseur (as i will 
call him for want of a better name) was exceedingly like the great 
tom-cat that you have seen so often in my chambers, and laugh at 
almost as often for his uncanny gravity of demeanour. grey whiskers has 
my tom--grey whiskers had the chasseur: grey hair overshadow the upper 
lip of my tom--grey mustachio hid that of the chasseur. the pupil of 
tom's eyes dilate and contract as i had thought cats' pupil only could 
do, until i saw those of the chasseur. to be sure, canny as tom is, the 
chasseur had the advantage in the more intelligent expression. he 
seem to have obtain most complete sway over his master or patron, 
whose looks he watched, and whose steps he followed, with a kind of 
distrustful interest that puzzle me greatly. 

there were several other group in the more distant part of the saloon, 
all of the stately old school, all grand and noble, i conjecture from 
their bearing. they seem perfectly well acquaint with each other, 
as if they were in the habit of meeting. but i was interrupt in my 
observations by the tiny little gentleman on the opposite side of the 
room coming across to take a place beside me. it is no difficult matter 
to a frenchman to slide into conversation, and so graceful did my 
pigmy friend keep up the character of the nation, that we were almost 
confidential before ten minutes had elapsed. 

now i was quite aware that the welcome which all had extend to me, 
from the porter up to the vivacious lady and meek lord of the castle, 
was intended for some other person. but it require either a degree of 
moral courage, of which i cannot boast, or the self-reliance and 
conversational powers of a bold and clever man than i, to undeceive 
people who had fallen into so fortunate a mistake for me. yet the 
little man by my side insinuate himself so much into my confidence, 
that i had half a mind to tell him of my exact situation, and to turn 
him into a friend and an ally. 

'madame is perceptible grow older,' said he, in the midst of my 
perplexity, glancing at our hostess. 

'madame is still a very fine woman,' reply i. 

'now, is it not strange,' continue he, lower his voice, 'how women 
almost invariable praise the absent, or departed, as if they were 
angel of light while as for the present, or the living'--here he 
shrug up his little shoulders, and made an expressive pause. 'would 
you believe it! madame is always praise her late husband to 
monsieur's face; till, in fact, we guest are quite perplexed how to 
look: for, you know, the late m. de retz's character was quite 
notorious,--everybody has heard of him.' all the world of touraine, 
thought i, but i made an assent noise. 

at this instant, monsieur our host came up to me, and with a civil look 
of tender interest (such as some people put on when they inquire after 
your mother, about whom they do not care one straw), ask if i had 
heard lately how my cat was? 'how my cat was!' what could the man mean? 
my cat! could he mean the tailless tom, born in the isle of man, and 
now supposed to be keeping guard against the incursion of rats and 
mice into my chambers in london? tom is, as you know, on pretty good 
terms with some of my friends, use their leg for rubbing-posts 
without scruple, and highly esteem by them for his gravity of 
demeanour, and wise manner of wink his eyes. but could his fame have 
reach across the channel? however, an answer must be return to the 
inquiry, as monsieur's face was bent down to mine with a look of polite 
anxiety; so i, in my turn, assume an expression of gratitude, and 
assured him that, to the best of my belief, my cat was in remarkably 
good health. 

'and the climate agree with her?' 

'perfectly,' said i, in a maze of wonder at this deep solicitude in a 
tailless cat who had lost one foot and half an ear in some cruel trap. 
my host smile a sweet smile, and, address a few words to my little 
neighbour, pass on. 

'how wearisome those aristocrat are!' quoth my neighbour, with a 
slight sneer. 'monsieur's conversation rarely extend to more than two 
sentence to any one. by that time his faculty are exhausted, and he 
needs the refreshment of silence. you and i, monsieur, are, at any 
rate, indebted to our own wit for our rise in the world!' 

here again i was bewildered! as you know, i am rather proud of my 
descent from family which, if not noble themselves, are allied to 
nobility,--and as to my 'rise in the world'--if i had risen, it would 
have been rather for balloon-like quality than for mother-wit, to 
being unencumbered with heavy ballast either in my head or my pockets. 
however, it was my cue to agree: so i smile again. 

'for my part,' said he, 'if a man do not stick at trifles, if he 
know how to judicious add to, or withhold facts, and is not 
sentimental in his parade of humanity, he is sure to do well; sure to 
affix a _de_ or _von_ to his name, and end his days in comfort. there 
is an example of what i am saying'--and he glance furtive at the 
weak-looking master of the sharp, intelligent servant, whom i have 
call the chasseur. 

'monsieur le marquis would never have been anything but a miller's son, 
if it had not been for the talent of his servant. of course you know 
his antecedents?' 

i was go to make some remark on the change in the order of the 
peerage since the days of louis xvi.--going, in fact, to be very 
sensible and historical--when there was a slight commotion among the 
people at the other end of the room. lacquey in quaint livery must 
have come in from behind the tapestry, i suppose (for i never saw them 
enter, though i sate right opposite to the doors), and were hand 
about the slight beverage and slight viands which are considered 
sufficient refreshments, but which look rather meagre to my hungry 
appetite. these footmen were standing solemn opposite to a 
lady,--beautiful, splendid as the dawn, but--sound asleep in a 
magnificent settee. a gentleman who show so much irritation at her 
ill-timed slumbers, that i think he must have been her husband, was 
try to awaken her with action not far remove from shakings. all in 
vain; she was quite unconscious of his annoyance, or the smile of the 
company, or the automatic solemnity of the wait footman, or the 
perplexed anxiety of monsieur and madame. 

my little friend sat down with a sneer, as if his curiosity was 
quench in contempt. 

'moralists would make an infinity of wise remark on that scene,' said 
he. 'in the first place, note the ridiculous position into which their 
superstitious reverence for rank and title put all these people. 
because monsieur is a reign prince over some minute principality, 
the exact situation of which no one has as yet discovered, no one must 
venture to take their glass of eau sucré till madame la princesse 
awakens; and, judge from past experience, those poor lacquey may 
have to stand for a century before that happens. next--always speak 
as a moralist, you will observe--note how difficult it is to break off 
bad habit acquire in youth!' 

just then the prince succeeded, by what means i did not see, in awake 
the beautiful sleeper. but at first she did not remember where she was, 
and look up at her husband with loving eyes, she smile and said: 

'is it you, my prince?' 

but he was too conscious of the suppress amusement of the spectator 
and his own consequent annoyance, to be reciprocal tender, and turn 
away with some little france expression, best render into english by 
'pooh, pooh, my dear!' 

after i had had a glass of delicious wine of some unknown quality, my 
courage was in rather better plight than before, and i told my cynical 
little neighbour--whom i must say i was beginning to dislike--that i 
had lost my way in the wood, and had arrive at the château quite by 
mistake. 

he seem mightily amuse at my story; said that the same thing had 
happen to himself more than once; and told me that i had better luck 
than he had on one of these occasions, when, from his account, he must 
have been in considerable danger of his life. he end his story by 
making me admire his boots, which he said he still wore, patch though 
they were, and all their excellent quality lost by patching, because 
they were of such a first-rate make for long pedestrian excursions. 
'though, indeed,' he wound up by saying, 'the new fashion of railroad 
would seem to supersede the necessity for this description of boots.' 

when i consult him as to whether i ought to make myself known to my 
host and hostess as a benighted traveller, instead of the guest whom 
they had taken me for, he exclaimed, 'by no means! i hate such 
squeamish morality.' and he seem much offend by my innocent 
question, as if it seem by implication to condemn something in 
himself. he was offend and silent; and just at this moment i caught 
the sweet, attractive eyes of the lady opposite--that lady whom i name 
at first as being no long in the bloom of youth, but as being 
somewhat infirm about the feet, which were support on a raise 
cushion before her. her looks seem to say, 'come here, and let us 
have some conversation together;' and, with a bow of silent excuse to 
my little companion, i went across to the lame old lady. she 
acknowledge my coming with the prettiest gesture of thanks possible; 
and, half apologetically, said, 'it is a little dull to be unable to 
move about on such evenings as this; but it is a just punishment to me 
for my early vanities. my poor feet, that were by nature so small, are 
now taking their revenge for my cruelty in force them into such 
little slipper ... besides, monsieur,' with a pleasant smile, 'i 
thought it was possible you might be weary of the malicious saying of 
your little neighbour. he has not borne the best character in his 
youth, and such men are sure to be cynical in their old age.' 

'who is he?' ask i, with english abruptness. 

'his name is poucet, and his father was, i believe, a woodcutter, or 
charcoal burner, or something of the sort. they do tell sad story of 
connivance at murder, ingratitude, and obtain money on false 
pretences--but you will think me as bad as he if i go on with my 
slanders. rather let us admire the lovely lady coming up towards us, 
with the rose in her hand--i never see her without roses, they are so 
close connected with her past history, as you are doubtless aware. 
ah, beauty!' said my companion to the lady drawing near to us, 'it is 
like you to come to me, now that i can no long go to you.' then 
turning to me, and graceful drawing me into the conversation, she 
said, 'you must know that, although we never met until we were both 
married, we have been almost like sister ever since. there have been 
so many points of resemblance in our circumstances, and i think i may 
say in our characters. we had each two elder sisters--mine were but 
half-sisters, though--who were not so kind to us as they might have 
been.' 

'but have been sorry for it since,' put in the other lady. 

'since we have married princes,' continue the same lady, with an arch 
smile that had nothing of unkind in it, 'for we both have married 
far above our original station in life; we are both unpunctual in our 
habits, and, in consequence of this failing of ours, we have both had 
to suffer mortification and pain.' 

'and both are charming,' said a whisper close behind me. 'my lord the 
marquis, say it--say, "and both are charming."' 

'and both are charming,' was spoken aloud by another voice. i turned, 
and saw the wily cat-like chasseur, prompt his master to make civil 
speeches. 

the ladies bowed with that kind of haughty acknowledgement which show 
that compliments from such a source are distasteful. but our trio of 
conversation was broken up, and i was sorry for it. the marquis look 
as if he had been stir up to make that one speech, and hope that he 
would not be expect to say more; while behind him stood the chasseur, 
half impertinent and half servile in his ways and attitudes. the 
ladies, who were real ladies, seem to be sorry for the awkward of 
the marquis, and address some trifling question to him, adapt 
themselves to the subject on which he could have no trouble in 
answering. the chasseur, meanwhile, was talk to himself in a 
growl tone of voice. i had fallen a little into the background at 
this interrupt in a conversation which promise to be so pleasant, 
and i could not help hearing his words. 

'really, de caraba grow more stupid every day. i have a great mind to 
throw off his boots, and leave him to his fate. i was intended for a 
court, and to a court i will go, and make my own fortune as i have made 
his. the emperor will appreciate my talents.' 

and such are the habit of the french, or such his forgetful of 
good manners in his anger, that he spat right and left on the 
parquet floor. 

just then a very ugly, very pleasant-looking man, came towards the two 
ladies to whom i had lately been speaking, leading up to them a 
delicate, fair woman, dress all in the softest white, as if she were 
_vouée au blanc_. i do not think there was a bit of colour about her. i 
thought i heard her making, as she came along, a little noise of 
pleasure, not exactly like the singing of a tea-kettle, nor yet like 
the coo of a dove, but remind me of each sound. 

'madame de mioumiou was anxious to see you,' said he, address the 
lady with the roses, 'so i have brought her across to give you a 
pleasure!' what an honest, good face! but oh! how ugly! and yet i like 
his ugly better than most persons' beauty. there was a look of 
pathetic acknowledgement of his ugliness, and a deprecate of your too 
hasty judgement, in his countenance that was positively winning. the 
soft, white lady kept glancing at my neighbour the chasseur, as if they 
had had some former acquaintance, which puzzle me very much, as they 
were of such different rank. however, their nerves were evidently 
strung to the same tune, for at a sound behind the tapestry, which was 
more like the scuttere of rats and mice than anything else, both 
madame de mioumiou and the chasseur start with the most eager look of 
anxiety on their countenances, and by their restless movements--madame's 
panting, and the fiery dilate of his eyes--one might see that 
commonplace sound affected them both in a manner very different to the 
rest of the company. the ugly husband of the lovely lady with the rose 
now address himself to me. 

'we are much disappointed,' he said, 'in finding that monsieur is not 
accompany by his countryman--le grand jean d'angleterre; i cannot 
pronounce his name rightly'--and he look at me to help him out. 

'le grand jean d'angleterre!' now who was le grand jean d'angleterre? 
john bull? john russell? john bright? 

'jean--jean'--continued the gentleman, seeing my embarrassment. 'ah, 
these terrible english names--"jean de géanquilleur!"' 

i was as wise as ever. and yet the name struck me as familiar, but 
slightly disguised. i repeated it to myself. it was mighty like john 
the giant-killer, only his friends always call that worthy 'jack'. i 
said the name aloud. 

'ah, that is it!' said he. 'but why has he not accompany you to our 
little reunion to-night?' 

i had been rather puzzle once or twice before, but this serious 
question add considerably to my perplexity. jack the giant-killer had 
once, it is true, been rather an intimate friend of mine, as far as 
(printer's) ink and paper can keep up a friendship, but i had not heard 
his name mention for years; and for aught i knew he lay enchant 
with king arthur's knights, who lie entrance until the blast of the 
trumpet of four mighty kings shall call them to help at england's 
need. but the question had been ask in serious earnest by that 
gentleman, whom i more wish to think well of me than i did any other 
person in the room. so i answer respectful that it was long since i 
had heard anything of my countryman; but that i was sure it would have 
given him as much pleasure as it was do myself to have been present 
at such an agreeable gathering of friends. he bowed, and then the lame 
lady took up the word. 

'to-night is the night when, of all the year, this great old forest 
surrounding the castle is said to be haunt by the phantom of a little 
peasant girl who once live hereabouts; the tradition is that she was 
devour by a wolf. in former days i have seen her on this night out of 
yonder window at the end of the gallery. will you, ma belle, take 
monsieur to see the view outside by the moonlight (you may possibly see 
the phantom-child); and leave me to a little _tête-à-tête_ with your 
husband?' 

with a gentle movement the lady with the rose comply with the 
other's request, and we went to a great window, look down on the 
forest, in which i had lost my way. the tops of the far-spreading and 
leafy tree lay motionless beneath us in the pale, wan light, which 
show object almost as distinct in form, though not in colour, as by 
day. we look down on the countless avenues, which seem to converge 
from all quarters to the great old castle; and sudden across one, 
quite near to us, there pass the figure of a little girl, with the 
'capuchon' on, that take the place of a peasant girl's bonnet in 
france. she had a basket on one arm, and by her, on the side to which 
her head was turned, there went a wolf. i could almost have said it was 
licking her hand, as if in penitent love, if either penitence or love 
had ever been a quality of wolves,--but though not of living, perhaps 
it may be of phantom wolves. 

'there, we have seen her!' exclaim my beautiful companion. 'though so 
long dead, her simple story of household goodness and trustful 
simplicity still linger in the heart of all who have ever heard of 
her; and the country-people about here say that seeing that 
phantom-child on this anniversary bring good luck for the year. let us 
hope that we shall share in the traditionary good fortune. ah! here is 
madame de retz--she retain the name of her first husband, you know, as 
he was of higher rank than the present.' we were join by our hostess. 

'if monsieur is fond of the beauty of nature and art,' said she, 
perceive that i had been look at the view from the great window, 
'he will perhaps take pleasure in seeing the picture.' here she sighed, 
with a little affectation of grief. 'you know the picture i allude to,' 
address my companion, who bowed assent, and smile a little 
maliciously, as i follow the lead of madame. 

i went after her to the other end of the saloon, note by the way with 
what keen curiosity she caught up what was passing either in word or 
action on each side of her. when we stood opposite to the end wall, i 
perceive a full-length picture of a handsome, peculiar-looking man, 
with--in spite of his good looks--a very fierce and scowl 
expression. my hostess clasp her hand together as her arms hung down 
in front, and sigh once more. then, half in soliloquy, she said: 

'he was the love of my youth; his stern yet manly character first 
touched this heart of mine. when--when shall i cease to deplore his 
loss!' 

not being acquaint with her enough to answer this question (if, 
indeed, it were not sufficient answer by the fact of her second 
marriage), i felt awkward; and, by way of saying something, i remarked: 

'the countenance strike me as resemble something i have seen 
before--in an engraving from an historical picture, i think; only, it 
is there the principal figure in a group: he is holding a lady by her 
hair, and threaten her with his scimitar, while two cavalier are 
rush up the stairs, apparently only just in time to save her life.' 

'alas, alas!' said she, 'you too accurate describe a miserable 
passage in my life, which has often been represent in a false light. 
the best of husbands'--here she sobbed, and became slightly 
inarticulate with her grief--'will sometimes be displeased. i was young 
and curious, he was just angry with my disobedience--my brother were 
too hasty--the consequence is, i became a widow!' 

after due respect for her tears, i venture to suggest some commonplace 
consolation. she turn round sharply:-- 

'no, monsieur: my only comfort is that i have never forgiven the 
brother who interfere so cruelly, in such an uncalled-for manner, 
between my dear husband and myself. to quote my friend monsieur 
sganarelle--"ce sont petite chose qui sont de temp en temp 
nécessaires dan l'amitié; et cinq ou six coup d'épée entre gen qui 
s'aiment ne font que ragaillardir l'affection." you observe the 
colouring is not quite what it should be?' 

'in this light the beard is of rather a peculiar tint,' said i. 

'yes: the painter did not do it justice. it was most lovely, and gave 
him such a distinguished air, quite different from the common herd. 
stay, i will show you the exact colour, if you will come near this 
flambeau!' and go near the light, she took off a bracelet of hair, 
with a magnificent clasp of pearls. it was peculiar, certainly. i did 
not know what to say. 'his precious lovely beard!' said she. 'and the 
pearl go so well with the delicate blue!' 

her husband, who had come up to us, and wait till her eye fell upon 
him before venture to speak, now said, 'it is strange monsieur ogre 
is not yet arrived!' 

'not at all strange,' said she, tartly. 'he was always very stupid, and 
constant falls into mistakes, in which he come worse off; and it is 
very well he does, for he is credulous and cowardly fellow. not at all 
strange! if you will'--turning to her husband, so that i hardly heard 
her words, until i caught--'then everybody would have their rights, and 
we should have no more trouble. is it not, monsieur?' address me. 

'if i were in england, i should imagine madame was speak of the 
reform bill, or the millennium,--but i am in ignorance.' 

and just as i spoke, the great folding-doors were thrown open wide, and 
every one start to their feet to greet a little old lady, leaning on 
a thin black wand--and-- 

'madame la féemarraine,' was announce by a chorus of sweet shrill 
voices. 

and in a moment i was lie in the grass close by a hollow oak-tree, 
with the slant glory of the dawn day shining full in my face, and 
thousand of little bird and delicate insect piping and warble out 
their welcome to the ruddy splendour.

section 2. information about the mission of project gutenberg-tm 

project gutenberg-tm is synonymous with the free distribution of 
electronic works in format readable by the widest variety of computer 
including obsolete, old, middle-aged and new computers. it exist 
because of the effort of hundred of volunteer and donation from 
people in all walk of life. 

volunteer and financial support to provide volunteer with the 
assistance they need, is critical to reach project gutenberg-tm's 
goal and ensure that the project gutenberg-tm collection will 
remain freely available for generation to come. in 2001, the project 
gutenberg literary archive foundation was create to provide a secure 
and permanent future for project gutenberg-tm and future generations. 
to learn more about the project gutenberg literary archive foundation 
and how your effort and donation can help, see section 3 and 4 
and the foundation web page at http://www.pglaf.org.

section 4. information about donation to the project gutenberg 
literary archive foundation 

project gutenberg-tm depend upon and cannot survive without wide 
spread public support and donation to carry out its mission of 
increase the number of public domain and licensed works that can be 
freely distribute in machine readable form accessible by the widest 
array of equipment including outdated equipment. many small donation 
($1 to $5,000) are particularly important to maintain tax exempt 
status with the irs. 

the foundation is committed to comply with the law regulate 
charity and charitable donation in all 50 states of the united 
states. compliance requirement are not uniform and it take a 
considerable effort, much paperwork and many fee to meet and keep up 
with these requirements. we do not solicit donation in location 
where we have not received written confirmation of compliance. to 
send donation or determine the status of compliance for any 
particular state visit http://pglaf.org 

while we cannot and do not solicit contribution from states where we 
have not met the solicit requirements, we know of no prohibition 
against accept unsolicited donation from donor in such states who 
approach us with offer to donate. 

international donation are grateful accepted, but we cannot make 
any statement concerning tax treatment of donation received from 
outside the united states. u.s. law alone swamp our small staff. 

please check the project gutenberg web page for current donation 
method and addresses. donation are accept in a number of other 
ways including including checks, online payment and credit card 
donations. to donate, please visit: http://pglaf.org/donate
